Caroline Askheim Trabado
Investigation of procurement of 
Product/Service-Systems:
A case with procurement of laboratory equipment
in a pharmaceutical company 
Master’s thesis in Design & Innovation Engineering
Supervisors: Daniela C. A. Pigosso & Tim C. McAloone
July 2022
==PAGE:2==
This is a Master’s thesis made in the MSc Design & Innovation Programme in the 
Department of Mechanical Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. 
The project was made in collaboration with the corporate procurement department 
in a pharmaceutical company.
The author would like to thank the supervisors of the project, for their expertise 
and valuable consultance:
Daniela C. A. Pigosso, Associate Professor, the Technical University of Denmark
Tim C. McAloone, Professor, PhD, the Technical University of Denmark
Paulina Laudrup, Manager of Laboratory Sourcing in the case company
The author would also like to thank the following experts within the field, who were 
consulted and contributed to the project:
Davide Bertondini, Associate Category Manager
Vita Gailiusaite, Project Manager
Mads Eklundh Sørensen, Procurement Consultant
ECTS:
30
Author:
Caroline Askheim Trabado
Submission date:
22nd of July 2022
Abstract
With an observed high resource consumption in procurement through empirical 
studies as driver, this thesis aims to investigate how more sustainable procurement 
could be facilitated by the application of Product/Service Systems (PSS), through a 
case study of laboratory equipment in a pharmaceutical company. Using a design 
research method, a literature review showed that a PSS could be an enabler of a 
circular economy because of its ability to create lasting added value for customers 
through different circular means, whilst simultaneously consuming as few resources 
as possible. However, despite having high potential in procurement, examples in 
literature and empirical studies of the application of PSS in the pharmaceutical 
industry and B2B contexts were rare. The lack of accessible discussion then led 
to the goal of the thesis; to develop support that would facilitate the selection of an 
optimal PSS for procurement in terms of economic and environmental performance, to 
support decision making and promote the application of PSS in procurement as a 
whole. Literature and empirical data investigated how to support the selection of 
the most optimal PSS to procure. The key findings and gaps identified were used to 
develop prototypes of a support purposed with guiding the selection of an optimal 
PSS through the entire procurement process. The prototypes were tested and co-
designed with stakeholders through five iterations. Despite the tool having identified 
limitations and improvement potentials that the case company can apply alterations 
and upgrades to over time, the final version of the support showed to successfully 
live up to its purpose through application and evaluation in two investment cases. 
Keywords - Product/Service-Systems, PSS, circular procurement, B2B, sustainability evaluation
i
==PAGE:3==
Learning Objectives
1.	 Plan, structure and execute a long-term project in collaboration with multiple 
stakeholders and make requested deliveries.
2.	 Collect and structure information and data from a problem context, using 
contextual interviews and other appropriate methods, and extract and utilise the 
relevant components for an analysis.
3.	 Develop a support that guides the selection of an optimal PSS, by evaluating 
economic and environmental performance, to support decision making in 
procurement.
4.	 Evaluate and justify where alternative business models should be applied using 
a decision-support model. This should be used by key stakeholders to conduct 
qualified decision making.
5.	 Develop guidelines for selecting beneficial PSS business models using an 
appropriate format, e.g. a model or method. This is to facilitate qualified and 
efficient decision making in other projects and other parts of the organisation.
6.	 Investigate, describe and present alternative business models and evaluate 
and justify which have the highest potential in terms of environmental impact 
reduction and cost saving.
7.	 Identify, describe and address barriers, risks and failures that may occur when 
implementing a selected PSS.
iii
ii
8.	 Connect the proposed recommendations to a timeframe and illustrate it using 
a roadmap. This should give key stakeholders an indication of when different 
actions would be beneficial.
9.	 Present the analysis, results and conclusions, and provide recommendations to 
key stakeholders in written, oral and graphical form, using appropriate methods, 
tools and models. The presentations should enable key stakeholders to make 
qualified decisions.

Introduction
The current socioeconomic system is the 
main cause of the depletion of natural 
resources (Michelini et al. 2017). The 
linear economic model for production and 
consumption relies on inexpensive and 
easily accessible materials and energy, and 
is characterised by discarding the product 
at the end of life (Jacobsen 2021). This 
leads to unnecessary losses of resources 
and energy and is thus not a sustainable 
economic model. Operating within this 
model, combined with an increasing 
consumption, result in a growing waste 
generation, which is expected to increase 
by 70% in 2050 compared to the state 
of 2016 (Kaza, S., Bhada-Tata, P., & van 
Woerden, F. 2018). In addition, according 
to the UN environment programme (2022), 
there will be a 50% increase in resource 
consumption from 2015 to 2050, resulting 
in a depletion of non-renewable natural 
resources. The knock-on effects would 
be on national, regional  and local levels, 
felt by suppliers, companies, workers, 
the population of the territory, and many 
other stakeholders (Ponomarenko, T., 
Nevskaya, M., & Jonek-Kowalska, I. 2021). 
This linear, high consumption behaviour 
is 
also 
found 
within 
procurement 
in 
companies, 
where 
products 
and 
equipment are purchased, used, and 
disposed of. As equipment breaks down, 
projects 
and 
priorities 
change, 
and 
technologies progress, equipment gets 
retired despite a short product lifetime, 
although it often has the capability to yield 
more and is expected to have a longer 
lifespan (Appendix A). The European 
Commission’s 
target 
for 
resource 
consumption has been set as reducing 
domestic material consumption by 70% 
by 2050 (BIO Intelligence Service, Institute 
for Social Ecology and Sustainable Europe 
Research Institute, 2012). Conversely, if 
the current day consumption pattern is 
continued, levels of resource consumption 
are expected to double by 2050 (UN 
environment programme, 2022). It is, 
therefore, necessary to innovate new 
lines of thinking that can enable drastic 
alteration of current processes and 
systems, to slow down or even halt this 
undesired trend. 
The Ellen MacArthur Foundation (2013) 
proposes a circular economy to decouple 
resource 
consumption 
from 
value 
creation and presenting it as an effective 
and promising solution to design out 
waste and pollution, keeping products 
in use and restoring and regenerating 
natural systems. Thus, reducing resource 
consumption while at the same time 
ensuring 
economic 
growth 
requires 
investigating alternative business models 
that are driven by these circular economy 
principles. Product/Service Systems (PSS) 
are considered important enablers to 
promote circular business models because 
of their product life-extending nature and 
their encouragement of closing material 
loops (Pieroni, M. P. P., McAloone, T. C., & 
Pigosso, D. C. A, 2019). Tan, McAloone & 
Gall (2007) define PSS as 
	
	
	
	
	
	
innovation strategies where 
instead of focusing on the value 
of selling physical products, 
one focuses on the value of the 
utility of products and services 
throughout the product’s life 
period (Tan, McAloone & Gall, 
2007, p. 1),
thus 
providing 
an 
opportunity 
for 
the 
provider 
to 
decouple 
resource 
consumption 
from 
value 
creation. 
With 
an 
observed 
high 
resource 
consumption 
in 
procurement 
as 
a 
driver (Appendix A), this thesis aims at 
investigating how a more sustainable 
procurement of laboratory equipment 
can 
be 
facilitated 
through 
PSS 
in 
terms of environmental and economic 
performance. 
The 
investigation 
is 
initiated with the following introductory 
research questions, that is explored 
in 
a 
systematic 
literature 
review:
a)	
How can PSS be enablers of a 	 	
	
circular economy?
b)	
Which PSS solutions are applied or 	
 
offered in the pharmaceutical  
	
industry?
c)	
Why is there a need for PSS in 	 	
	
procurement?

Methodology
The thesis is structured around the 
Design Research Methodology (DRM), 
as proposed by Blessing, L. T. M., & 
Chakrabarti, A. (2009). This methodology 
was developed as a response to the lack 
of methods for managing the complexity 
and 
dynamics 
that 
characterise 
design processes and to improve the 
effectiveness and efficiency of design 
practice. The authors describe design as
	
The intention of the DRM is to support 
a more rigorous research approach by 
guiding the selection and application 
of 
an 
appropriate 
approach 
and 
suitable methods, boosting reflection 
on 
the 
approach 
and 
methods 
used and improving the chances of 
obtaining more valid and useful outcomes 
(Blessing, L. T. M., & Chakrabarti, A., 2009). 
The DRM consists of four stages as seen 
in Figure 1:
1. Research Clarification (RC)
2. Descriptive Study I (DS-I)
3. Prescriptive Study (PS) 
4. Descriptive Study II (DS-II)
Although the DRM is composed of these 
four stages, it is not a linear process, but 
rather involves a lot of iterations between 
phases. 
Research Clarification (RC)
On the basis of a literature review, the 
intention of the Research Clarification 
is to formulate a research plan for the 
thesis, including research focus, goals and 
research questions.
Descriptive Study I (DS-I)
Having a clear goal and research plan for 
the thesis, the objective of the Descriptive 
Study I is to elaborate the description 
of the existing situation, on the basis of 
empirical data analysis, and what is found 
in literature. The goal is to obtain a better 
understanding of the current situation 
and identify gaps. 
Prescriptive Study (PS)
Using the increased understanding of 
the existing situation, the researcher 
elaborates a description of the desired 
situation. The description should contain 
a vision on how addressing the gaps 
identified in the existing situation would 
lead to the desired situation. The goal 
of this phase is to define the intended 
support that is necessary to achieve the 
improved situation, to present the actual 
support, and to present an outlined 
evaluation plan for the support, which is 
applied in the Descriptive Study II (DS-II). 
Descriptive Study II (DS-II) 
In the Descriptive Study II (DS-II), the 
intention is to investigate the impact of 
the support developed in the Prescriptive 
Study (PS), by using and evaluating it. On 
basis of the evaluation, the researcher 
would most probably have to return 
to the Prescriptive Study to revise the 
support in accordance with the findings 
and applicability of the support. When 
a 
complex, 
multi-faceted 
phenomenon, 
involving: 
people, 
a developing product, a process 
involving a multitude of activities 
and procedures; a wide variety of 
knowledge, tools and methods; an 
organisation; as well as a micro-
economic 
and 
macro-economic 
context (Blessing, L. T. M., & 
Chakrabarti, A., 2009, p. 2).
Figure 1 DRM framework (Blessing, L. T. M., & Chakrabarti, A., 2009)
an updated support is completed, an 
evaluation of the support impact would be 
conducted again. The expected iterations 
between the Prescriptive Study (PS) and 
the Descriptive Study II (DS-II) is illustrated 
in Figure 1.

Research Clarification
In this section, a systematic literature review is performed to identify 
literature with existing research on the introductory research questions 
for the thesis. This constitutes the basis of a literature review, which is 
conducted to find evidence on the introductory research questions. Finally, 
an overall research plan for the thesis is presented based on the findings 
and knowledge obtained through the literature review. In the Research 
Clarification, the aim is to answer the following introductory research 
questions: 
a)	
How can PSS be enablers of a circular economy?
b) 
Which PSS solutions are applied or offered in the pharmaceutical  
	
industry?
c)	
Why is there a need for PSS in procurement?
RC
DS-I
PS
DS-II
Systematic literature review
A 
systematic 
literature 
review 
was 
conducted to identify evidence in literature 
and industry to the questions presented 
in Box 1. 
Question a was investigated by looking 
into literature, whereas for question b, 
it was necessary to take a look both in 
literature and industry. Both literature 
and empirical data was used to answer 
question c. The empirical data was based 
on expert interviews with procurement 
responsibles in a pharmaceutical company 
and suppliers, and the data they provided. 
The literature review was based on a 
structure inspired by Biolchini et al. (2005), 
covering the phases Planning, Execution, 
and Analysis.
Planning phase
Initially in the planning phase, a list of 
search terms were developed for the areas 
of interest of the study. These were related 
to the topic of PSS, circular economy, the 
pharmaceutical industry, and procurement. 
The identified search terms related to the 
different topics were collected, and can 
be seen in Figure 2. Then, three search 
strings were formulated, as seen in Box 2. 
For the search string connected to 
question c, the search words from block 
Y were included at first. This however, 
limited the search too much. Therefore, 
the search block Y was eliminated from the 
search string for question c, to get more 
results. Furthermore, the search string 
containing search block Y did not provide 
any additional papers than the simplified 
search string provided. 
Execution phase
In the execution phase, the search 
strings were used in different literature 
databases. The searches were performed 
in February 2022, where 107 and 39 
papers were found in Scopus and DTU 
Findit, respectively, for search string a. 
181 and 199 papers were found in Scopus 
and DTU Findit, respectively, for search 
string b. For search string c, 573 papers 
were found in Scopus, and 1073 found in 
DTU FindIt. In order to limit the searches, 
two exclusion criteria were identified:
1.	
Search terms from search block X  
	
should appear in the title
2.	
Search terms from search block Z 	
	
should appear in the title
Box 1 Introductory research questions for 
the thesis
a.	
How can PSS be enablers of a 	
	
circular economy?
b.	
Which PSS solutions are applied or 	
	
offered in the pharmaceutical 	
	
industry?
c.	
Why is there a need for PSS in 	
	
procurement?

Box 2 Search strings
Search string a
(“Product/Service Systems” OR “Product/Service 
System” OR “Product Service Systems” OR “Product 
Service System” OR “PSS” OR “Product Service 
Solution” OR “Function-oriented business model” 
OR “as-a-service”) AND (“enabler” OR “enable” OR 
“support”) AND (“Circular Economy” OR “CE” or 
“circularity” OR “Cradle-to-Cradle”)
Search string b
(“Product/Service Systems” OR “Product/Service 
System” OR “Product Service Systems” OR “Product 
Service System” OR “PSS” OR “Product Service 
Solution” OR “Function-oriented business model” 
OR “as-a-service”) AND (“pharma*” OR “medico” 
OR “healthcare”) AND (“industry” OR “sector”)
Search string c
(“Product/Service Systems” OR “Product/Service 
System” OR “Product Service Systems” OR “Product 
Service System” OR “PSS” OR “Product Service 
Solution” OR “Function-oriented business model” 
OR “as-a-service”) AND (“need” OR “potential” OR 
“demand”) AND (“procurement” OR “purchase” OR 
“purchasing” OR “B2B”)
Figure 2 Search blocks
The first search resulted in a total of 2178 
papers on both databases. These were 
then screened using the first exclusion 
criterion. The papers that did not meet this 
criterion were excluded from the literature 
review. The criterion ensured that the 
main focus of the remaining articles was 
concentrated around PSS. 63 papers 
were identified for additional screening 
after applying the first selection criterion. 
Then, the second exclusion criterion was 
used for identifying the most relevant and 
specific articles. The criterion ensured 
that the discovered papers also gave 
insight surrounding circular economy, the 
pharmaceutical industry, and procurement, 
in line with review question a, b, and c, 
respectively. The screening resulted in 41 
remaining papers, all of which underwent 
further assessments of their eligibility. 
This involved examining the titles and 
abstracts in light of their relevance to the 
review questions. In general, the excluded 
articles were too specific on irrelevant 
topic in too much detail. The final result 
was 29 articles identified for further 
study. All steps of the literature search are 
demonstrated in Figure 3. 
Some additional papers were identified 
indirectly through the papers in the 
literature search. Some were referred 
to by the authors, and some were cited 
and identified in the bibliography lists. 
Grey literature was also used to answer 
the introductory research questions. To 
answer question a, a report from 2013 by 
the Ellen MacArthur foundation was used, 
which was identified through Google. 
To answer question b, it was necessary 
to look for evidence in both literature 
and industry. Thus, different companies 
offering Business-to-Business (B2B) PSS 
solutions were suggested by professors 
at DTU and a category manager in a 
pharmaceutical company. The company 
webpages were found using Google as 
search engine. 
Analysis phase
In the analysis phase, the strength of the 
evidence found in literature is evaluated in 
order to judge how well the introductory 
research questions have been answered.

Literature review
A 
literature 
review 
was 
conducted 
following the structure defined in the 
systematic literature review. The aim of 
the review was to answer the introductory 
questions for the thesis, presented in Box 
1. 
PSS as enabler of a circular 
economy 
The economy that dominates today can 
be described as a linear system that 
follows a take-make-dispose pattern. 
Companies harvest and extract materials 
to manufacture products and sell the 
products to customers, who then discard 
them when they no longer serve their 
purposes (The Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 
2013). This can be characterised as an 
extractive industrial model (Halstenberg, 
F. A., & Stark, R., 2019) with an inherent 
ineffectiveness of value systems e.g. due 
to bad design, sub-optimal processes and 
unconcerned behaviour from multiple 
actors (Pieroni, M. P. P., McAloone, T. C., 
& Pigosso, D. C. A, 2019). As resource 
completion continues to grow into one of 
the most significant global challenges, it 
is clear that this linear economic system 
has its limitations and will expire. In this
respect, the Ellen MacArthur Foundation 
(2013) emphasises that the need to adapt 
to a more sustainable economy is growing 
and has become an urgent matter.
Circular economy
The circular economy aims to establish a 
resource-effective and efficient economic 
system that is more appropriate to 
respect planetary boundaries (Pieroni, M. 
P. P., McAloone, T. C., & Pigosso, D. C. A, 
2019). Geissdoerfer, M. et al. (2017, p. 759) 
define circular economy as
The Ellen MacArthur Foundation (2013) 
illustrates this in the Butterfly Diagram, 
shown in Figure 4. The left side represent 
the biological cycle of the circular 
economy, aiming at having biological 
nutrients 
cascade 
through 
different 
applications before extracting valuable 
feedstock, and at last re-introducing their 
nutrients into the biosphere (The Ellen 
MacArthur Foundation, 2013). The right 
side represents the technical cycle of the 
circular economy, which shows how the 
value of technical materials should be 
kept through continuous loops based 
on strategies, i.e. sharing, maintaining/
prolonging, 
reusing/redistributing, 
refurbishing/remanufacturing 
and 
recycling (Kjaer, L. L. et al., 2019). The Ellen 
MacArthur Foundation (2013) emphasises 
that this given sequence is preferred, 
as value is better preserved in the inner 
circles, and the loss of value would 
increase moving towards the outer circles. 
The ultimate goal of a circular economy is 
to keep the value of resources, materials 
and products for as long as possible, and 
to minimise generation of waste (The Ellen 
MacArthur Foundation, 2013).
10
a regenerative system in which 
resource input and waste, emissions, 
and energy leakage are minimised 
by slowing, closing, and narrowing 
material and energy loops. This can 
be achieved through long-lasting 
design, maintenance, repair, reuse, 
remanufacturing, refurbishing, and 
recycling (Geissdoerfer, M. et al., 
2017, p. 759).

Research Clarification
==PAGE:11==
Figure 4 The Butterfly Diagram (The Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2013) 
11
According to Kjaer, L. L. et al. (2019), 
the adaption of the circular economy is 
required in order to brake and eventually 
stop the progress of resource depletion, 
while ensuring economic growth and job 
creation. Pieroni, M. P. P., McAloone, T. 
C., & Pigosso, D. C. A. (2019) emphasise 
that companies therefore must adopt 
circular economy as a business strategy 
and perceive it as a business opportunity 
to provide and maintain products with 
the highest value while consuming as 
few resources as possible. This requires 
investigating alternative business models 
that are compatible with circular economy 
principles. Nußholz, J. L. K. (2017, p. 13) 
defines a business model for circular 
economy as
	
	
Product/Service-Systems
A Product/Service-System (PSS) is often 
presented as a potential enabler for 
building new business models for circular 
economy due to its product life-extending 
nature and its encouragement of product 
take-back (Pieroni, M. P. P., McAloone, T. C., 
& Pigosso, D. C. A, 2019). There is a number 
of definitions of a PSS, but presenting 
one of them, Mont (2002, p. 239) defines 
a PSS as “a system of products, services, 
supporting networks and infrastructure 
that is designed to be: competitive, 
satisfy customer needs and have a lower 
environmental impact than traditional 
business models”. Tan, McAloone & Gall 
(2007) add that in a PSS, one focuses on 
the value of the utility of products and 
services throughout the product’s life 
period instead of focusing on the value 
of selling physical products. Thus, the 
business is shifted from designing and 
selling physical products only, to designing 
and selling value consisting of a system 
of products and services with the ability 
of jointly fulfilling specific client needs 
(Manzini, E., & Vezzoli, C., 2003). As Baines 
(2007) express it, a PSS delivers value in 
use through an integrated product and
service 
offering. 
Tukker, 
A. 
(2004) 
visualises this by dividing a PSS into a 
tangible product content and an intangible 
service content, as seen in Figure 5. The 
proportion of product vs. service content 
varies for each PSS (Tukker, A., 2004). 
12
Research Clarification
Research Clarification
how 
a 
company 
creates, 
captures 	
and 
delivers 
value, 
with a value creation logic designed 
to improve 	 resource 
efficiency 
through contributing to extending 
the useful life of products and 
parts (e.g. through long-life design, 
repair and remanufacturing) and 
closing material loops (Nußholz, J. 
L. K., 2017, p. 13).
==PAGE:12==
Figure 5 Main and subcategories of PSS (Tukker, A.,2004)
Tukker, A. (2004) divides PSS into three 
different categories: 
Product oriented (A)
Use oriented (B) 
Result oriented (C)
Category A is product-oriented services, 
which is concentrated around sales of 
products, but with extra services added, 
e.g. advice and consultancy. Category B 
is use-oriented services, mainly providing 
product availability instead of selling the 
physical products themselves. This implies 
that the ownership often stays with the 
provider, and the product is made available 
in various forms, e.g. product lease, 
product renting or sharing, and product 
pooling, which means that these PSS often 
are shared by different users. Category C 
is result-oriented services, where there is 
no pre-determined product involved, but 
the client and provider simply agree on 
a result. Then the provider has complete 
freedom on how that result should be 
delivered. This could be done through 
activity management, pay-per-service unit 
or as a functional result. 
Tukker, A., & Tischner, U. (2006) use a car 
as an example to illustrate the different 
variations of PSS. A car might be sold in a 
traditional manner, where the ownership 
of the product is transferred to the user. 
This is categorised as a pure product-
based value, which often encourages a 
linear take-make-dispose pattern where 
products are mass produced, sold, used 
and disposed (The Ellen MacArthur 
Foundation, 2013). As a consequence, 
a vast amount of waste is generated. 
According 
to 
the 
Ellen 
MacArthur 
Foundation. (2018), a car is parked 92% 
13
of its lifetime, which means that the car 
is only in use 8% of the time, as seen in 
Figure 6. This implies that a relatively 
large quantity of energy and resources is 
consumed in making a product that is not 
even utilised to its full potential in relation 
to the overall usage of the product by 
the consumer (The Ellen MacArthur 
Foundation, 2013). In a product oriented 
PSS, however, Tukker, A., & Tischner, 
U. (2006) describe how a car might be 
delivered as a PSS, where value is based 
on a combination of product and service, 
as seen in Figure 7. First, a car might 
be sold with a product related service or 
advice, e.g. maintenance or eco-driving 
course, respectively. This may prolong the 
lifetime of the car and reduce resource 
consumption during the use phase of the 
product life cycle, respectively. Then, the 
authors present numerous examples of 
use-oriented PSS solutions for the car that 
have even higher sustainability potential: 
Product lease (where the customer uses 
the product exclusively without having 
ownership of the car), product sharing/
renting (where the customer does not use 
the car exclusively, but is either the owner 
of the car, and sharing it, or the provider is 
the owner, and renting it), product pooling
Figure 6 Structural waste in the mobility system (The Ellen MacArthur Foundation., 2018) 
14
Research Clarification
Research Clarification
==PAGE:13==
Figure 7 Variants of PSS (Tukker, A., & Tischner, U., 2006)
15
(where the car is used simultaneously by 
several users) and pay-per-service unit 
(where the user pays for the output of the 
product according to the use level, e.g. an 
amount of money is paid per kilometre). 
In these cases as well, maintenance is 
often included in the offering, which will 
prolong product lifetime, according to 
Tukker, A. (2004). In addition, the usage 
of the product is intensified and better 
utilised. Tukker, A., & Tischner, U. (2006) 
present 
outsourcing 
and 
functional 
result of a car as result-oriented solutions. 
In outsourcing, a third party owns the 
product and provides a product related 
service, e.g. a taxi, that charges a price 
per kilometre. In a functional result, the 
service provider delivers a specific result 
– in this case getting from A to B. How the 
result is delivered is determined by the 
service provider and is insignificant to the 
receiver. For instance, the transportation 
of a person could be performed using 
train or bus, where a specific amount of 
money is paid for travelling from A to B. 
According to Tukker, A., (2004), the result-
oriented way of value delivery has an 
even higher sustainability potential than 
product oriented and use oriented PSS. 
This is due to the fact that the provider 
often feels an incentive for continually 
improving the product with lifecycle 
performance in mind, as this often 
will result in a cost reduction for their 
operations. 
The 
Ellen 
MacArthur 
Foundation (2018) also emphasises that 
cities would be much more resource 
efficient if the focus shifted from moving 
vehicles to moving people. At last, Tukker, 
A., & Tischner, U. (2006), present the most 
radical and most sustainable form of value 
delivery as a pure service-based value, 
where an activity is provided without 
using any products, e.g. teleportation. 
Without a product involved in the service 
offering, material waste is avoided, and 
value creation is decoupled from resource 
consumption (Kjaer, L. L., et al. 2019). 
The circular economy potential of 
Product/Service-Systems
Tukker, A. (2004) shows that PSS provides 
alternative ways to deliver added value, 
which reveals numerous opportunities 
surrounding 
business 
potential 
and 
circular economy. First, creating added 
value is a major reason for being in 
business and for the sustainability and
prosperity of a business. Thus, a PSS gives 
a unique opportunity to increase the 
competitiveness of a company. Secondly, 
Kjaer, L. L. et al. (2019) express that with 
a shift in focus from selling products 
to selling value through service and 
performance, resource consumption can 
be decoupled from value creation and 
economic growth. Jacobson, H., Carlson, A., 
& Lindahl, M. (2021), Kjaer, L. et al. (2004), 
also explain that several PSS strategies 
and activities have a life-prolonging effect 
on the products, increase operational 
efficiency, and intensify product usage. 
Examples of activities that have a life-
prolonging 
effect 
on 
the 
products 
and 
promote 
operational 
efficiency 
are durability, modularity, adaptability, 
upgradability, 
continuous 
maintenance, 
and other services or operational support 
provided by the supplier. Integrating take-
back arrangements or other End-of-Life 
(EoL) management services also have the 
potential for prolonging the life of the 
products by taking them back into the 
loop for further use by other consumers. 
Product sharing, renting, and pooling are 
examples of PSS strategies that intensify 
product usage. Lindahl, M., Sundin, E., & 
16
Research Clarification
Research Clarification
==PAGE:14==
Sakao, T. (2014) and Tukker, A. (2015) 
emphasise 
that 
provider 
incentives 
also promote resource efficiency within 
PSS. For instance, it is in the supplier’s 
best interest to offer solutions that do 
not require extensive resources for e.g. 
production, maintenance and operation, 
to ensure good profitability. Furthermore, 
having a versatile product means that 
the service offered by the provider can 
solve multiple customer wants and needs 
using the same product. This would also 
optimise resource efficiency, as there 
would be no need for multiple product 
versions, enabling the circular actions of 
reuse and adaptability. The provider of a 
function may also benefit by educating the 
buyer on using the hardware to decrease 
the energy use and wear of the product 
(Jacobson, H., Carlson, A., & Lindahl, M., 
2021).
Although PSS has potential as enabler for 
a circular economy, Kjaer, L. et al. (2019) 
highlight that there are no guarantees 
that this will be the case. A reduction in 
resource consumption is not necessarily 
an automatic result of PSS. This is because 
of the usual rebound effects connected to
a PSS, which consequently may create 
further rebound effects resulting in higher 
induced impacts than the initial ones 
being avoided (Kjaer, L. L., Pigosso, D. C. 
A., & McAloone, T. C., 2017). For instance, 
in the case of use-oriented services, 
where products and services are made 
available for e.g. sharing systems, more 
accessibility to products and services 
may lead to more people increasing 
consumption. Taking car sharing systems 
as an example, easier access to cars 
could make people drive more rather 
than utilising public transport (Madsen, 
M. B., 2015). Also, Tukker, A. (2004) adds 
that as the ownership lies with the car 
provider, it may result in less responsible 
user 
behaviour. 
Consequently, 
more 
damages may occur on the car, which 
require frequent repairs and product 
substitutions. Hence, there will be an 
increase of environmental impacts. Take-
back services may also require product 
redistribution and remanufacturing, which 
involve additional resources and energy 
consumption. Thus, PSS often causes 
changes in behaviour that can counteract 
the resource reduction potential (Kjaer, L. 
et al. 2019). Thus, the design of the
business models needs to be appropriate 
to avoid higher material consumption 
and adverse rebound effects (Jacobson, 
H., Carlson, A., & Lindahl, M., 2021). Kjaer, 
L. L., Pigosso, D. C. A., & McAloone, T. C. 
(2017) suggest that scenarios should 
be compared and measured against a 
defined reference system in order to 
evaluate whether the PSS has a favourable 
environmental performance, and whether 
it contributes to a circular economy.
17
18
Applied or offered 
PSS solutions in the 
pharmaceutical industry
The pharmaceutical sector is an innovative 
branch of the economy that focuses 
on the development and production 
of drugs and medical devices (Salwin, 
M. et al. 2021). The PSS concept and its 
application has been growing fast in the 
industry across multiple areas. However, 
according to Xing, K., Rapaccini, M., & 
Visintin, F. (2017), studies investigating 
PSS in the pharmaceutical sector are 
still 
limited. 
Leading 
manufacturers 
of machines and equipment for the 
production of pharmaceutical products 
offer machines in numerous variants. 
Services, on the other hand, constitute a 
narrow part of their offer (Salwin, M. et 
al. 2021). However, this specific industry 
is perceived as an area where PSS can 
have great potential. Yip, M. H., Phaal, R., 
& Probert, D. R. (2015), p. 243) exemplify 
that “a combination of tangibles and 
intangibles such as products, hardware, 
services, 
facilities, 
professionals 
and 
patients, can offer greater opportunities 
for 
collaboration, 
innovation 
and 
performance improvement (Yip, M. H., 
Phaal, R., & Probert,D. R., 2015, p. 243)”.
However, this is an opportunity not utilised 
enough by the pharmaceutical sector. In a 
study of 60 PSS design methods, conducted 
by Salwin, M., Kraslawski, A., & Lipiak, J. 
(2020), most of the methods were directed 
to the mechanical engineering sector. The 
study did not identify any information 
on methods used in the pharmaceutical 
sector. This is despite the fact that the 
pharmaceutical sector consists of 4,106 
enterprises employing 595,751 people 
in the EU, and the production value is 
around 287,89 billion Euros (Salwin, M. et 
al. 2021). Yip, M. H., Phaal, R., & Probert, 
D. R. (2015) argue that one probable 
reason for the limited presence of PSS in 
the pharmaceutical sector is the complex 
nature of such a regulated industry, 
as well as the participation of multiple 
stakeholders with conflicting priorities. 
The industry is also characterised by 
meeting 
strict 
requirements, 
high 
accuracy and cleanliness (Salwin, M. et 
al. 2021), which add complexity to the 
external environment. According to Yip, 
M. H., Phaal, R., & Probert, D. R. (2015), the 
external environment itself is a significant 
aspect in the design of a new offering, as
it affects the product design, customer 
experience, the interests and concerns 
of stakeholders, as well as the social and 
ethical priorities for the design. 
Xing, K., Rapaccini, M., & Visintin, F. (2017) 
point out a few examples of PSS solutions 
offered in the pharmaceutical industry, 
such as PSS for single-use medical devices, 
consisting of a more systematic solution 
involving strong collaboration among 
stakeholders, and additional agreements 
and services, e.g. take-back and improved 
waste management. For example, the 
pharmaceutical company, Ambu, has 
succeeded in implementing take-back of 
their single-use devices from the hospitals. 
One of Ambu’s sustainability engineers 
explains in an interview that thanks to 
well-functional partnerships, they are 
able to get materials back into the loop 
and promote a circular economy. Other 
examples that Xing, K., Rapaccini, M., & 
Visintin, F. (2017) give are PSS solutions 
offered for ambient assisted living, where 
social and health services with hardware 
(customised 
monitoring 
devices 
and 
sensors) are linked with information 
communication technology platforms, 
Research Clarification
Research Clarification
==PAGE:15==
home automation systems and care 
environments 
to 
support 
personal 
wellness for autonomy enhancement 
and comfort. This enable the aged and 
disabled people to have a managed 
lifestyle at home (Xing, K., Rapaccini, M., & 
Visintin, F., 2017).
By examining further B2B PSS offerings 
in the pharmaceutical industry, more 
examples can be found of companies 
providing extensive waste and surplus 
inventory management that aim to 
repurpose excess inventory. One example 
is Sur+ International, which offers surplus 
inventory 
management 
for 
several 
industries, including the pharmaceutical 
industry. According to the company itself, 
Sur+ International connects companies 
that want to get rid of surplus inventory 
to potential buyers with the benefit of 
increasing 
environmental 
credibility, 
freeing up storage space, and not to 
mention lifting financial efficiency (Sur+ 
International: A World of Solutions for 
Surplus Stock, 2022). There are also 
companies providing PSS via leasing of 
chemicals for e.g. cleaning of equipment 
and machinery. For example, SAFECHEM
provides full service offers through 
chemical lease to several industries, which 
include a customised package covering 
a number of tasks related to equipment 
cleaning, supply of necessary chemicals, 
improving 
cleaning 
results, 
optimise 
consumption and taking care of waste 
management (SAFECHEM: We Provide 
Sustainable Solutions., 2022). Avantor is 
another company offering end-to-end 
chemical management solutions in the 
pharma sector, which includes expert on-
site chemical management to support cost 
reduction, improve safety and efficiency, 
and comply with environmental, health 
and safety regulations (Avantor: Chemical 
management solutions, 2022).
A need for PSS in 
procurement
As seen in literature, Tukker, A. (2004) 
express that PSS promises to enhance 
the competitiveness of a company by 
delivering superior and unique value to 
customers that goes beyond traditional 
product offerings, thereby enabling long-
term business success for the supplier. 
Everhartz, J., Maiwald, K., & Wieseke, J. 
(2014) point out that while the positive 
outcomes of the offerings are frequently 
described in literature from the supplier’s 
perspective, the customers’ perspective 
and the customer’s motives of procuring 
a PSS in a B2B context is rarely the unit of 
analysis. Graça, S. S. (2021) also believes that 
the effect that servitisation has on buyer-
supplier relationships is underexplored. 
The papers that do discuss this, however, 
suggest that the main motives for the 
customers are the possibility to purchase 
an individualised offering tailored to 
their own requirements and obtaining 
knowledge and skills from an external 
company. Everhartz, J., Maiwald, K., & 
Wieseke, J. (2014) present task sharing as 
a driver for a company to purchase a PSS, 
as it involves sharing lifecycle activities, 
which might enhance focus on core 
competencies and avert risk. Thus, Graça, 
S. S. (2021) states that the customer has 
the opportunity to make better decisions 
and improve daily operations by procuring 
PSS.  
A need for reducing environmental 
impact
Empirical studies within a pharmaceutical 
company, as part of this research, offer 
results from a calculation of procurement’s 
contribution to its total CO2 footprint. 
Results 
showed 
that 
procurement 
constitutes 90% of the company’s total 
CO2 footprint as procurement takes 
part in a significant share of the value 
chain (Appendix B). As another example, 
Coloplast, 
a 
Danish 
multinational 
company that develops and delivers 
medical products and services (Coloplast, 
2022), revealed in their sustainability 
report from 2021 that approximately 
80% of their emissions lie in their scope 
3 emissions. These account for all the 
company’s other indirect emissions from 
its supply chain, where procurement of 
raw materials and goods constitutes the 
most significant part (Coloplast. 2021). 
Thus, it can be argued that procurement 
has a huge potential for contributing to 
reduce the total environmental impact of a 
company. As Kjaer, L. L. et al. (2019) already 
have highlighted, procuring PSS could be 
one way to pursue a lower environmental 
impact, due to the potential for decoupling 
resource consumption from economic 
growth. 
Own empirical studies    in procurement 
of laboratory equipment showed that a 
vast amount of pieces of equipment are 
discarded annually when they no longer 
serve their purpose. Then, the equipment 
often ends up in the basement and 
remains unused. There are many reasons 
for the disposal of the equipment, as 
presented in Table 1. The manager of 
Laboratory Sourcing, mentioned that 
the most common reasons are outdated 
pieces of equipment (Appendix C and 
Appendix D). The equipment may no 
longer function correctly or the spare 
parts might be obsolete, which means 
that the equipment cannot be repaired. 
In other cases, newly updated versions 
of the product with improved technology 
have arrived at the market, which better 
fulfil the required functions. Öhgren, M. 
Research Clarification
Research Clarification
19
20
==PAGE:16==
(2019) emphasise that reconditioning and 
reselling the the discarded products would 
mean significant environmental gains 
and cost savings for the procurement 
departments. However, most importantly, 
the authors express that to make a more 
significant impact, there is a need for 
transitioning to a circular economy, which 
will require the adaption of new business 
models.
A need for reducing cost
An 
inherent 
characteristic 
of 
the 
procurement of products in the capital 
goods manufacturing, is high investment 
cost (Sarancic, D., Pigosso, D. C. A., & 
McAloone, T. C., 2021). Own empirical 
studies in a pharmaceutical company 
confirmed this statement, as it revealed 
that 
the 
procurement 
department 
exercises a spend on new equipment 
corresponding to 90% of the total spend, 
to either substitute discarded equipment 
or for expansion purposes (Appendix 
E). This amount is higher than what has 
been allowed for in fiscal budgeting. 
However, the team leader states that this 
is necessary to stay up to date with the 
state-of-the-art technology. 
Sarancic, D., Pigosso, D. C. A., & McAloone, 
T. C. (2021) express that by rising to 
the level of a PSS, the total number of 
products purchased could be reduced 
while still achieving the same value or an 
even higher value. Thus, both material 
cost and waste could be reduced. Rödger, 
J. M., Kjær, L. L., & Pagoropoulos, A. (2017) 
show that products in capital goods 
manufacturing do not only have high 
acquisition costs but are also associated 
with different types of costs throughout 
the product lifecycle that the customer 
needs to consider, e.g. variable costs such 
as energy cost, electricity cost, cleaning 
cost, and maintenance cost, and fixed 
costs such as taxes, insurance, logistics 
cost, capital cost, and depreciation cost. 
A PSS can however, according to Sarancic, 
D., Pigosso, D. C. A., & McAloone, T. C. 
(2021), help better allocating costs and 
responsibility between the company and 
the suppliers. A product lease for instance, 
would eliminate the acquisition cost for 
the customer and the cost would rather 
be concentrated around the use of the 
product (Tukker, A., 2004).
Table 1 Reasons for equipment disposal (Appendix C, Appendix D)
A need for integrated solutions
In the same round of own empirical 
studies in a pharmaceutical company, the 
need for integrated solutions has been 
expressed (Appendix C). For example, in the 
laboratory, chemicals and consumables 
are required for executing tasks related to 
the equipment. Therefore, chemicals and 
consumables must be ordered separately 
from the equipment. As a consequence, 
chemicals and consumables are sourced 
from a high number of different suppliers, 
resulting in a fragmented procurement. 
Therefore, the type of chemical and 
consumable is sometimes a bad match 
for the equipment, and does not work. 
In other cases, the chemicals and 
consumables must be bought in high 
quantities, despite that what is actually 
needed might just be a small fraction of 
it. This means that redundant material 
must be stored and might never be used 
before it has to be disposed of due to 
past expiry date. The opposite might also 
happen, where insufficient chemicals 
and consumables are ordered, meaning 
that the analyses and tasks related to 
the equipment cannot be executed. The 
consequences are non-value-adding
activities, e.g. unnecessary inventory 
management, which require time and cost 
for the company. Battaglia, D., Borchardt, 
M., & Patrício, L. (2016) stress that suppliers 
should accommodate these customer 
issues by providing more integrated and 
bundled solutions. The authors perceive 
the integrated offering as an opportunity 
for suppliers to pay more attention to 
the customer’s processes and activities 
and provide them with the necessary 
resources and support over the entire 
lifecycle. Graça, S. S. (2021) claims that 
this allows companies to compete with 
more customised and unique bundles 
that deliver more differentiated solutions 
to customers. These additional intangible 
benefits are also difficult for competitors 
to replicate, which will give the supplier a 
more competitive position.  
A need for improved B2B relationships
In a study from 2021, Salwin, M. et 
al. (2021) have identified the needs 
of 
a 
pharmaceutical 
company 
and 
pharmaceutical machine manufacturer in 
the context of the purchase of a machine, 
training, drug production process and 
sanitary and hygienic requirements, as
seen in Table 2. When purchasing the 
machine, the company expresses the 
need to eliminate high acquisition cost 
in addition to the option of receiving full 
service of the machine throughout its 
life. After installation of the machine, the 
company lacks operator training related 
to new machines and training of new 
employees. During production, support 
for the production processes and error 
diagnoses are needed in the company. 
Furthermore,  meeting the sanitary and 
hygienic requirements for pharmaceutical 
production and adequate protection of 
production workers are musts. As seen 
in Table 2, the company has many needs 
occurring at different times during the 
lifecycle. In order for the supplier to 
accommodate the customer’s needs, 
it is necessary to collect data from the 
customer, e.g. declaration of the period 
of use of the machine, information about 
needed training and schedules, operation 
of the machine, and reported changes 
within compliance. This would require 
constant contact with the company 
(Salwin, M. et al. 2021) and improved 
communication 
between 
the 
parties 
(Battaglia, D., Borchardt, M., & Patrício, L., 
Research Clarification
Research Clarification
21
22
==PAGE:17==
Table 2 The needs of a pharmaceutical company and pharmaceutical machine manufacturer (Salwin, M. et al. 2021)
2016).
Battaglia, D., Borchardt, M., & Patrício, 
L. (2016) claim that in order to be able 
to source state-of-the-art products and 
services, it is important to build strong 
links and connections with the suppliers. 
When 
the 
suppliers 
have 
a 
closer 
relationship to the customers, they have 
the opportunity to continuously identify 
customer requirements and needs, and 
satisfy them. This will subsequently 
result in more customised and higher 
quality products and services for the 
customers over time. The authors clarify 
that moving from a simple, transaction-
based customer interaction to multiple 
relationship-based 
interactions 
selling 
complete solutions through long-term 
relationships, the connection between 
supplier and customer is strengthened. 
Thus, the development of relationships 
between suppliers and buyers is a relevant 
aspect to be considered in PSS offerings. 
Quality of the offering is however, 
according to Everhartz, J., Maiwald, K., & 
Wieseke, J. (2014), not the only motive for 
strengthening B2B relationships. It also 
creates the opportunity for extending the
24
knowledge base, letting skills, resources 
and information be shared across the 
different parties. According to Graça, 
S. S. (2021), high levels of information 
exchange has been found to greatly 
increase buyer’s satisfaction with the 
supplier’s performance. 
Jacobson, H., Carlson, A., & Lindahl, M. 
(2021) and Stoughton, M., & Votta, T. (2003) 
describe the traditional constellation 
between supplier and customer as a tug-
of-war where the buyer wants to invest as 
little money as possible for the purchase, 
while the provider has the opposite 
notion. In addition, the customer wants 
to decrease material volume and waste 
generation, whereas the provider wants 
to sell as much as possible, which would 
increase the volume. An example of this 
dichotomy can be seen in the procurement 
of chemicals, as illustrated in Figure 8, 
where manufacturers are faced with the 
environmental and public welfare desire 
to reduce chemical use (Stoughton, M., 
& Votta, T., 2003). As a consequence of 
these conflicting incentives, the bottom 
line consists of minimum acceptable 
conditions for quality, price, and details
of delivery for both parties. PSS however, 
provides 
business 
models 
that 
can 
align incentives in supplier-customer 
relationships by creating shared gains, 
as seen in Figure 9. This would depend 
on constant communication between 
the 
parties 
(Jacobson, 
H., 
Carlson, 
A., & Lindahl, M., 2021). This mutual 
cooperation might be valuable and might 
create relationships that benefit from 
greater trust, commitment and mutual 
satisfaction. Graça, S. S. (2021, p. 4) refers 
to mutual cooperation as “the degree 
to which buyers and suppliers help one 
another and work together to achieve 
mutually beneficial goals” (Graça, S. S., 
2021, p. 4). As a result, loyalty and allegiance 
is created both ways, the author states, 
which is key for a sustainable partnership. 
Sustainability in B2B relationships is a 
desired state because it reduces cost and 
increases profitability, in particular within 
a global B2B context. Through time, 
successful exchanges build relationship 
capital and foster relationship longevity 
(Graça, S. S., 2021).
Salwin, M. et al. (2021) continued the study 
in a pharmaceutical company by mapping
Research Clarification
Research Clarification

out the interests in machine user services 
related to the pharmaceutical machine, 
drug manufacturing, health and safety at 
work, etc. The result from this is shown in 
Table 3.  In order to answer these needs, 
it is evident that long-term partnerships 
with sufficient information and knowledge 
exchange and that can prioritise solutions 
that can directly contribute to positive 
performance outcomes, are necessary 
(Graça, S. S., 2021).

Analysis of Literature Review
PSS as enablers of a circular 	  	
	
economy
According to what is found in literature, 
PSS provides many opportunities for 
companies to rethink their business 
models and add more value to their 
offerings while simultaneously consuming 
as few resources as possible (Pieroni, M. P. 
P., McAloone, T. C., & Pigosso, D. C. A, 2019). 
Kjaer, L. L. et al. (2019) argue that this will 
result in resource consumption becoming 
decoupled 
from 
economic 
growth. 
Tukker, A., & Tischner, U. (2006) also show 
examples of how different variations of 
PSS have diverse sustainability potentials. 
Therefore, strong evidence is found for 
PSS to have a great potential to be an 
enabler for a circular economy. However, 
Kjaer, L. L. et al. (2019) also point out 
that one often expect the environmental 
performance to automatically improve as 
a result of implementing a PSS, although 
this may not be the case. There is no 
guarantee that the overall environmental 
impact will decrease when applying a 
PSS. A reason for the high environmental 
expectations
might be due to the fact that the intention 
of a PSS in many cases is to increase 
environmental performance, and because 
the goal of having a lower environmental 
impact compared to traditional business 
models is embedded in a number of PSS 
definitions, e.g. as Mont (2002) and Banes 
(2007) define it. Therefore, as understood 
by literature, it is important to have 
in mind that improved environmental 
performance is not an implicit result of a 
PSS.
Applied or offered PSS solutions 
in the pharmaceutical industry
More 
companies 
in 
industry 
are 
requesting and sourcing more bundled, 
and flexible solutions from their suppliers, 
which allow them to be more agile and 
increase the adaptability for change. 
However, after reviewing several papers 
on applied or offered PSS Solutions in the 
pharmaceutical industry, there is strength 
in evidence regarding limited examples 
of PSS solutions applied or offered in 
the pharmaceutical industry (Salwin, M., 
Kraslawski, A., & Lipiak, J., 2020; Xing, K., 
Rapaccini, M., & Visintin, F., 2017; Yip, M. 
H., Phaal, R., & Probert, D. R., 2015). The 
authors agree that companies in the 
pharmaceutical industry are locked in 
traditional procurement in a higher 
degree than companies from other 
industries. Yip, M. H., Phaal, R., & Probert, 
D. R. (2015) argue that the complex 
context connected to the pharmaceutical 
industry is one critical reason for that, 
involving 
multiple 
stakeholders 
with 
conflicting priorities, health, safety and 
environmental compliance, and social and 
ethical considerations. Due to the variety 
of aspects that must be considered, as 
listed by Yip, M. H., Phaal, R., & Probert, D. 
R. (2015), it can be argued that companies 
in the pharmaceutical industry might 
experience a more significant barrier 
to apply PSS compared to companies in 
other industries, and might explain the 
conservative nature of the pharma sector 
in general. Thus, extensive work in change 
management might be required in order 
for procurement in the pharmaceutical 
industry to progress.
However, some examples of PSS in the 
pharmaceutical sector can be found in 
industry, e.g. PSS for single-use medical 
devices and PSS for ambient assisted 
27
living (Xing, K., Rapaccini, M., & Visintin, 
F., 2017). A weakness, however, is that the 
authors do not cover applied PSS in B2B 
relationships. The limited focus on the 
B2B customer perspective in PSS business 
models has been a recurring issue when 
investigating literature. Utilising additional 
databases for the search could, however, 
provide additional relevant papers and 
other insights into the application of PSS 
in B2B contexts. Nevertheless, as seen in 
industry, the layout of B2B PSS offerings 
in the pharmaceutical industry is different 
than the B2C. Companies providing waste 
and 
inventory 
management 
services 
dominate the field, while companies 
providing more radical offerings, such 
as result-oriented PSS, are limited. This 
might be due to the fact that waste and 
inventory management services have 
less interference with the core business 
and 
operations, 
and 
its 
regulatory 
requirements, 
which 
means 
that 
service agreements and collaborations 
require less effort. A result-oriented PSS 
would, on the other hand, have to be 
more adapted and integrated into the 
companies’ core business, which means 
that pharmaceutical regulations would 
have to be considered in a higher degree.
A need for PSS in procurement
With strong evidence from literature and 
empirical studies, it can be concluded 
that 
procurement 
is 
facing 
issues 
that PSS can solve. For instance, in 
procurement, there is a need for more 
integrated package solutions that can 
eliminate purchasing redundant material, 
unnecessary inventory management, and 
other non-value adding activities that 
might be consequences of fragmented 
procurement. Since most PSS involve 
more bundled and customised offerings, 
it can be an efficient way to solve these 
issues. There is consensus among several 
authors (Battaglia, D., Borchardt, M., & 
Patrício, L., 2016; Everhartz, J., Maiwald, 
K., & Wieseke, J., 2014; Graça, S. S., 
2021; Salwin, M. et al. 2021) regarding 
the need for improved communication 
with the supplier through stronger B2B 
relationships, as this would make it 
possible for the supplier to continuously 
adapt their offerings to the changing 
customer needs and requirements, e.g. 
upgrading equipment in accordance with 
the required technology. This would also 
enable a more affluent knowledge base 
for both parties. A PSS could be a way 
to stimulate such long-term sustainable 
relationships 
between 
supplier 
and 
customer. 
Strong 
collaboration 
and 
relationships between companies should 
also be fostered to drive the sustainable 
agenda, where partnerships constitute an 
essential strategy to encourage a circular 
economy 
and 
reduce 
environmental 
impact. 
A 
sound 
basis 
for 
environmental 
improvement would be to start internally 
in the company. As seen in own empirical 
studies in the case company, procurement 
of equipment company tends to follow 
the linear economy described by The Ellen 
MacArthur Foundation. (2013),  where 
products are sourced, used, and disposed 
of when damaged or no longer serve their 
purpose. This was the case for products 
that also had a short life-time. Then it 
could be questioned whether products 
should be acquired and owned if having 
a short lifetime. Rather, access to the 
needed functionalities through PSS could 
reduce waste of equipment if maintained 
and managed by the provider throughout 
the lifecycle, thus reducing 
28
Research Clarification
Research Clarification
==PAGE:20==
the need for purchasing new equipment. 
This will naturally also reduce costs for 
the buyer. Further, if ownership stays with 
the provider, the acquisition cost may 
be eliminated, which would increase the 
overall net working capital (NWC), free up 
space in the budget for other investments, 
and a broader portfolio might be afforded. 
A summary of how PSS can accommodate 
the gaps and needs in procurement is 
depicted in Table 4.    
Procurement 
is 
an 
interesting 
area 
to explore regarding the application 
of PSS due to its high impact on the 
environment, hence the high potential 
for environmental improvement. From a 
company’s perspective, the impact from 
suppliers can be perceived as challenging 
to control. However, the sourcing of PSS 
might enable more control and mitigation 
of upstream impacts due to the high 
environmental potential connected with 
PSS. Thus, it is essential to require more 
value and environmental transparency 
from the suppliers in their offerings. As 
a result, procurement has much power 
regarding the environmental performance 
of sourced products and  services,
Table 4 How PSS can accommodate the identified needs in procurement
29
particularly in large enterprises with high 
spend and large supplier lists. 
Conclusion of literature review
Based on findings from literature it 
is possible to conclude that PSS has 
potential to be an enabler of a circular 
economy as it involves strategies and 
activities that have a life-prolonging 
effect on products, increase operational 
efficiency, and intensify product usage, 
while simultaneously creating lasting 
added value for customers (Pieroni, M. P. 
P., McAloone, T. C., & Pigosso, D. C. A, 2019; 
Tukker, A., 2004). However, Kjaer, L. L. et 
al. (2019) highlight that PSS may cause 
changes in behaviour that can counteract 
the resource reduction potential, hence 
increasing environmental impact. Thus, 
in order to evaluate whether a PSS is 
environmentally favourable, and whether 
it can contribute to a circular economy, it 
is necessary to have a reference system in 
order to evaluate the alternative situation 
(Kjaer, L. L., Pigosso, D. C. A., & McAloone, 
T. C., 2017).
The 
pharmaceutical 
industry 
is 
characterised by a complex nature, where 
meeting strict requirements, high accuracy 
and cleanliness, and accommodating 
multiple stakeholders with conflicting 
priorities are central elements (Yip, M. H., 
Phaal, R., & Probert, D. R., 2015; Salwin, 
M. et al. 2021). According to Yip, M. H., 
Phaal, R., & Probert, D. R. (2015), these 
are influencing factors for the limited 
presence of PSS in this particular industry. 
However, the authors express that PSS has 
great potential in precisely this industry, 
as a combination of products and services 
can facilitate better opportunities for 
collaboration, innovation and performance 
improvement. A few examples of applied 
PSS were found for B2C in literature. Xing, 
K., Rapaccini, M., & Visintin, F. (2017) point 
out examples of applied PSS as extended 
collaboration 
among 
stakeholders 
involving additional agreements and 
services, e.g. in the business of single-
use equipment. Examples of PSS in B2B 
relationships 
in 
the 
pharmaceutical 
sector were limited in literature, hence 
examples had to be collected from 
industry. The most frequent applications 
were companies offering waste and 
surplus inventory management for other 
companies and leasing of chemicals for 
e.g. cleaning of equipment and machinery 
(Sur+ International: A World of Solutions 
for Surplus Stock, 2022; SAFECHEM: We 
Provide Sustainable Solutions., 2022;
Avantor: 
Chemical 
management 
solutions., 2022).
Based on what is found in literature 
and empirical studies, it is possible to 
conclude that there is a need for PSS in 
procurement, due to the ability of PSS 
to solve common issues in this area 
(Battaglia, D., Borchardt, M., & Patrício, L., 
2016; Everhartz, J., Maiwald, K., & Wieseke, 
J., 2014; Graça, S. S., 2021; Salwin, M. et al. 
2021). Issues mentioned were a high waste 
generation of procured products, high 
spending on new products, and non-value 
adding activities to manage inventory and 
waste in the areas in which the products 
were used. Battaglia, D., Borchardt, M., 
& Patrício, L. (2016) suggest that more 
integrated and bundled solutions, such 
as PSS, could be an answer to solve these 
issues. If procuring lifecycle management 
services as part of the offering, e.g. 
maintenance, 
repair, 
refurbishment, 
activity management, take-back, etc., 
waste, 
cost, 
and 
non-value-adding 
activities could be reduced. A more 
committed and powerful B2B relationship 
with high levels of information exchange 
would also, according to Battaglia, D., 
30
Research Clarification
Research Clarification
==PAGE:21==
Borchardt, M., & Patrício, L. (2016) and 
Graça, S. S. (2021), make it easier for the 
supplier to respond to customer needs 
continuously and adapt their offerings 
accordingly. 
31
Overall research plan
Based on the literature review, the 
research clarification concludes with an 
overall research plan, including research 
focus and goal, research problems and 
main research questions, and expected 
contribution and deliverables. 
Research focus and goal
The conclusions of the literature review 
imply a need for PSS in procurement, as 
it has high potential for reducing cost and 
environmental impact, increasing product 
and service customisation and quality, 
and strengthening supplier and customer 
relationship. With this backdrop, the 
thesis aims at investigating possibilities 
for procurement of PSS, using a case of 
procurement of laboratory equipment 
in a pharmaceutical company. This will 
involve exploring ways a company and its 
suppliers can collaborate when receiving 
and providing products and services. A 
presented in Box 3, the goal of the thesis 
is to develop a support that will facilitate 
the selection of the most optimal PSS for 
procurement in terms of economic and 
environmental performance. This is to 
increase procurement of more sustainable 
solutions and to support decision-making 
within procurement. 
Resarch questions
As a result of the literature review, a range 
of areas have been found interesting 
and relevant to investigate further. Due 
to the limited findings on the customer 
perspective in B2B PSS relationships 
in literature, an empirical study within 
procurement in industry could be valuable 
for the thesis. Thus, the first research 
question explores the PSS offerings 
or applications within procurement in 
industry. Furthermore, it has been seen 
that the application of PSS should be 
more extensive to fill the identified gaps 
in procurement. Therefore, it is relevant to 
investigate how to support the selection 
of the most optimal PSS, to facilitate the 
prevalence of PSS in procurement, which 
is the second research question. This is 
explored by collecting several factors that 
must be considered when procuring a PSS, 
identifying which mechanisms influence 
the environmental potential of a PSS, 
and finding existing guidelines, methods 
and tools for the selection process in 
literature. As the goal of the thesis is to 
develop a support for selecting PSS in 
procurement, the last research question 
implies evaluating how well the 
support works. For this, it is necessary to 
assess how useful and usable the support 
is in supporting the selection of the most 
appropriate PSS.

Descriptive Study I
In this section the following research questions are investigated to 
understand which PSS are offered or applied in procurement in B2B 
contexts and how to support the selection of the most optimal PSS for 
procurement. As a result of the findings the identified gaps are collected 
and a requirement specification for the support is made.

Empirical data analysis
The research questions are investigated by 
collecting empirical data, looking into both 
industry and literature. RQ 1 is explored 
by looking into what literature offers 
regarding applied or offered PSS solutions 
within procurement in B2B contexts, as 
well as conducting an empirical study 
within procurement in a pharmaceutical 
company. How to support the selection of 
the most optimal PSS is investigated in RQ 
2, where a list of factors to consider when 
procuring a PSS is collected from literature. 
Secondly, the key mechanisms influencing 
the environmental potential of PSS are 
presented. At last, existing guidelines, 
methods and tools for selecting a PSS in 
procurement are collected and presented. 
Applied or offered PSS 
solutions within B2B 
contexts
Office environments
In literature, there are many cases of 
procuring PSS for office environments. 
For instance, as discussed in a study 
conducted by Jacobson, H., Carlson, A., & 
Lindahl, M. (2021), procurement of lighting 
is a good example of a functional result-
based PSS, which can be relevant for public 
institutions, companies and other office 
environments. Good lighting is crucial for 
receiving the correct information about 
one’s surroundings and affects both 
cognitive abilities and biological processes 
in the body. Thus, to work efficiently, 
have good mental and physical health, 
and reduce risk of injury, good lighting is 
necessary.  In order words, lighting has 
several functions that it can or should fulfil, 
and with the potential of more efficient 
use, there are opportunities for economic 
and environmental benefits for both the 
provider and customer. Usually, in such 
provider and customer relationships, the 
ownership of the lighting equipment stays 
with the provider and aims to 
provide the function of light. This entails 
the installation, operation, maintenance, 
and retirement of the lighting (Jacobson, 
H., Carlson, A., & Lindahl, M., 2021). 
Another classic example of the application 
of PSS in office environments is the 
use of printers. Several providers of 
printers offer printers as all-inclusive 
services (Atea: Printere og kopimaskine 
til kontoret., 2022; Comby: PrintBy - 
Printers as a Service., 2022; Maga Print 
& Visuel: RICOH Multifunktionsprintere., 
2022), often through leasing, renting, 
or pay-per-service unit contracts, e.g. 
pay-per-print, implying a use-oriented 
PSS. Depending on the contract,  these 
solutions might include different services 
from the provider. However, common 
features are installation, maintenance, 
repair, expert counselling and take-back 
of the printer by contract expiration. This 
might enhance a flexible, economical and 
environmentally sustainable, and reliable 
solution for many customers (Comby: 
PrintBy - Printers as a Service, 2022).
A third example is Software-as-a-Service 
(SaaS), which has become an emerging
Descriptive Study I
38
==PAGE:25==
trend within IT. SaaS enables users to 
access an application through the Internet 
that resides and runs on a SaaS provider’s 
servers and use the application in return 
for a fee based on usage level. This 
requires low investment for the customer 
in hardware, IT infrastructure, computer 
servers, and IT personnel for maintaining 
IT operations, while still having access 
to the function/service (Van de Weerd, I., 
Mangula, I. S., & Brinkkemper, S., 2016).     
In industry
Companies that need heating, ventilation, 
air conditioning and cooling for their 
business or operation can for example 
be offered an integrated, self-regulating 
solution including all these elements by 
the company, Danfoss (Danfoss: HVACR-
integration., 2022). Thus, they are provided 
a functional result that fulfils the need of 
heat regulation in a room. This eliminates 
the need for procuring equipment, tools 
and services separately to fulfil these 
functions, e.g. HVACs, air conditioners, 
heat pumps, tubes, filters, etc., as well as 
installation, operation and maintenance 
of the system. This reduces customer 
complexity and heating cost by 90%, 
according to Danfoss, and excess heat 
can be reused for other heating purposes 
internally or sold externally (Danfoss: 
HVACR-integration., 2022).
Other 
examples 
are 
companies 
or 
customers that need to move water from 
A to B and therefore have a need to buy 
pumps with additional services, from for 
example the company, Grundfos. This 
can be for several purposes including 
e.g. providing drinking water for villages 
and high skyscrapers, or treating and 
removing wastewater. The additional 
services entail optimisation and repair, 
and digital and operation services. Since 
services, according to Grundfos, constitute 
the largest part of their offerings, they 
describe their solutions as “helping our 
partners and customers move water to 
where it is supposed to go” (Grundfos: 
Solutions., 2022). Reading this definition 
of Grundfos’ solutions, it is clear that their 
focus is moved away from the product 
itself and rather towards the functionality 
of their product and services combined in 
a PSS.
Other PSS offered in industry are chemical 
management services (CMS), where the 
provider not only sells the chemicals 
but is in charge of specific handling and 
management tasks connected to chemicals 
instead of the customer. This saves the 
customer human resources, management 
time and most probably inventory space, 
which often costs five to ten times more 
than the chemicals themselves (Tukker, 
A., 2004). Another PSS is chemical leasing, 
where the provider instead sells the 
functions performed by the chemicals, as 
well as the management of the chemicals 
throughout its lifecycle (OECD., 2017). 
Stoughton, M., & Votta, T. (2003) argue that 
there is a high potential for the reduction 
of chemical use in such PSS, which is 
strongly desirable for environmental and 
public welfare reasons. On the contrary, 
the conventional relationship between 
supplier and chemical customer creates 
supply-side 
incentives 
for 
increased 
chemical use. Thus, offering chemical 
services as part of a PSS, such as CMS 
and leasing, aligns economic incentives 
with reduced chemical use rather than 
focusing on chemical volume and mass 
sales. In other words, CMS and chemical 
leasing create an industry whose focus 
is rather efficient chemical use, sound 
chemical information management, risk 
management and regulatory compliance 
(OECD., 2017; Stoughton, M., & Votta, T., 
2003).
Results from empirical studies
A 
case 
study 
in 
a 
pharmaceutical 
company was conducted as part of the 
research in this project, to identify other 
applied PSS in procurement in B2B 
contexts. The goal was to get an overview 
of which types of PSS are offered and 
applied in procurement, and whether the 
implementation of it was successful or not. 
Three different procurement departments 
in a pharmaceutical company took part in 
a survey, where 10 different participants 
from the departments contributed to 
the study in total. The results from the 
survey showed that the majority of 
laboratory equipment is purchased new 
(88%), with only a very small percentage 
of new equipment being PSS, e.g. rented 
or leased (Appendix F). However, when 
purchasing PSS, as depicted in Figure 11, 
the entire scale of PSS types is applied in 
procurement in the company, with 
product related services and advice as the 
most frequent applications, corresponding 
to 90% and 80% of the procurements, 
respectively. Other commonly applied PSS 
are product lease and outsourcing, with 50% 
of the participants having procured these. 
The least applied PSS is product sharing/
renting. Examples of product oriented 
PSS are machinery covered by service 
level agreements, and manufacturing 
equipment including service, maintenance 
and training. 
Applied use oriented PSS are mostly 
renting and leasing of equipment. Building 
management system, automation system 
for production, environmental monitoring 
system, and heating and cooling system 
for purification plants are examples 
of result oriented PSS. Use of contract 
manufacturing organisations (CMOs) were 
also frequently mentioned in extended 
interviews conducted as a follow-up of the 
study, where drug manufacturing services 
are outsourced and provided on

participants that have been offered the PSS type (Appendix F)

explained that the services included in the 
offerings did not cover all their needs. The 
participants who, however, implemented
the PSS have provided their comments on 
their criteria for that decision. Numerous 
participants mentioned economy based 
criteria, such as competitive total cost of 
ownership (TCO) or overall cost reduction. 
Another criterion mentioned was the 
potential for reduction of environmental 
impact. Product related factors, such 
as 
product 
quality, 
state-of-the-art 
technology, power consumption, and 
product longevity were also criteria 
mentioned by several participants for 
procuring PSS, in addition to the level 
of support and services included in 
the offerings. One participant argued 
that as long as the value obtained by 
implementing the PSS is higher than the 
cost and efforts endured in the process, it 
makes sense procuring the PSS. All criteria 
mentioned in the study are collected in 
Box 4.

that procured the PSS considered the 
implementation 
successful.

was 
explained by higher value obtained 
through 
optimised 
operation, 
cost 
reduction, increased quality, lifecycle 
management of products and services, 
increased supplier responsibility, more 
support and services included in the 
offering, 
and 
identified 
lower 
cost 
and environmental footprint. The one 
participant perceiving the implementation 
of PSS as unsuccessful justified that the 
PSS, in the case of product lease, was

The decision process for procuring 
equipment 
After conducting the described study, 
six extended interviews were arranged 
with stakeholders within procurement to 
obtain more details around the current 
decision process of procuring PSS. Four 
procurement 
responsibles 
and 
two 
equipment responsibles from line of 
business participated in the interviews 
(Appendix G.1, Appendix G.2 and Appendix 
G.3). The interviewees were first asked 
to explain the basis for their decision of 
investigating other procurement models 
than a regular transactional purchase of 
pure product. The main reasons brought 
up were related to time frame for using 
the equipment. For instance, in situations 
where equipment is needed for a project, 
which takes place within a shorter period 
of time, owning the equipment would not 
be the best option. Furthermore, there 
is a risk for the project to be shut down 
before expected, which would mean 
that the equipment would no longer be 
necessary to use. The same applies for 
situations where equipment is needed for 
testing, experiments and research within 
a limited time period.  In these cases the 
equipment is only needed for that specific 
purpose. The equipment itself could also 
be tested and tried out before potentially 
purchasing the equipment. Other reasons 
for investigating PSS were limited budget 
and lack of capacity and infrastructure 
for acquiring the equipment, while still 
needing urgent access to the technology. 
Also, if upgrading the equipment or 
other type of additional services from 
the supplier are needed, it is relevant 
for them to look into other relational 
supplier agreements. The current reasons 
for investigating PSS in procurement are 
collected in Box 5.
Secondly, the stakeholders were asked 
about reasons preventing them from 
investigating PSS, as depicted in Box 6. The 
main reasons were related to situations 
where they are dealing with very
Box 5 Reasons for considering 
procurement of PSS
•	
Testing and experiments in shorter 
timeframe
•	
Testing of equipment 
•

•

•	
Urgent need for access to the needed 
equipment
•

•

•
