•	
Added service from supplier 
specialised equipment and where there 
are only few suppliers that are able to 
deliver it.  Several times, equipment also 
has to be compatible with another piece 
of equipment or software, that only a 
specific supplier can provide. Thus, it is 
difficult for stakeholders in procurement 
to source competitively when the end 
users know exactly what product they 
want, and are already at an early stage 
of the process determined on specific 
and trusted suppliers that can deliver 
it. Other reasons for not considering 
other 
procurement 
models 
were 
situations when there is a budget to buy 
and own. This situation might also be 
applicable in other companies with high 
cashflows, where it might be difficult to 
understand the purpose of investigating 
other alternatives, when all types of 
equipment can be afforded. PSS is also an 
unfamiliar territory, and there is limited 
experience in the area. The interviewees 
further explained that products always 
have been bought and owned, which is 
rarely questioned. This is because the 
current procurement system is designed 
for buying and owning products. As a 
consequence, a product-oriented mindset
has been rigidly rooted in all processes 
within this system. As a continuation of 
these observations, it made sense to 
map the current procurement process in 
collaboration with the stakeholders, to 
gain an overview of the different steps and 
information flow between the different 
roles, which is presented in Figure 14 and 
Figure 15.
Procurement of equipment is either 
sourced 
for 
equipment 
substitution 
purposes or for expansion of facilities, 
as seen in Figure 14 and Figure 15, 
respectively. As show in the swim lane 
diagram in Figure 14, the procurement 
process for substitution is initiated when 
the 
equipment 
responsible 
or 
end 
user identifies a need for equipment 
substitution. This is usually the case when 
equipment breaks down, when it is no 
longer compatible to other equipment, 
or if the supplier no longer supports the 
equipment, e.g. spare parts are outdated 
or can no longer be ordered. Then, the 
equipment responsible contacts trusted 
supplier, which creates an offer. The 
offer is then reviewed by the equipment 
responsible, who applies for money in

local budgets. Finance considers and 
potentially approves the application, and 
procurement sources the requested offer. 
Thus, the end user goes into a product 
oriented mode as the first step of the 
process by determining which product 
should substitute the old equipment. 
The consequence is that other available 
offerings, e.g. PSS, which might potentially 
better fulfil the need, are not investigated. 
Figure 15 Swim lane diagram of the procurement process in relation to product expansion and identified breakdowns
As seen in the swim lane diagram in 
Figure 15, the procurement process for 
expansion is initiated when equipment 
responsible or end user identifies a need 
for expansion of facilities. In these cases, 
the end user is more open to using other 
types of products, and procurement is 
involved earlier in the decision process, 
compared to the substitution cases. 
Procurement investigates and identifies
at least two different suppliers that can 
provide the same product, in order to 
live up to the standards of sourcing 
competitively. Regardless, PSS options are 
rarely investigated. Upon receiving offers

from the suppliers, TCO calculations 
are made to compare cost for the 
different alternatives. In some cases, 
environmental considerations are also 
made, by mostly looking at ecolabels for 
the supplier and the equipment. However, 
several 
stakeholders 
stressed 
the 
problem that evaluating environmental 
impact is a challenge due to the many 
different metrics to measure and the 
fact that they currently have no tools to 
conduct an environmental evaluation. 
Thus, environmental impact is currently 
only measured based on supplier labels, 
which rarely give a nuanced picture of 
the environmental performance of the 
individual 
offerings 
themselves. 
This 
creates a rather weak environmental 
evaluation 
of 
the 
different 
supplier 
offerings. 
The decision process for procuring 
consumables 
Consumables are many cases required for 
running and operating the equipment. 
Two interviews regarding procurement of 
consumables as PSS were therefore also 
conducted (Appendix G.4). Consumables 
as PSS was an unfamiliar topic in the 
laboratories, thus the interviews were 
made 
with 
stakeholders 
in 
charge 
of sourcing consumables for coffee 
machines and printers due to more 
frequent application of PSS in these areas 
in particular. 
The main consideration when deciding 
to procure coffee consumables as PSS 
is minimised efforts on their side. For 
instance, if establishing a pay-per-coffee 
PSS agreement, the coffee machine and 
consumables would often come as a 
package, where all activity management, 
e.g. inventory, logistics, provision and 
refill of consumables, would be supplier 
responsibility. In addition, the supplier 
would most likely offer lifecycle services 
and maintenance to the equipment as 
part of the business model. However, 
due to the supplier’s cash inflow being 
directly linked to the consumption of 
coffee, the business model would be bad 
for the supplier in cases where there is 
low consumption on specific machines, 
which could threaten the agreement. 
Thus, they would probably want to have 
an impact of the location of the machine, 
and behaviour of consumption. There are
also situations where the company would 
like the flexibility to choose between 
different coffee brands due to cost and 
quality. As the pay-per-service business 
model in most cases involve back-to-back 
agreements with specific coffee bean 
producers, meaning that they usually 
only work with one brand of coffee beans, 
this PSS would not be the best option in 
those situations. Instead, consumables 
could be bought separately from the 
machine according to consumption, and 
the company would have the freedom 
to change brands and suppliers and 
adjust quality. This would be a regular 
transactional purchase of consumables, 
where inventory management, logistics 
and refill are handled internally. Access 
to the coffee machine could, however, 
be 
ensured 
through 
other 
supplier 
agreements, e.g. leasing or renting, where 
different lifecycle services are included, 
such as regularly maintenance, repair, 
and upgrade.    
A combination of a pay-per-service unit 
and a leasing agreement is also possible 
when 
purchasing 
consumables 
to 
equipment. This has been observed for 
procurement of printers, where the 
hardware (printer) is leased and the 
printing is covered by a click fee, where 
a sum is paid per print (Appendix G.5). 
In addition, toners are included in the 
click fee. The main reasons for choosing 
this business model are the increased 
availability 
of 
maintenance, 
up-time 
reliability, upgradability, and a 15% lower 
spend compared to a traditional purchase. 
Thus, this procurement model for printers 
tends to be the preferred option and is 
considered the state-of-the-art industry 
standard. Although the described PSS 
solutions are used for coffee machines 
and printers, it is presumed that the 
same principles within the procurement 
models could be applied for sourcing 
consumables to other types of equipment 
as well, e.g. laboratory equipment. 
Support for selecting the 
most optimal PSS
Key factors to consider when 
procuring PSS
Key factors to consider when procuring 
PSS are described in Box 7, and were 
collected 
based 
on 
their 
frequent 
occurrence in literature, provided by 
Jacobson, H., Carlson, A., & Lindahl, 
M. (2021); Jasim Hadi, H. et al. (2020); 
Pagoropoulos, A. (2017), and Tukker, A. 
(2004). These key factors are supposed to 
highlight elements that are important to 
have in mind and guide decisions when 
procuring PSS.  
Cost 
When procuring integrated solutions, 
such as PSS, it is important to keep in mind 
that the cost structure will change (Wise, 
R., & Baumgartner, P., 1999). In traditional 
product sales, the physical product itself 
only accounts for a small proportion of the 
total expenditures. In fact, the largest part 
of the expenses is hidden in the operating 
expenditures (OpEx) spent on services, 
e.g. product related services, operation, 
administration, insurance, maintenance,
Box 7 Factors to consider when procuring 
            a PSS
•	
Cost
•	
Environmental impact
•	
Legal regulation
•	
Supplier relationship
•	
Security, privacy or confidentiality
•	
Organisational readiness
•	
Risks and uncertainties
and repair. The procurement of a PSS 
would rearrange this cost structure, as 
the value delivery would be changed. 
Looking at a product-oriented PSS, some 
of the previous OpEx would be included in 
the provider’s product related services. In 
a use-oriented PSS, the acquisition cost of 
the hardware would be eliminated,
Descriptive Study I
Descriptive Study I
47
48
==PAGE:30==
processes 
and 
systems 
related 
to 
planning and building budgets need to 
be restructured to accommodate this 
change. Several companies experience this 
transformation as a significant challenge. 
The typical cost distribution for traditional 
product sales (presented by Wise, R., & 
Baumgartner, P., 1999) compared to an 
anticipated cost distribution for PSS is 
seen in Figure 17. The same cost structure 
has also been observed for laboratory 
equipment in R&D in the case company, 
as illustrated in Figure 16, where the 
equipment itself only accounts for 24% 
of the annual total cost of ownership. 
The main contributors to the total cost 
are 
services 
and 
maintenance, 
and 
consumables and chemicals.  
Lindahl, M., Sundin, E., & Sakao, T. 
(2014) claim that when compared to the 
traditional product sales, PSS has the 
ability to reduce cost, seen from a lifecycle 
perspective.  Kambanou, M. L., & Lindahl, 
M. (2016) argue that if a PSS is optimally 
designed so that the products and services 
are integrated, the cost of the PSS should 
be less than the sum of products and 
service cost from the pre-PSS business
49
PSS cost < service cost + product cost 	
           (I)	
and the physical product and services 
would 
normally 
become 
integrated 
into a package. A similar cost structure 
would apply for a result-oriented PSS. As 
a result of procuring a more functional 
sales business model, such as PSS, the 
customer will pay for all the resources 
required to make the hardware deliver 
the function, whereas for the ordinary 
production sales, these costs would not 
appear on the price tag (Jacobson, H., 
Carlson, A., & Lindahl, M., 2021). Thus, 
including more services into the PSS offer 
would make the OpEx more visible, as 
these would be a part of the customer’s 
fixed expenditures. This would make 
the monthly expenses more predictable 
and enhance cost control. The fact that 
that the OpEx becomes more visible for 
the PSS, whereas the OpEx connected 
to a traditional pure product purchase is 
less transparent, will sometimes falsely 
represent the PSS as the most expensive 
option, which is important to be aware of. 
Another aspect that is important to have 
in mind is that, when procuring PSS, the 
customer will most probably undergo 
a shift from having fixed cost to more 
variable cost. This means that the

equipment in R&D of the case company 
(Appendix H)
Figure 17 Cost structure of personal computers, locomotives and automobiles (Wise, R., & Baumgartner, P., 1999) put conceptually up against potential cost structures of PSS

model, as described in Equation I. In order 
for it to make sense to procure a PSS, the 
overall cost should be lower compared to 
procuring in the traditional manner. Thus, 
cost reduction is a keyfactor to consider 
when procuring PSS. However, cost
reduction is not always the the case, as 
seen in the study of applied or offered PSS 
in procurement, where some participants 
experienced a cost increase (Appendix F). 
Pagoropoulos, A. (2017) therefore stresses 
the importance of ensuring that the
cost improvements have a lifecycle outlook 
and that they do not result in cost shifting. 
The expected economic potential of PSS is 
also illustrated conceptually in Figure 17.

Environmental impact
When procuring PSS, the design of the 
business model needs to be appropriate 
to avoid higher material consumption 
compared to the traditional pure product 
purchase and negative rebound effects 
(Jacobson, H., Carlson, A., & Lindahl, M., 
2021). Rebound effects occur when the 
new solution incentivises the customer 
to consume more. As seen in Figure 18, 
Kjaer, L. L., Pigosso, D. C. A., & McAloone, 
T. C. (2017) distinguish between two types 
of rebound effects: Direct and indirect. 
The former occurs as a direct response to
As depicted in Figure 19, the goal is 
rather to have more avoided impacts 
than induced impacts and, preferably, 
secondary benefits as well (Kjaer, L. 
L., Pigosso, D. C. A., & McAloone, T. C., 
2017). The authors explain that avoided 
impacts occur when a PSS replaces or 
reduces the need for processes in the 
reference system, and secondary benefits 
occur when the new solution makes the 
customer avoid additional consumption. 
An example of a secondary benefit would 
be if a car owner is motivated to drive less 
by using an energy efficient car.  
a change, e.g. when a car owner buys 
a more fuel-efficient car but increase 
the amount of driving, meaning that 
the absolute fuel consumption remains 
unchanged or has even increased. The 
latter, however, occurs as an indirect 
response to a change, such as increasing 
consumption in other areas, e.g. saved 
money on fuel when using a more fuel-
efficient car is spent on consumption 
of other goods or services. The size and 
impact of rebound effect depend on how 
the money is spent.

Figure 19 Comparing a PSS with a reference system in terms of environmental impact (Kjaer, L. L., 
Pigosso, D. C. A., & McAloone, T. C., 2017)
Legal regulation
A challenge when procuring PSS might 
be the lack of legal framework regulating 
this type of business model. The ordinary 
product sales business model, however, 
is supported by long-established rules 
and regulations as the current legislation 
is based on and developed within the 
prevailing societal and economic customs, 
which have been in place for more than 150 
years. In such transactions, the customer 
contacts the provider, an agreement 
is made, the products and services are 
delivered, and money is transferred 
from customer to provider. Primarily, the 
contract regulates the specifics of the 
product, e.g. delivery time, quality, price, 
and payment information. This makes 
such legal acquisition quite conventional 
and uneventful. The PSS business model, 
on the other hand, is still relatively new, 
and there is little knowledge about the 
legal aspects regarding the relationship 
between provider and customer, e.g. how 
contracts should be formulated. From 
a legal perspective, a PSS bears several 
characteristics that are different from the 
ordinary sales transaction as it presents a 
new way to make products and services 
Descriptive Study I
Descriptive Study I
51
52
==PAGE:32==
accessible at the market (Jacobson, H., 
Carlson, A., & Lindahl, M., 2021). According 
to Jacobson, H., Carlson, A., & Lindahl, 
M. (2021), the fact that the ownership 
of the material goods often stays with 
the provider in these business models, 
instead of transferring the ownership 
from provider to customer, will colour 
the agreement between the parties in 
different ways, changing the agreement 
contents. In addition to that, the PSS 
relationships remain for a longer period 
of time, involving more cooperation and 
communication, which also should be 
reflected in the contract. The authors 
fear that by changing the business model 
without a proper legal foundation, the 
parties of such contracts might find 
themselves in a legal wilderness, where the 
outcome of civil litigation is unpredictable. 
For instance, it might not be defined what 
time limits are to be adhered to, what will 
happen if the function of the delivery is 
substandard according to the contract, or 
if the payment is late. Due to the lack of 
regulations connected to PSS, the picture 
becomes blurry when describing the legal 
implications of such contracts, which 
complicates matters for parties involved
in the PSS. For instance, Jacobson, H., 
Carlson, A., & Lindahl, M. (2021) mention 
that, according to Swedish law, if an 
object is attached to a real estate by a 
party different from the owner of the real 
estate, the ownership of this object will 
be transferred to the owner of the real 
estate if it has been attached in a way that 
does not allow easy removal. Seen in the 
case of laboratory equipment, this could 
be applicable for procuring large pieces 
of equipment as a PSS, that need to be 
installed and fixed to physical premises. 
Thus, such rules should be investigated 
before signing any contract. Since the PSS 
contracts can be challenging, Jacobson, H., 
Carlson, A., & Lindahl, M. (2021) emphasise 
the importance of clearly stating the 
rights and obligations of each party, 
and define what the demanded function 
should deliver, and which methods to be 
used for evaluating this. The authors sum 
up several contractual risks that need to 
be considered when procuring a PSS, as 
depicted in Box 8. 
Box 8 Contractual risks when procuring 
           PSS (Jacobson, H., Carlson, A., &   
           Lindahl, M., 2021)
•	
PSS business models are not regulated in 
any legal system
•	
In some cases, it is difficult to establish 
who is considered to be the owner of the 
products delivering the function
•	
The function to be delivered might not be 
specified sufficiently
•	
The evaluation of the function might be 
defined in an unclear manner
Then, designing the offer in accordance 
with the customer needs over time, and 
having an ongoing discussion with the 
customer is crucial for the success of the 
PSS. This will promote adaptability for the 
provider, as the continuous communication 
will create the possibility for adjusting to 
changes in requirements, new situations 
and environments over time. It is clear that 
this collaborative relationship requires 
constant involvement, interaction, and 
engagement from all parties. The benefits 
are long term partnerships and building 
trust between the parties. This differs 
significantly from the traditional product 
sales, which contains a minimum of 
human interaction during a short-term 
perspective, as it only covers the process 
of negotiation and selling or buying the 
goods (Jacobson, H., Carlson, A., & Lindahl, 
M. 2021). 	
Supplier relationship
Another aspect to be aware of is the 
change 
in 
supplier 
and 
customer 
relationship 
when 
contracting 
PSS 
business models, according to Jacobson, 
H., Carlson, A., & Lindahl, M. (2021). A PSS 
contract opens for cooperation between 
the parties of the contract, which means 
that the parties will have to communicate 
with each other along the duration of the 
contract. Thus, it builds upon a different 
view of the relationship between the 
contracting parties than the traditional. 
The authors illustrate that the traditional 
contract can be perceived as a tug-of-war 
between the parties, where the customer 
wants to invest as little as possible for 
the purchase, while the provider has the 
opposite notion. The consequence is a 
minimum acceptable condition for quality, 
price, and details of delivery for each 
party. The PSS contract, on the other hand 
depends more on the cooperation, and 
continuous communication between the 
contracting parties. During procurement, 
the customer has to formulate what 
functions are required and the provider 
needs to be precise about how and to 
what extent these requests can be met. 
Security, privacy, and confidentiality
In more outcome based and result 
oriented PSS, the role of the provider 
is centred around the optimisation and 
on the support of different capabilities 
so that the customers can achieve their 
desired functions. Therefore, it is crucial 
for the provider to develop knowledge 
and skills related to the customer’s line 
of business essentials (Avlonitis, V. et al., 
2012). As a result, the provider would 
be more involved into the customer’s 
processes and daily operations (Graça, 
S. S., 2021). This requires the customer 
to be transparent about their processes 
and share data. Therefore, Jasim Hadi, 
H. et al. (2020) point out security, privacy 
and confidentiality as important factors 
influencing the intention to adopt a 
PSS. For instance, a concern for many 
companies is data security, and losing 
control of confidential information. Thus, 
to avoid these security issues, it is crucial 
with a robust non-disclosure agreement 
connected 
to 
the 
contract, 
where 
each party agrees not to disclose any 
information covered by the agreement 
(Jacobson, H., Carlson, A., & Lindahl, M., 
2021). Then, the customer can trust the 
Descriptive Study I
Descriptive Study I
53
54
==PAGE:33==
provider with confidential matters and 
business can take place securely and 
reliably (Jasim Hadi, H. et al., 2020). 
Ensuring reliable business and trust will 
also promote a sustainable and stronger 
relationship 
between 
customer 
and 
supplier (Graça, S. S., 2021).
Organisational readiness
Van de Weerd, I., Mangula, I. S., 
& 
Brinkkemper, 
S. 
(2016) 
mention 
organisational readiness as a key factor 
influencing the procurement of PSS. The 
authors describe organisational readiness 
as the availability of (a) sufficient financial 
resources for adopting innovations, (b) 
human resources with the sufficient 
skills, experience and knowledge for 
implementing 
and 
integrating 
PSS 
applications, and (c) sufficient installed and 
in-use enterprise systems and network 
technologies on which PSS applications 
can be built. In the existing literature, a 
common idea is that the absence of one or 
more of these resources is likely to hinder 
a company to adopt an innovation, such as 
a PSS. However, a qualitative comparative 
analysis conducted by Van deWeerd, I., 
Mangula, I. S., & Brinkkemper, S. (2016) in 
a case of implementing SaaS showed 
that the lack of budget allocation for 
IT infrastructure and the necessary 
expertise in the company actually led 
to SaaS adoption. This is because the 
necessary infrastructure would be made 
available by the provider, as well as the 
required competencies and resources 
for implementing and handling the PSS. 
Because procurement of PSS, such as 
SaaS, often require low investment in 
necessary infrastructure, lack of budget 
allocation would be a driver for PSS 
adoption. In addition, lack of necessary 
competencies was used as motivations 
to procure PSS. This means that the 
level of organisational readiness should 
be challenged when investigating the 
possibilities for procurement of PSS, 
and companies can be advised not to 
necessarily reject procuring PSS if they 
believe their organisational readiness to 
be low (Van de Weerd, I., Mangula, I. S., & 
Brinkkemper, S., 2016).
Risks and uncertainties
The willingness to adopt an innovation is 
related to how the risks and uncertainties 
associated with this new innovation are 
handled (Van de Weerd, I., Mangula, I. S., 
& Brinkkemper, S., 2016). According to 
Goodwin, N. (2017), any successful strategy 
for change and how well these risks and 
uncertainties are handled depends on the 
organisational readiness, as described, 
e.g. the resources and competencies 
at disposal and the environment in 
which it is operating. Although risks and 
uncertainties are associated with the 
change itself, risks, uncertainties and 
responsibilities are often reduced or 
completely transferred from the customer 
to the provider in the application of a PSS 
business model. This is a result of the 
supplier taking over the management of 
more activities for the customer, hence 
sharing of risks and incentives becomes 
essential (Avlonitis, V. et al. 2012). The 
degree of difficulty of controlling these 
liabilities might be reflected in the risk 
premium in the price of the PSS (Tukker, 
A., 2004).  
Mechanisms influencing the 
environmental potential of a PSS
The findings from two papers are 
collectively presented in this section. 
Tukker, A. (2004) presents different 
mechanisms 
influencing 
the 
environmental 
performance 
of 
PSS, 
including a tentative indication of the 
magnitude 
of 
environmental 
impact 
reductions. Koide, R., Murakami, S., & 
Nansai, K. (2022) have also identified 
circular economy strategies with different 
potential for impact reduction in terms of 
GHG improvements, as well as potential 
risk for backfire or rebound effect related 
to the strategies. The latter mentioned 
authors have calculated improvement 
rates based on a scenario-level analysis 
conducted for different modes of PSS 
combined with different circular economy 
strategies, compared to a reference 
situation. The reference situation in this 
case is the conventional product sales. 
The scale of the improvement rates and 
backfire rates is depicted in Table 7. 
Mechanisms leading to incremental/
moderate impact improvements 
(4%-40%)
Most 
product-oriented 
circular 
economy strategies (CE) such as design 
for durability and refurbishment and 
remanufacturing 
showed 
incremental 
impact improvements in the studies 
conducted by Koide, R., Murakami, S., & 
Nansai, K. (2022), with an improvement 
rate of 0,04 and 0,4 respectively. This is 
due to the fact that the strategies can 
lead to a prolonged product life, reducing 
the material consumption. Tukker, A. 
(2004) also identifies maintenance as a 
mechanism leading to a prolonged 
Table 7 Scale for improvement rates and backfire rates (Koide, R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K., 2022)
product life, contributing to an incremental 
impact reduction of 10-20%. This can 
also result in incremental efficiency 
improvements, meaning that less energy 
and consumables are needed in the use 
phase. The use-oriented PSS, leasing, was 
also categorised with incremental impact 
potential with an impact improvement 
rate of 0,03. Along with the impact 
improvements, Koide, R., Murakami, S., & 
Nansai, K. (2022) present the proportion 
rate of backfire scenarios for the CE 
strategies. The backfire rate of design for 
durability was identified as very high (0,4), 
leasing as moderate (0,1-0,2)1, while the 
backfire rate of refurbishment and
Descriptive Study I
Descriptive Study I
55
56
==PAGE:34==
remanufacturing 
was 
classified 
as 
incremental (0,06). The reason for this 
high backfire rate for design for durability 
might be connected to cases where large 
amounts of material and resources are 
spent on producing durable products that 
are disposed of much earlier than what 
is expected when creating the product. 
The moderate backfire rate and low 
improvement potential for leasing might 
be explained by the fact that the lessee 
might lack incentives to use the product 
in a careful way compared to a situation 
where the lessee owns the product 
(Tukker, A., 2004).  Tukker, A. (2004) does 
not mention any backfire or rebound 
effect related to maintenance. The impact 
improvements and backfire rates for 
the circular economy mechanisms are 
depicted in Figure 20. 
Mechanisms leading to moderate/high 
impact improvements (40%-85%)
As seen in Figure 20, the CE strategies that 
proved to have moderate/high impact 
improvements are product renting (0,4), 
pooling (0,85), sharing (0,85), and repairing, 
upgrading and modularity (0,6) (Koide, R., 
Murakami, S., & Nansai, K., 2022). The use-
oriented mechanisms, renting, pooling, 
and sharing can lead to more intensive 
use, increasing the value utilisation 
of the product, hence the moderate 
to high impact improvements. Use of 
considerably more efficient technology 
made possible by a higher economy of 
scale might also be a reason for these 
impact improvements. Tukker, A. (2004) 
justifies the high impact improvements 
for pooling and sharing with less needed 
energy and other auxiliary materials in 
the use phase. The author also mention 
use-oriented strategies, such as pay-per-
service unit, as having average to high 
impact reduction potential as the provider 
has strong incentives for optimising 
consumption and performance to reduce 
lifecycle cost as much as possible. The 
product-oriented CE strategies, repairing, 
upgrading and modularity, have a
moderate improvement rate because they 
can contribute to a prolonged product 
life. Looking at the backfire rates, renting 
turned out to be severe with a rate of 0,3, 
sharing was moderate with a rate of 0,2, 
while repairing, upgrading and modularity 
had no studies reporting rate of backfire 
effects. The reason for the severity of 
the backfire effect from renting might be 
similar to the one connected to leasing, 
where products are frequently disposed 
of due to uncareful use and damage of 
the product. Tukker, A., 2004 does not 
mention the backfire effects of pay-per-
service unit.  
1 Assumption 1: Koide, R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. (2022) 
do not mention the exact number of the backfire rate of 
leasing, but characterise it as “not associated with such severe 
possibility of backfire effect”, hence moderate could be argued 
to be the most appropriate level for impact improvements.
Figure 20 Improvement rates and backfire rates of different CE strategies, extracted from Koide, R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. (2022)

Mechanisms leading to very high impact 
improvements (90%-100%) 
Both Tukker, A. (2004) and Koide, R., 
Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. (2022) found 
mechanisms related to result-oriented PSS 
to have very high impact improvements, 
with a rate of 0,9. This is because a result 
is promised, and the provider can decide 
the necessary approach to deliver the 
result. Thus, the provider will have strong 
incentives for delivering the result in the 
most cost-effective way, which stimulates 
application 
of 
radically 
different 
technological system with radically lower 
impacts, e.g. a functional result (Tukker, A., 
2004). Koide, R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. 
(2022) also present the product-oriented 
CE strategy, reuse, as a mechanism with 
very high impact improvement, with a 
score of 1. This might be due to the fact that 
reusing a product eliminates the need for 
a new virgin product, which is an efficient 
way of removing all environmental impact 
occurring in the lifecycle stages involved 
in producing a new product. Although the 
CE strategies connected to result-oriented 
PSS have very high potential for impact 
improvement, they also have high risk of 
serious rebound effects, with a backfire
Table 8 Environmental impact improvement potential for different PSS types, based by collected results from 
Koide, R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. (2022) and Tukker, A. (2004)  
rate of 0,6 (Koide, R., Murakami, S., & 
Nansai, K., 2022). Reuse however, had a 
moderate backfire rate of 0,1.
The results from Koide, R., Murakami, S., 
& Nansai, K. (2022) and Tukker, A. (2004) 
show a clear pattern regarding the impact 
improvement potential and the PSS types. 
Table 8 shows all the different types of PSS 
and their impact potential compared to 
the reference situation. In previous 
studies, e.g. Tukker, A. (2004), the 
environmental impact reduction potential 
tend to increase moving towards the right 
side of the PSS scale, referring to Figure 
7 in the literature review of the Reseach 
Clarification. This disposition has been 
confirmed to a high extent by the newer 
studies conducted by Koide, R., Murakami, 
S., & Nansai, K. (2022). However, the
authors present a few extruders: repairing, 
upgrading and modularity, that tend 
to be supporting activities in product-
related services, proved to have high 
improvement potential, although Tukker, 
A. (2004) discovered the overall picture to 
be that product-related services can lead 
to impact reductions, but are likely to be 
incremental at best. 
Reasons for backfire effects
Koide, R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. (2022) 
have identified reasons for deviations in 
global warming potentials and backfire 
effects on the different CE strategies, as 
depicted in the heat map in Figure 21. 
The rows in the heat map represent the 
reported reasons for deviation in global 
warming potentials and backfire effects, 
and the columns show the CE strategies. 
The colours in the heatmap indicate the 
proportion of studies that reported the 
relevant reasons, with 83 reports in total, 
and with the darker colour indicating 
the higher proportion. Overall, transport 
related factors, corresponding to 23% 
of the studies, and number of uses, also 
corresponding to 23% of the studies, were 
the most frequently reported reasons for 
Figure 21 Heat map of reasons for deviations in global warming potential and backfire effects
(Koide, R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K., 2022)
potential backfire effect. These factors 
were in particular applicable for result 
and use-oriented PSS, e.g. pooling, renting, 
sharing and leasing. Transport can cause 
backfire effects depending on factors 
such as transport mode, distance, and 
efficiency for accessing service spots 
and delivery or pick-up of products. The 
different locations of service, delivery, and 
pick-up points might lead to additional 
Descriptive Study I
Descriptive Study I
59
60
==PAGE:36==
travel distance. The number of uses can 
result in backfire effects especially for 
use-oriented PSS, as  products might not 
be designed for such a high intensity 
usage, increasing the risk for damage and 
frequent disposal of the products. Product 
substitution was also an important factor 
influencing several CE strategies (18%) as 
the introduction of a PSS would replace 
current well-functional products, creating 
a significant rebound effect. The fourth 
most important reason for potential 
backfire effects was product lifetime, and 
was mainly related to product-oriented 
CE strategies, e.g. repairing, refurbishing, 
durability, but also to renting schemes. 
Other reasons for potential backfires can 
be seen in Figure 21. 
Existing guidlines, methods, 
and tools for selecting a PSS in 
procurement
As the scope of the thesis is set on selecting 
PSS for procurement based on economic 
and environmental performance, the 
ambition of this study has been to identify 
guidelines, methods and tools that can 
evaluate these parameters, constituting 
a fundament for the selection process. In 
addition, tools for evaluating risks in the 
selection of PSS have been identified. 
Environmental performance
Three different methods for evaluating 
environmental performance have been 
identified (seen in Box 9) and are described 
in this section: Life Cycle Assessment 
(LCA), Six step approach for evaluating 
environmental performance of PSS, Life 
Cycle Screening (LCS), and Material Flow 
Analysis (MFA).
Box 9 Guidelines, methods and tools for 
evaluating environmental performance
•	
Life Cycle Assessment (LCA)
•	
Six-Step Approach 
•	
Life Cycle Screening (LCS)
•	
Material Flow Analysis (MFA)
Life Cycle Assessment (LCA)  
Kjaer, L. L. et al. (2016) describe Life Cycle 
Assessment (LCA) as a state-of-the-art 
method for conducting environmental 
assessments of systems consisting of 
goods or services, or a combination of the 
two.  Hauschild, M. Z., Rosenbaum, R. K., & 
Olsen, S. I. (2018) define LCA as a science 
based, comprehensive, and quantitative 
assessment tool for environmental impact, 
taking a whole lifecycle perspective, that 
covers a broad range of environmental 
issues. The tool is applicable for situations 
such 
as 
product 
development 
and 
improvement, strategic planning, public 
policy making, and marketing. Thus, LCA 
is a valid tool for comparing different PSS 
alternatives against conventional product
sales scenarios, in terms of environment, 
and can be useful in such decision making 
situations. The practitioner must go 
through five separate phases, as depicted 
in Figure 22: Goal definition, scope 
definition, inventory analysis, impact 
assessment, and interpretation. The two 
first phases are about setting the goal 
and scope for the assessment, including a 
clarification of why the study is performed, 
which questions it is intended to answer, 
for whom it is performed, and how the 
assessment is framed and outlined. Then, 
the inventory analysis involves a mapping 
of all physical flows in the product system, 
in terms of input of resources, materials, 
semi-products, and products, and the 
output of emissions, waste, and valuable 
products. The next phase is the impact 
assessment, which translates all physical 
flows and interventions from the inventory 
analysis into impact on the environment. 
Finally, the results of the study are 
interpreted in order to make a conclusion 
of the assessment. The interpretation is 
also integrated into each of the four first 
phases of the LCA. In order to ensure 
comprehensiveness and consistency of 
the study, the LCA technique is iterative
(Hauschild, M. Z., Rosenbaum, R. K., & 
Olsen, S. I., 2018).
Figure 22 Framework of LCA modified from the ISO 14040 standard (Hauschild, M. Z., Rosenbaum, R. K., & 
Olsen, S. I., 2018)
Descriptive Study I
Descriptive Study I
61
62
==PAGE:37==
Despite LCA being a well-suited and 
comprehensive 
tool 
for 
assessing 
environmental performance on tangible 
products, Kjaer, L. L. et al. (2016) argue that 
it lacks specific attention to more complex 
systems, such as PSS, which also consists 
of intangible elements. For instance, 
defining an appropriate reference system, 
ensuring functional equivalence in the 
functional unit (see Box 10)  definition, 
and managing uncertainties and the 
dynamic nature of the assessment, 
might be a challenge as a consequence 
of limited knowledge about the system 
surrounding the PSS and its contextual 
factors. In addition, PSS often pursues 
product 
lifetime 
extensions 
through 
multiple lifecycles, hence challenging the 
determined reference system, which must 
be defined in order to allocate impacts 
between lifecycles. Another challenge may 
occur due to the complex nature of a PSS 
and its reliance on supporting systems as 
this would disturb the system boundaries. 
Thus, for studies evaluating the changes 
caused by the PSS, it should be given 
special attention to defining the reference 
system and whether the reference system 
can be fixed to one comparable alternative
or it needs to be expanded towards 
multiple systems, in order to cover all 
relevant changes and potential rebound 
effects. Furthermore, the functional unit 
should be determined accordingly and 
functional equivalence should be fulfilled 
by composing the reference system of 
existing products and services (Kjaer, L. L. 
et al. 2016).  
Box 10 Functional Unit (FU) (Hauschild, M.  
Z., Rosenbaum, R. K., & Olsen, S. I., 2018)
A functional unit must be defined to support 
a fair and relevant quantitative comparison of 
alternative ways of providing a function. The 
functional unit contains the qualitative aspects 
and quantifies the quantitative aspects of the 
function, which generally involves answering the 
questions “what?”, “how much?”, “for how long/
how many times?”, “where?”, and “how well”? 
Example of a comparison of outdoor paint:
“Complete coverage of 1 m2 primed outdoor wall 
for 10 years in Denmark in a uniform colour at 
99,9% opacity”.
Six-step approach to evaluate environmental 
performance of PSS
As an add-on to LCA, Kjaer, L. L., Pigosso, D. 
C. A., & McAloone, T. C. (2017) suggest a six-
step approach to evaluate environmental 
performance of PSS. As seen in Figure 23, 
the approach links to the LCA framework, 
but has special attention to more complex 
systems, such as PSS: 
1.	 Goal definition
In this step, the purpose and desired 
learnings of the study are decided, and 
the intended audience defined. The 
complexity of the study and the available 
time and resources are considered.
2.	 PSS and reference system exploration
Here, the scope of the study and reference 
system are defined. Thereafter, the PSS 
and its reference system are explored to 
define the systems that are being analysed 
in the next steps.
3.	 Comparability assessment
In this step the functional unit is defined in 
order to be able to compare the analysed 
systems. The aim is to identify differences 
in performance, and potential rebound 
effects.
4.	 Process mapping
The process mapping involves gaining 
an overview of the quantitative data that 
must be gathered. A visual overview of all 
processes is created using a flowchart, 
followed by definition of the system 
boundaries, which is supposed to delimit 
the processes included in the assessment. 
Finally, the relevant impact categories that 
are going to be measured are determined.
5.	 Quantification
The purpose of this step is to collect 
inventory data for all relevant processes 
within the defined system boundaries. 
The type of data and impact assessment 
method are defined.
6.	 Interpretation and recommendations
In the final step, the results are  presented 
and 
interpreted 
quantitatively 
and 
qualitatively in order to highlight the most 
important influencing parameters.
Descriptive Study I
Descriptive Study I
63
64
==PAGE:38==
Figure 23 The six step approach in relation to LCA phases (Kjaer, L. L., Pigosso, D. C. A., & McAloone, T. C., 2017)
Life Cycle Screening (LCS)
In a guide for improving environmental 
impact through product development, 
McAloone, T., & Bey, N. (2008) present 
quantitative environmental assessment 
tools that together provide a Life Cycle 
Screening (LCS) of the environmental 
performance of products and services. The 
LCS method is less comprehensive than 
an LCA but is perceived to be applicable 
for the evaluation of environmental 
performance of a PSS. The identified 
relevant steps found in the tool are as 
follows: 
Step 1: Describe the use context
The first step is about understanding 
how the product is used, by whom it is 
used, and for what period of time. This 
involves defining the functional unit of 
the product. The aim of the step is to 
identify environmental issues in relation 
to the product’s performance to the user, 
and enable a valid comparison between 
different systems.   
Step 2: Create an overview of the 
environmental impacts
The second step involves looking at the 
environmental impacts happening at 
different stages of the lifecycle of the 
product. This requires insight into how 
the product is produced, manufactured, 
distributed, and disposed. 
Step 3: Make an environmental profile 
and find causes
As 
a 
continuation 
of 
Step 
2, 
the 
environmental impacts in the lifecycle 
are divided into four categories in 
a 
MECO 
board: 
Materials, 
Energy, 
Chemicals, and Other. This will create an 
environmental profile. Then, the causes 
to the environmental impacts should be 
identified. 
Step 4: Quantify the environmental 
impacts
The last relevant step for the LCS involves 
putting numbers on the environmental 
impacts and create scenarios for potential 
processes, 
materials, 
and 
lifecycle 
courses. The probability of these scenarios 
happening should be considered.
Material Flow Analysis
Another tool for assessing environmental 
performance 
in 
terms 
of 
resource 
consumption is Material Flow Analysis 
(MFA). The assessment systematically 
maps all flows and stocks of material within 
a system defined in space and time. Due to 
the law of matter,  the results of an MFA can 
be controlled by a simple material balance 
comparing all inputs, stocks, and outputs 
of a process. This makes the method 
suitable for decision support in resource 
management, waste management, and 
environmental management (Brunner, 
P. H., & Rechberger, H., 2004). Thus, in 
the context of procurement, it can be 
beneficial for comparing waste generated 
from the conventional products against a 
PSS.  
The steps involved in an MFA are 
presented in Figure 24. First, the definition 
of the problem and adequate goals are 
made. Then, relevant substances, goods, 
processes, 
and 
appropriate 
system 
boundaries are selected. The second and 
third step involve determination of mass 
flows of goods, and balancing these, 
respectively. This means controlling that
Descriptive Study I
Descriptive Study I
65
66
==PAGE:39==
Figure 24 Procedures for MFA (Brunner, P. H., & Rechberger, H., 2004)
the outputs and process stocks have 
the same material mass as the inputs, 
following the mass-balance principle 
described in Equation II and Figure 25. 
If inputs and outputs do not balance, 
one or several flows are missing or 
calculated incorrectly. Subsequently, the 
substance concentration of the material 
flows are determined and balanced. It 
is important that the steps are executed 
iteratively to accommodate the objectives 
of the project and optimise the accuracy 
of data, by refining mass flows and 
concentrations, 
redetermining 
goods, 
improving the system and data, and 
redefining the problem. The results of 
the MFA are presented in an appropriate 
way to visualise the conclusions and to 
facilitate implementation of goal-oriented 
decisions (Brunner, P. H., & Rechberger, 
H., 2004). 
Economic performance
Cost is an important evaluation metric 
in PSS, and particularly from a life cycle 
perspective. Thus, in order to justify the 
selection of a PSS as a business model 
within procurement, cost improvements 
need to constitute a true change without 
the occurrence of a burden shift in 
the lifecycle (Pagoropoulos, A., 2017). 
Three tools have been identified to be 
relevant for evaluating cost improvement 
in relation to procurement of PSS, as 
depicted in Box 11: Life Cycle Costing 
(LCC), Total Cost of Ownership (TCO) and 
economic investment analysis.
Life Cycle Costing (LCC)
Due to the possibility of a shift in cost 
ownership when procuring a PSS, it is 
necessary to better understand the PSS 
costs spanning from design to the end 
of life. To enable this understanding, 
Life Cycle Costing (LCC) can be used by 
providers and customers (Kambanou, M. 
L., & Lindahl, M., 2016).  Woodward, D. G. 
(1997) refers to LCC as the sum of “all the 
cost factors relating to the asset during its 
operational life” (Woodward, D. G., 1997, 
p. 335). This should be mapped out 
according to the method shown in Figure 
26. LCC has many similarities to LCA, and 
is based on the same logic as LCA. They 
both have the same view of the lifecycle 
(cradle-to-grave), they have equivalent 
system boundaries with a few exemptions, 
and consist of similar phases (McAloone, 
T., & Pigosso, D., 2021):
1.	 Goal and scope definition
2.	 Information gathering
3.	 Interpretation and identification of 
hotspots
4.	 Sensitivity analysis and discussion
Box 11 Guidelines, methods and tools for 
evaluating economic performance
•	
Life Cycle Costing (LCC)
•	
Total Cost of Ownership (TCO)
•	
Economic Investment Analysis

69
According to Kambanou, M. L., & Lindahl, 
M. (2016), most of the uncertainty related 
to LCC is centred around the use stage as 
cost data regarding service, maintenance, 
and repair can be difficult to collect. 
There are also uncertainties connected 
to disposal costs, due to variations in 
quality of the returned products, cost of 
dismantling, etc. There are also discussions 
on whether a potential second life of the 
product should be included in the LCC or 
not. As the possibility of cost-benefits from 
a second life is a strong driver for PSS, 
excluding it from LCC calculations could 
influence the results to a high degree. 
Thus, 
preferences 
regarding 
system 
boundaries create some methodological 
inconsistencies 
connected 
to 
LCC. 
Pagoropoulos, A. (2017) point out that 
another limitation with LCC is that it lacks 
focus on the service orientation of a PSS, 
but rather focuses on capital intensive 
products with high capital costs. This can 
be an issues when calculating LCC for a 
PSS as the underlying technical system is 
not necessarily capital intensive. The LCC 
structure, including all product lifecycle 
phases, is depicted in Figure 26.
Total Cost of Ownership (TCO)
According to Bonetti, S., Perona, M., 
& Saccani, N. (2016), the Total Cost of 
Ownership (TCO) concept can support 
the assessment of PSS, as it adopts a 
lifecycle perspective, thus enabling a 
“whole cost” evaluation. TCO is the sum 
of all costs associated with owning and 
using a product or service throughout 
its lifecycle, and is generally regarded as 
the “true cost” of buying an item from a 
particular supplier. Thus, the concept is 
suitable for comparing the total cost of 
a traditional product offering and of PSS 
business models, and also of different 
PSS alternatives. TCO models are typically 
based on the data described in Box 12.
Box 12 Relevant data for TCO models   
(Bonetti, S., Perona, M., & Saccani, N., 
2016)
Technical 
data, 
e.g. 
standard 
production 
capacity of a machine, which depend on 
technological features of the considered product
Operational modalities adopted by the end 
user, e.g. expected output of a machine and 
maintenance frequency, which affect costs of 
product utilisation to a high extent
Cost of production factors or resources 
consumed by or interacting with the product, 
e.g. cost of energy consumed by a machine and 
cost of machine operators and supervisors 
70
Descriptive Study I
Descriptive Study I
==PAGE:41==
TCO considers both captial expenditures 
(CapEx) and operating expenditures (OpEx) 
in its calculations. The CapEx concerns 
mainly the acquisition cost, which includes 
expenditures such as product purchase, 
taxes, financing costs, and depreciation 
cost. The OpEx, on the other hand, is 
more related to the ownership costs, such 
as downtime costs, risk costs, cycle time 
costs, maintenance costs, operating costs, 
training costs, and post-ownership costs 
such as disposal costs, environmental 
costs, warranty costs, product liability 
costs. Thus, the CapEx is considered to 
be the more visible part but contributing 
the least to the total cost, constituting the 
“tip of the iceberg”, whereas the OpEx 
involves more hidden costs, as illustrated 
in Figure 27 (Sarancic, D., 2021). The TCO 
is calculated using Equation III.
Figure 27 TCO divided into CapEx and OpEx, where the CapEx usually constitute the “tip of the iceberg”
 (Sarancic, D., 2021)

Economic investment analysis
An economic investment analysis can be 
a good method to verify if the investment 
of a PSS is attractive or not, and guide 
decision making in the context of 
investment scenarios (Sarancic, D., 2021). 
Economic investment analysis includes 
the six following steps:
 
1.	 Define project general premises, 
e.g. capital cost or discount rate (r), 
period for analysis, depreciation 
period
2.	 Define premises associated to 
revenue, e.g. offering price, sales 
forecast
3.	 Identify costs, e.g. raw material 
cost, wages, transportation, etc, and 
investments, e.g. infrastructure and 
development
4.	 Calculate revenue, cost and 
investment flows
5.	 Calculate the cash flows
6.	 Analyse 
indicators, 
cash 
flow 
behaviour, and perform sensitivity 
analysis
From the economic investment analysis 
it 
is 
possible 
to 
identify 
cashflow, 
accumulated cashflow, payback period for 
the investment, Net Present Value (NPV), 
and Internal Rate of Return (IRR), which 
are all indicators on profitability of the 
investment. The indicators are presented 
in Box 13.

Environmental and economic key 
performance indicators
In the area of circular economy there is 
a clear relation between environmental 
impact and cost. For example, a more 
energy efficient machine will reduce 
energy 
consumption, 
reducing 
both 
cost spent on energy and environmental 
impact. Another example is that reuse of 
a product will avoid impact in raw material 
production, sourcing, and manufacturing, 
as well as cost, e.g. total material cost, cost 
of production and manufacturing, cost 
of transportation during manufacturing, 
and packaging materials from suppliers 
(Kravchenko, M., Pigosso, D. C., & 
McAloone, T. C., 2019). Thus, it might make 
sense for companies to combine these two 
parameters when evaluating a potential 
procurement of PSS.  Kravchenko, M., 
Pigosso, D. C., & McAloone, T. C. (2019) 
have made a sustainability screening tool 
that includes a database with 306 different 
Key Performance Indicators (KPIs) to 
measure environmental, economic, and 
social sustainability. The tool can identify 
appropriate KPIs for the user, based on 
criteria and important elements relevant
for the applicable context or situation, and
proposes how to measure them (CIRCit 
Norden: Circular economy sustainability 
screening., 2019). Examples of KPIs are 
shown in Table 9.
Table 9 Examples of KPIs and their distribution according to environmental and economic aspects, extracted from the sustainability screening tool created by Kravchenko, M., 
Pigosso, D. C., & McAloone, T. C. (2019)

Risks and uncertainties
Risks and uncertainties can be dealt 
with through a variety of qualitative and 
quantitative models such as complex 
probabilistic 
models 
and 
artificial 
intelligence (Kambanou, M. L., & Lindahl, 
M., 2016). However, in the context of 
selecting a PSS for procurement, the tool 
that has been identified to be relevant 
is of the qualitative variant, where the 
decisions would be based on the level 
of risk and the ability to mitigate and 
manage the risk.
Failure Mode and Effect Analysis (FMEA)
Failure Mode and Effect Analysis (FMEA) 
is a tool that is seen to be relevant for 
determining the level of risk in connection 
with procuring a PSS, and the ability to 
handle this risk. FMEA is used to define, 
identify, and eliminate known or potential 
problems, errors from the system, design, 
process, and service (Ben-Daya, M. et al., 
2009). The following activities are intended 
to be performed as part of the FMEA:
Box 14 Factors in which each failure mode 
and effect are rated (Ben-Daya, M. et al., 
2009)
Severity of the consequence of the failure 
Occurrence is the probability of the failure 
occurring
Detection is the probability of the failure being 
detected before the impact of the effect is 
realised 
1.	 Identify 
and 
recognise 
potential 
failures including their causes and 
effects
2.	 Evaluate 
and 
prioritise 
identified 
failure modes
3.	 Identify and suggest actions that can 
eliminate or reduce the chance of 
potential failures from occurring. 
Identifying known and potential failure 
modes is an important task in FMEA, which 
can be done by using data and knowledge 
of the PSS. Each potential failure mode 
and effect is rated in each of the following 
three factors: Severity, occurrence and 
detection. These are described in Box 14. 
The factors are multiplied, as described in 
Equation XI, constituting the risk priority 
number (RPN), which reflects the priority 
of the failure modes. These FMEA tasks 
are summarised in Figure 29.
Risk Priority Number = Severity x Occurrence x Detection 	
	
	
	
	
	
	
(XI)
The method was originally developed and 
used by the US military (MIL-STD-1629., 
1980). Nowadays, in the context of PSS, 
FMEA is utilised from the provider’s point 
of view when designing, developing and 
delivering a PSS (Ben-Daya, M. et al. 
2009). Despite this, the method has been 
evaluated to be applicable for procuring 
PSS as well, in decision making situations 
and as a preventive action to the 
implementation of PSS business models. 
Whereas the methods for evaluating 
environmental impact and cost savings 
provide 
binary 
decision 
possibilities, 
the output of the FMEA requires more 
qualitative interpretation to make a 
decision regarding procurement of PSS.
Figure 29 Important FMEA tasks (Ben-Daya, 
M. et al., 2009)

Discussing the application of PSS in  
B2B contexts
In relation to RQ 1, there is evidence in 
literature and industry for successful 
procurement of PSS, as seen for the cases 
of PSS applied in both office environments 
and more industrial purposes. The success 
of PSS has also been confirmed by eight 
out of nine procurement specialists and 
category managers through empirical 
studies conducted in a pharmaceutical 
company as part of the research of this 
study. The application of PSS has proved 
to be a flexible solution for several 
customers, often due to the removal 
of ownership, added services from the 
provider, and better upgradability, hence 
easier access to the newest technology. 
In the perspective of a manufacturing 
company, this flexibility could be useful in 
some cases  and unnecessary in others. 
For instance, in areas such as R&D, where 
there is a need for changing equipment 
frequently, as different type of testing and 
research is done regularly, the flexibility 
that PSS provide would be valuable. 
Procuring use-oriented PSS, where the 
equipment would not be owned, the 
provider could change or substitute
equipment regularly according to the 
scientists’ wishes. Use-oriented PSS would 
give the scientists access to state-of-the 
art technology, allowing them to test 
different types of equipment and evaluate 
the most optimal to use for various 
projects or experiments. Furthermore, 
in situations where there is an urgency 
for more capacity, needs can be fulfilled 
outside premises through e.g. outsourcing 
or purchasing of a functional result. For 
areas with traditional production and 
manufacturing, on the other hand, the 
need for flexibility might be lower. For 
instance, an injection moulder might 
be considered a classic equipment in 
a manufacturing company for plastic 
devices, and is needed in operation during 
many years. This piece of equipment 
would often be difficult to share with other 
users through product sharing or pooling, 
as the device would be set-up, calibrated 
and validated for specific production 
purposes. However, product durability, 
longevity, and few breakdowns could be 
significant product capabilities in this 
situation. Thus, a product-oriented PSS 
would perhaps be the best PSS alternative, 
if additional services and maintenance
Discussion of DS-I
from the provider could contribute to these 
product capabilities. In R&D, however, it 
might be more acceptable and beneficial 
to be more radical by testing use-oriented 
or result-oriented PSS solutions.
The mindset in procurement might still be 
set on product acquisition due to old habits 
originating from an owning culture. For 
example, in big companies with large and 
steady cashflows it is natural that buyers 
do not see the need for investigating other 
alternatives to fulfil their requirements 
than to buy and own, if money for large 
investments is not an issue. This owning 
mindset also makes sense, as most 
elements, infrastructures and systems 
within procurement are structured in a 
product-oriented way. For instance, the 
business spend management platform 
that is used for sourcing equipment in 
the case company, presents the supplier 
offers as physical products or assets. Most 
purchasing platforms are built like this. 
It could however be interesting to test 
out change in behaviour and potential 
change in mindset for both suppliers and 
customers if the offers were presented as, 
e.g. functions. If this were the case, the
customer would source the function 
that is required and the supplier would 
provide this function through different 
means. This would rather be a “need-
fulfilment-system” that accommodates 
final customer needs instead of focusing on 
physical products only fulfilling the needs. 
It can be expected that buyers would like 
the value proposition of access to newest 
technology.  
Limitations of the empirical study
The empirical study that was conducted 
to map out existing PSS offerings 
or application in B2B contexts gave 
interesting insights regarding the type of 
PSS offered or applied and whether the 
participants considered the procurement 
of PSS as successful or not.  Nevertheless, 
the study could have been strengthened 
by including more participants, also from 
different companies and industries. Due 
to the limited number of participants, the 
results are not fit for statistical purposes. 
However, this does not rule out the 
opportunity for using the quantitative and 
qualitative answers descriptively. 
The first question of the survey used 
in the study concerned the type of PSS 
offered to the participants. However, 
including a preliminary question asking 
the participants whether they actually 
had been offered a PSS or not, could add 
more value to the results of the study. 
This would also include a higher number 
of participants in the study, assuming 
that the participants that were unable to 
answer the first question, regarding the 
type of PSS offered, did not submit the 
survey. 
A lot of useful and qualitative comments 
were provided about why they considered 
the PSS as successful. As a follow-up, it 
would have been interesting to list up 
common or expected benefits from PSS 
application, and let them select those that 
they experienced in their implementation. 
This action would most probably help them 
in recalling accomplished benefits that 
they were unaware of. A pie chart could 
then illustrate the experienced benefits, 
e.g. how many experienced product 
longevity, 
elimination 
of 
hazardous 
materials, waste reduction, increased 
quality, use optimisation, reduced TCO.
Descriptive Study I
Descriptive Study I
Discussing the mechanisms that  
influence the environmental 
potential of PSS
To explore SRQ 2.2, several mechanisms 
influencing the environmental potential of 
PSS have been identified through studies 
conducted by Koide, R., Murakami, S., & 
Nansai, K. (2022) and Tukker, A. (2004). 
Although their papers were published 
with a time difference of 18 years, a 
significant part of their findings match. 
However, as the study by Koide, R., 
Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. (2022) is more 
recent, the validity of the results might be 
stronger, e.g. development in technology 
might change the performance of some 
PSS. Both highlight the increase of impact 
improvement potentials moving towards 
the right side of the PSS scale. However, 
Koide, R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. 
(2022) add the perspective of risk of 
backfire effects linked to each CE strategy. 
As a result of their study, most result-
oriented and several use oriented PSS 
were found to provoke higher backfire 
rates. Thus, considering the trade-offs 
connected to the combination of high 
impact improvement and high backfire 
risk, it could be argued that some lower-
79
80
==PAGE:46==
risk 
strategies 
with 
moderate 
improvement potentials, e.g. repairing, 
upgrading, 
modularity, 
refurbish, 
remanufacturing and pooling, should be 
more extensively utilised. Nevertheless, 
in addition to prioritising lower risk 
CE 
strategies, 
incorporating 
higher 
risk strategies with high improvement 
potentials might also be necessary in 
order to achieve a significant improvement 
of environmental impact. This should 
however be evaluated based on the ability 
to avoid or control backfiring or rebound 
effects in each given situation. Thus, 
the most frequently occurring reasons 
for potential backfire effects that Koide, 
R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. (2022) 
identified, such as transport, number of 
uses, and product substitution should be 
taken into account when assessing how 
well backfiring or rebound effects might 
be managed. It may also be possible 
that rebound effects might be controlled 
by incorporating multiple CE strategies 
simultaneously, e.g. combine design for 
durability with leasing. As a result, the 
use-intensity might not lead to frequently 
damaged products. 
It is important to keep in mind that 
Koide, R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. 
(2022) uses global warming potential to 
measure the environmental improvement 
and the backfire effects. From a holistic 
environmental impact perspective, the 
results might look different. For instance, 
if including more impact categories, 
e.g. resource depletion, human toxicity 
or 
terrestrial 
eutrophication, 
the 
improvement and backfire rates would 
probably change. Tukker, A. (2004) does not 
provide information on what parameters 
or impact categories are included in his 
calculations of impact reductions. The 
improvement and backfire rates are also 
dependent on which type of products 
Koide, R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. 
(2022) have based the calculation on. 
For instance, there might be a significant 
difference for energy consuming and 
non-energy 
consuming 
products. 
Looking at the high backfiring effect for 
durability, energy intensive products of 
large appliances might pull this median 
up. If these products break and cannot 
be fixed, they are disposed of despite of 
the significant amount of energy and 
resources going into manufacturing the 
evaluating 
the 
environmental 
performance of PSS. Nevertheless, this 
method might also be considered time 
consuming in a procurement context as 
there are many sub-steps within each 
of the six steps. Conducting an LCS for 
each procurement decision would also 
be too exhaustive. Additionally, according 
to what is understood by McAloone, T., 
& Bey, N. (2008), LCS does not account 
for rebound effects. An MFA would, on 
the other hand, require less, as it only 
focuses on the environmental issue of 
resource consumption. However, such 
a narrow scope is contradictory to what 
Pagoropoulos, A. (2017) says regarding 
environmental and economic assessments 
for evaluation purposes. He stresses that 
improvements need to constitute a true 
change without the occurrence of burden 
shift in the lifecycle, which might be a 
risk when excluding that many impact 
categories in an MFA. 
Considering the guidelines, methods, and 
tools for evaluating the economic potential 
of a PSS, LCC faces, as LCA, methodological 
challenges (Pagoropoulos, A., 2017), and 
might be too comprehensive and time 
products. 
Therefore, 
the 
backfiring 
effect might be different for non-energy 
consuming products such as books and 
clothes. Although Koide, R., Murakami, 
S., & Nansai, K. (2022) present the risk 
of backfiring for different CE strategies, 
and potential reasons for it, they do not 
provide suggestions on how to mitigate 
these risks, which would be valuable 
knowledge when procuring PSS.             
Which guidelines, methods, and tools 
are suitable for environmental and 
economic evaluation of PSS?
As 
part 
of 
investigating 
SRQ 
2.3, 
numerous 
guidelines, 
methods, 
and 
tools for supporting the selection of PSS 
in terms of environmental improvement 
and cost reduction have been identified. 
First, looking at the tools for assessing 
environmental impact, LCA is proven to 
be a state-of-the-art method, covering 
the entire lifecycle and a wide range of 
environmental issues (Hauschild, M. Z., 
Rosenbaum, R. K., & Olsen, S. I., 2018). 
However, in the context of procuring 
PSS, it might not be the perfect fit. Due 
to the comprehensiveness of an LCA, 
it might take up to one to two years to 
complete an entire study. This time frame 
would, however, not match a situation 
in procurement where a decision or 
contract should be made within days or 
weeks. Therefore, it is worth investigating 
environmental assessment methods that 
are less comprehensive and faster to 
conduct. The six-step approach presented 
by Kjaer, L. L., Pigosso, D. C. A., & McAloone, 
T. C. (2017) however, is less comprehensive 
than LCA, and is especially developed for 
Descriptive Study I
Descriptive Study I
consuming for procurement purposes. 
TCO and economic investment analyses, 
however, are relatively quick to perform, 
and 
provide 
useful 
indications 
of 
cost 
improvements 
and 
investment 
attractiveness. Selecting and measuring 
relevant KPIs, as proposed by Kravchenko, 
M., Pigosso, D. C., & McAloone, T. C. 
(2019), might be a competitive method 
for evaluating both the environmental 
and economic performance of the PSS 
simultaneously, as it allows for prioritising 
the elements to be measured depending 
on each situation, which might vary from 
time to time. 
While solid tools, such as LCA and LCC, 
are time consuming, tools that are 
considered 
less 
comprehensive 
and 
time efficient, such as LCS, MFA and KPI 
measuring, might not cover all impact 
categories, potential rebound effects, or 
burden shifting. Since a valid assessment 
is important when dealing with such large 
investments as those in procurement of 
e.g. laboratory equipment, this trade-off 
needs to be evaluated in each situation. 
Literature does not mention how much 
environmental impact and cost should 
81
82
==PAGE:47==
be reduced in order to consider the 
implementation of PSS as successful. This 
might mean that success criteria and KPI 
prioritisations should be evaluated in 
each case. 
As a result of investigating this research 
question, it is obvious that there are 
limited guidelines, methods, and tools 
accommodating quick and valid decision 
making, which would be beneficial in 
situations where the selection of PSS is 
based on environmental impact and cost. 
This thesis identifies this as an important 
research gap and opportunity.
Exploring RQ 1, the success of different 
types of PSS offerings were found in 
procurement for both office and industrial 
purposes, e.g. for lighting and printing, and 
heat regulation and movement of water. 
Furthermore, through empirical studies 
conducted in a pharmaceutical company 
as part of this research, findings show 
that all types of PSS, that are presented 
by Tukker, A. (2004), are procured but 
in different degrees. The most frequent 
applications were product-related services 
and advice, leasing and outsourcing. Nine 
out of ten participants that were offered 
a PSS procured the PSS, and seven out 
of nine considered the procurement 
as a success due to factors such as 
reduced responsibility, higher equipment 
reliability, 
increased 
operational 
effectiveness, added services and support 
from supplier, access to state-of-the art 
technology through upgradability, and 
reduced cost and environmental impact. 
Literature also mentions advantages such 
as stronger B2B relationships, optimised 
use of resources, increased focus on 
core activities, and increased cost control 
and predictability. The advantages of 
procuring PSS, which were identified in

literature and empirical studies, are

that did not consider the implementation

was too expensive compared to a 
conventional pure product purchase. 
Based on this, and the fact that the 
environmental performance sometimes 
is worse than the reference scenario, as 
argued by Kjaer, L. L. et al. (2019), it is 
possible to conclude that the procurement 
of PSS does not make sense in some cases.
Through a mapping of the decision 
process in procurement as part of 
extended interviews with stakeholders, 
different motives for investigating PSS in 
procurement were identified. Most of the 
motives were related to either time frame, 
budget, and the need for access to state-of-
the-art technologies without necessarily 
having the infrastructure or capacity to 
own 
the 
equipment. 
Simultaneously, 
reasons preventing the investigation of 
PSS were collected from the studies, which 
were mostly connected to dealing with 
very specialised equipment, where only 
few suppliers  are available, the product-
oriented design of the current 
procurement processes, and the fact that 
PSS is an unfamiliar territory for several 
stakeholders. 
A list of factors to consider when procuring 
PSS was identified in literature when 
investigating SRQ 2.1. Cost is a factor to 
consider, due to e.g. the expected change 
in the economic structures, as described 
by Wise, R., & Baumgartner, P. (1999). 
According to Kjaer, L. L., Pigosso, D. C. A., 
& McAloone, T. C. (2017), environmental 
impact and related risks of induced impacts 
and rebound effects should also be kept in 
mind. Due to the lack of legal framework 
and contractual complexities connected 
to the procurement of PSS, legal regulation 
and supplier relationship have also been 
identified as important factors (Jacobson, 
H., Carlson, A., & Lindahl, M., 2021). As a 
consequence, there would presumably 
be more comprehensive paperwork and 
difficulties connected to gaining overview 
of the terms and conditions. The increased 
interdependency 
and 
communication 
between supplier and customer also 
requires 
higher 
levels 
of 
supplier 
capabilities and customer confidence and 
trust. Other identified factors were

factors or mechanisms that influence 
the environmental potential of the PSS, 
which were explored as part of SRQ 2.2. 
In general, CE strategies that are included 
in result-oriented PSS have the ability 
to create high environmental impact 
improvements. However, according to 
Koide, R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. (2022), 
these PSS are associated with high risk of 
backfire effects. In addition, CE strategies 
such as reuse, pooling, renting, and 
repairing, upgrading and modularity have 
demonstrated to improve environmental 
performance but are connected to different 
degrees of potential backfire effects. The 
three most frequent reasons for backfire 
effects identified were transport, number 
of uses, and product substitution. However, 
the authors do not provide suggestions 
on how to mitigate the backfire effects or

it can be concluded that there is a lack of 
methods, guidelines, and tools to quickly,

Problem statement
On basis of the identified problems in 
procurement as part of this study, a 
problem statement is formulated:
In the Research Clarification it has been 
justified that a PSS has the potential to 
solve the identified issues in the current 
state and push towards an optimised 
future state, as depicted in Figure 30. 
However, as observed in literature and 
studies within the case company, there 
are some gaps between those two 
states that might prevent integration 
of PSS in procurement, which should be 
filled to reach the future state. Figure 
30 substantiates the difference that 
separates the existing from the desired 
state.
Procurement has an important 
responsibility 
when 
it 
comes 
to 
reducing 
environmental 
impact because of its significant 
contribution to companies’ total 
footprint. Purchased products are 
disposed of despite a short life time 
in several cases. Disposal is due to 
several reasons such as outdated 
technology, 
breakdowns, 
high 
operational cost, and lack of system 
compatibility. As a result, new 
equipment has to be purchased 
to 
substitute 
these 
products, 
which causes a high resource 
consumption and high cost. Thus, 
the way products are procured 
needs to be changed, higher 
requirements to the suppliers are 
necessary, and more sustainable 
customer-supplier 
relationships 
must be established.

As identified through SRQ 2.3, there is a 
lack of tools to quickly evaluate the PSS 
in terms of environmental performance 
for procurement purposes. Therefore, 
the support should enable this. The case 
company has decided that procurement 
should invest 10% of the total time spent 
for each investment on environmental 
evaluation. Since the total active time 
spent for each investment is estimated to 
be 10 hours in average (Appendix I), the 
support should (3) enable an evaluation of 
the environmental performance of the PSS, 
not spending more than one hour actively.

CE strategies within PSS are linked to 
different risks of rebound effects. Although 
this creates awareness, it does not provide 
much guidance to the selection process 
of procuring PSS. Therefore, the support 
could (4) link recommended mechanisms or 
CE strategies to the proposed PSS together 
with associated risks of rebound effects 
and possibilities for mitigation. This would 
possibly increase the attentiveness and 
transparency around the sourced PSS.

the buyer to identify the PSS with highest 
sustainability performance, through tools 
and methods that evaluate economic and 
environmental performance, is the goal 
of the thesis, and has highest priority.

as a supplement to give more depth to 
the environmental evaluation. Thus, this 
requirement is considered a wish to the 
support, rather than demand, which will

Prescriptive Study
In this section a support is developed to solve the gaps that were identified 
in the Descriptive Study I. First, the development process of the support is 
described, followed by a presentation of the support, and how it should be 
used. In the end, an evaluation plan of the support is outlined. The chapter 
is divided into the following sections:

As seen in Figure 31, the development of 
the support was done in five iterations in 
total. Four of the iterations were made in 
the Prescriptive Study, whereas the last 
was made in the Descriptive Study II as part 
of the final evaluation of the support. The 
purpose of performing multiple iterations 
was to develop and test the support at 
different levels of detail. The first iteration 
consisted of developing and testing the 
support at concept level, the second and 
third iteration at prototype level, and the 
fourth and fifth iteration at a more detailed 
level. The testing of the prototypes in the 
early stages of the design process made 
it easy to make changes to the support 
based on the feedback from stakeholders, 
due to their low level of detail. The goal 
and target group of the support is defined 
based on the findings from the Descriptive 
Study I, and are shown in Box 17. 
First iteration: 
Defining the support content
In the Descriptive Study I, the current 
procurement 
process 
was 
mapped, 
including its associated problems and 
gaps. The fact that the decision process 
is heavily impacted by organisational 
cultures and traditional mindsets makes 
the issues even more complex, as it might 
be difficult to influence these rigid aspects. 
However, it is important that these are 
addressed when developing the support. 
Yip, M. H., Phaal, R., & Probert, D. R. (2015) 
claim that  systemic  thinking  needs  to 
be  in  place  to  design    solutions    that 
should    consider    and    incorporate 
human      factors,      economic,      social 
and      environmental  implications,  and 
advances  of  technologies. Thus, the 
swim lane diagrams (Figure 14 and Figure 
15) that were developed in the Descriptive 
Study I to illustrate the procurement 
process, were reconsidered to regain 
a systematic overview (Appendix J.1 
and Appendix J.2). These were used as 
boundary objects between designer and 
main stakeholders in procurement to gain 
a common understanding of the decision 
process and agree on the current gaps,
