The purpose of the need definition is to 
support the buyer in defining a need, i.e. 
required function/output. This action is 
supposed to eliminate the product oriented 
thinking, and rather promote a function 
based approach to sourcing. It will also 
enable the identification of a larger range 
of procurement model alternatives, and 
avoid locking the purchase to a specific 
product and supplier relationship before 
having investigated other promising or 
even better options. 
The purpose of  the procurement model 
guidance is to invite the user into new 
dimensions 
of 
procurement 
models 
by introducing different types of PSS. 
The intention is to ensure that the user 
investigates 
different 
procurement 
models and establishes awareness around 
the numerous alternatives for supplier 
relationships 
before 
making 
a 
final 
contract with supplier, to avoid that more 
suitable solutions are being overlooked.

The support should respond to these 
breakdowns in order to reach the goal of 
enabling an identification of the supplier 
offering 
with

with the main stakeholders it was decided

Figure 34 Content of the support in the context of the decision process in the procurement process
Second iteration: Prototype 1
The second iteration involved developing 
the support from a concept level to a more 
concrete prototype level. The goal of this 
iteration was to test and present a minimal 
viable product of the three support 
elements (need definition, procurement 
model guidance and offer evaluation) to the 
target group of the support. The feedback 
from the testing was used to develop a 
second prototype in the next iteration.  
Need definition 
As described in the First iteration, the 
purpose of the need definition is to 
support the buyer with explicitly defining 
and quantifying the required output, need 
or function of what they are sourcing. This 
is to ensure openness around how 
this output, need or function should 
be fulfilled, and avoid that supplier 
agreements 
are 
made 
based 
on 
assumptions, tradition or other experience 
or expertise based bias. As described in Box 
10 in the Descriptive Study I, in the field of 
environmental assessments the functional 
unit is a method for defining a required 
function to enable a fair and quantitative 
comparison 
of 
alternative 
ways 
of 
achieving the desired function. Thus, the 
first prototype of the need definition was 
highly inspired by such a functional unit. 
Hauschild, M. Z., Rosenbaum, R. K., & 
Olsen, S. I. (2018) express the importance 
of defining the functional unit correctly as 
it significantly influences the results and 
interpretation in comparative studies. 
This is because the functional unit serves
as a reference point for deciding which 
unit processes to include within the 
system boundaries. Therefore, it is crucial 
to ensure that the functional unit fully 
captures the relevant functional aspects 
of the studied systems. In this process, the 
buyer can ask the questions “what?”, “how 
much?”, “for how long/how many times?”, 
“where” and “how well?” to help defining 
a correct functional unit. For example, the 
functional unit to enable a comparison of 
outdoor paint can be built as illustrated in 
Figure 35. The aforementioned questions 
were set up in an Excel sheet where the 
users could insert their answers, and thus 
support the definition of their functional 
unit (presented in Appendix K.1). 
Figure 35 Example of how to build a functional unit
Prescriptive Study
Prescriptive Study
99
100
==PAGE:56==
Procurement model guidance
The 
intention 
of 
the 
procurement 
model guidance was to ensure that the 
buyer investigates alternative supplier 
relationships before making a decision. 
The buyers could spend a lot of time 
investigating 
different 
procurement 
models 
and 
supplier 
relationships. 
However, the limited time available during 
the procurement process has been a 
frequently expressed matter in empirical 
studies of this research. Hence, it was 
investigated how procurement model 
awareness could be enabled in a quick 
manner with reduced effort. As a result 
of discussions and considerations with 
stakeholders, it became clear that a tool 
suggesting a suitable direction for type of 
supplier relationship based on the specific 
conditions that are true for a specific 
need and situation, could be helpful. The 
buyer would only have to provide relevant 
conditions as input, and they would know 
which procurement models to consider 
asking the supplier about. A flowchart 
(Figure 36) was made as the first prototype 
based on the various considerations 
that must be taken in the procurement 
process, which were collected through 
Figure 36 Prototype 1: Flowchart matching a procurement model with investment conditions (Appendix K.1) 
the extended interviews with equipment 
responsibles and other stakeholders from 
procurement, and is presented in Figure 
36 and Appendix K.2.  
Based on the answer to the different 
conditions 
of 
the 
investment, 
the 
user is guided through the flowchart 
either 
towards 
a 
product-oriented 
recommendation, where the technology 
is owned by the company, or towards a 
use or result-oriented direction, where 
the ownership of the technology remains 
with supplier. Based on the patterns of 
behaviour in the different situations and 
conditions, it was possible to generate 
an overview representing archetypical 
examples 
of 
different 
procurement 
situations, constituting a main model that 
different procurement situations could 
merge into, as depicted in Figure 37. 
Dependent on whether the technology 
is needed for ongoing basis or for a 
limited time frame, e.g. in a project, 
whether the technology is fast changing 
or not, and whether there is a budget, 
capacity, and infrastructure for acquiring 
the 
technology, 
four 
archetypes 
of 
procurement situations were made. The
primary reason for building the archetypes 
on these specific characteristics was 
due to the fact that these were the main 
game changers for most participants, 
from the empirical study presented in 
the Descriptive Study I, when considering 
whether to buy the products or letting 
the ownership of the products stay with 
supplier. Secondly, all archetypes are built 
to decrease resource consumption, both 
economically and materially.  For instance, 
Archetype A.1 represents a situation 
where a non-fast changing technology is 
needed at an ongoing basis, and there is 
a budget, capacity, and infrastructure for 
acquisition. If the users find themselves 
in this situation, they should consider a 
supplier relationship where ownership 
is transferred to customer. However, in 
situations where there is limited budget 
and infrastructure for acquiring the 
technology, as represented by Archetype 
A.2 and A.3, access to the technology 
could be ensured through a supplier 
relationship where the ownership remains 
with supplier. Similarly, if the technology 
is fast-changing, which asserts itself in 
Archetype A.4, it might be beneficial to let 
the ownership stay with supplier, as there 
would be a high probability for improved 
versions of the technology emerging at 
the market. This would avoid disposal of 
short-lived equipment for replacement of 
newer technology, and hence decrease 
resource consumption. In addition, a 
service including upgrade when needed 
could be a part of the supplier offering. 
Similarly, if the technology is needed 
within a limited timeframe, e.g. for a 
project, and cannot be used outside the 
project, the buyer should also consider 
a procurement model that prevents 
acquisition of the technology but provides 
access to it for the needed time period, 
again to decrease resource consumption. 
This is reflected in Archetype B.1.  
Offer evaluation
The offer evaluation should be divided 
into a part that can be used to evaluate 
the economic performance and a part that 
can be used to evaluate the environmental 
performance of different offers, to reflect 
the goal of the support. 
One way to evaluate the business case of 
a supplier offering, is to compare against 
a reference case. Thus, a benchmark of 
Prescriptive Study
Prescriptive Study
101
102
==PAGE:57==
Figure 37 Archetypical procurement situations 
minimum two offers could be used to 
evaluate the economic performance of 
an offer. However, to allow the user the 
possibility to evaluate several offers, the 
prototype of the economic evaluator was 
made to enable a benchmark of three 
different offers. The prototype was not 
built to accommodate the evaluation of 
more than three offers, due to increased 
complexity for the buyer in the decision 
process. Comparing up to three offerings 
would still enable the buyer to e.g. 
benchmark one PSS from one supplier, 
the same PSS from another supplier 
against buying a traditional pure product. 
The economic evaluation was first built as 
a tool in Excel to benchmark TCO of three 
different offers, based on input data from 
the user (see Appendix K.3 and Appendix 
K.4), as TCO proved to be a suitable 
method to rapidly compare the economic 
conditions of different PSS in procurement 
settings, according to findings in the 
Descriptive Study I. 
The prototype of the environmental 
evaluator was also built in Excel. Due to 
the comprehensive character of most 
methods to evaluate environmental
impact from a lifecycle perspective, 
as discovered through SRQ 2.3 in the 
Descriptive Study I, it was important to 
develop a tool that would allow a faster, 
yet valid evaluation. As elaborated in the 
Descriptive Study I, measuring various 
KPIs could be an approach to conduct 
the evaluation as it could cover several 
impact categories while at the same 
time focus on the highest impacts. This 
would provide a more nuanced picture 
of the environmental performance of the 
offering, compared to the current way of 
measuring, which mostly involve ecolabels 
for the supplier and the equipment. 
However, the numbers of KPIs to measure 
should be limited to avoid demotivating 
the user. The indicators should also be 
relevant to the specific situation. Therefore, 
the first prototype of the environmental 
evaluation tool involved a KPI selection 
activity to filter KPIs based on relevance, 
which is presented in Appendix K.5. The 
intention was that the users should tick 
off the boxes in a check list if it applied to 
their supplier offerings. As a result, the 
relevant environmental parameters and 
their suggested KPIs would be highlighted 
in a KPI database, as presented in 
Appendix K.6. The KPI database used was 
retrieved from the sustainability screening 
tool developed by Mariia Kravchenko, in 
collaboration with CIRCit Norden and the 
Technical University of Denmark. Each 
KPI had a detailed description of what 
the KPI contains, description of purpose 
and significance of indicator value, how 
the indicator can be measured, and the 
required unit of measure. The relevant 
KPIs could be calculated for different offers 
directly in an Excel sheet when input data 
would be available. Inspired by the MECO 
board described in the Descriptive Study 
I, an additional Excel sheet was provided 
to the users to support the identification 
of the highest impact in the lifecycle of all 
assessed offerings, which could be used for 
weighting the highlighted KPIs (Appendix 
K.7). All phases of the lifecycle should be 
allocated a weight by the users from one 
to three based on the anticipated impact 
in their specific situation. Subsequently, 
each supplier offering should be scored 
from one to five (with five indicating the 
highest and one the lowest impact) with 
regards to material, energy, chemical, 
and water consumption. As a result, the 
weighted scores for each offering with 
Prescriptive Study
Prescriptive Study
103
104
==PAGE:58==
regards to each lifecycle stage could be 
calculated and visualised, together with 
the impact distribution for each offering 
for the selected impact categories. 
Third iteration: Prototype 2
The third iteration involved developing an 
improved prototype based on feedback 
after testing the first prototype. The goal 
of this iteration was to test and present 
an updated and improved minimal viable 
product of the support to the target group. 
The feedback from the testing was used 
for developing the support at a detailed 
level. 
Need definition
During the testing of the first prototype, 
the need definition was evaluated as being 
functional. However, feedback informed 
that the activity might seem too abstract 
for most users and might be difficult to 
execute. In order for it to be applicable 
in practise, providing an example could 
inspire and work as a guidance for the 
user.  Thus, an example was included in 
the second iteration of the prototype, as 
depicted in Appendix L.1.  In addition, 
common types of mistakes when defining 
105
a functional unit, as explained in Box 20, 
were also expressed in the tool to avoid 
focus on physical products but rather put 
the attention on the function.   
Procurement model guidance
Both feedback on usefulness and usability 
was provided during the presentation of 
the procurement model guidance, as seen in 
Appendix L.2. Regarding the usefulness of 
the tool, several stakeholders expressed 
that 
they 
were 
missing 
a 
detailed 
description, explanation, visualisation and 
concrete examples of each procurement 
model. In addition, it was mentioned by 
multiple stakeholders that an overview 
of all procurement model types could be 
beneficial. On the subject of usability, some 
stakeholders found it requiring too much 
effort to read and follow the flowchart. 
Hence, the format of the guidance should 
be reconsidered. Whether the questions 
and considerations were understandable 
to the user should also be evaluated, and 
reformulations should be considered.   
As the flowchart format was found 
complex for the user to interpret, the 
second prototype of the procurement
model 
was 
developed 
with 
the 
requirement 
of 
enabling 
quick 
PSS 
suggestions with minimal effort from the 
user’s side. Thus, the flowchart was used 
to build a decision model that provides 
the user with suggestions for suitable 
procurement models based on a few 
conditions that are defined by the user. 
Building the model in an Excel format was 
evaluated as a good fit for the prototype 
due to the possibility of quick setup, and 
the flexibility to change after new rounds 
of testing. The flowchart was still provided 
as an additional appendix in the tool 
to give the user the possibility to gain a 
more detailed overview when making the 
decision. The prototype of the decision 
model is presented in Appendix L.2. 
Reckoning several stakeholders’ need to 
have additional details on the contents of 
the different procurement models, a one-
pager (Appendix L.3) was made to visualise 
each PSS (as presented by Tukker, A. 2004) 
with concrete examples that are relevant 
for procurement, considerations to have 
in mind for each specific PSS, and some 
key pros and cons. The one-pager made 
also gives the buyer an overview of when 
to consider letting ownership stay with
Box 20 Common types of mistakes when defining a functional unit (Hauschild, M. Z., Rosenbaum, R. K., & Olsen, S. I. (2018). Life Cycle Assessment. 
Springer.)
1.	 Assuming that the same physical quantity of product equals the same function:
Example: «1 kg of packaging material»
Explanation: A physical quantity, e.g. mass, is not a function. The mass required to provide a packaging function often depends on type of material. Therefore, focus on 
                         the packaging function rather than the physical material.
Suggestion: «Contain and protect 24 glass bottles of 0,75 L for 6 hours in Norway, and enable lifting by a human being»  
2.	 Being too restrictive:
Example: «Enable watching of television with a 30 W power consumption for 10,000 h»
Explanation: Here, only television with that exact power consumption can be included in the study, althoug a fixed power consumption is usually not relevant to the user 
                         of a television. Therefore, ensure that the functional unit only covers what relates to the function of the product, i.e. to watch television. 
Suggestion: «Enable watching of television 5 hours a day for 10 years in Spain, with a minimum of 8K-resolution and 3D sound»
3.	 Incorrect use of technical standards or legal requirements:
Example: «Driving 1000 average person-kilometres in a diesel passenger car that fulfils the Euro 6 standard and, therefore, emitting less than 0,08 g Nox per kilometre 
                   (Euro 6 standard) during use»
Explanation: A passenger car complying with the Euro 6 standard may emit more NOx than 0,08 g/km, depending on the driving pattern, climate, etc. A misinterpretation 
                        of  a technical standard in the functional unit can therefore lead to unfair comparisons. Therefore, rather focus on the conditions of use. 
Suggestion: «Transporting four persons 1000 average person-kilometres per week for 12 years in Denmark, emitting less than 0,08 g NOx per kilometre during use»
Prescriptive Study
Prescriptive Study
106
==PAGE:59==
supplier, and when transfer of ownership 
to customer should be considered. 
Offer evaluation
The prototype of the economic evaluation 
tool was evaluated as being functional 
after presented to stakeholders within 
the target group. However, with economic 
savings playing an important role in 
procurement, it would, according to 
stakeholders, be beneficial to know the 
savings obtained from procuring the 
different supplier offerings, compared to 
the reference (Appendix L.4). In addition, 
an economic investment analysis could 
provide a more solid justification of 
the offer selection. Thus, a financial 
analysis benchmark was included in the 
economic evaluation tool, which presents 
the 
cashflow, 
accumulated 
cashflow, 
calculates the NPV, IRR and payback period 
for the compared offerings (Appendix L.5). 
These indicators were included because 
of their ability to illustrate the profitability 
of the investment, as described in the 
Descriptive Study I in the section of 
Economic investment analysis.  
The stakeholders that were presented the 
prototype of the environmental evaluation 
tool expressed that there were too many 
questions to consider when filtering 
relevant KPIs (Appendix K.5). Furthermore, 
the impact evaluation was found being 
time consuming to conduct (Appendix K.7), 
as it required allocating a score for each 
supplier offering considering four impact 
categories and five lifecycle stages. Some 
stakeholders were also sceptical to the 
result mainly being based on subjective 
anticipations and assumptions. With basis 
in this feedback, an updated prototype of 
the evaluation tool was developed.  First, 
the filtering method was redesigned with 
the goal of enabling a more rapid filtering. 
The KPI database retrieved from the 
sustainability screening tool contains 274 
environmental KPIs. Thus, delimiting this 
database was evaluated as an efficient 
approach to jumpstart the filtering process 
before the user interacts with the tool. As 
KPIs are highly connected to strategy, the 
included KPIs should reflect the business 
objectives of a specific area or company. 
Thus, the KPIs that got included in the 
database were selected according to the 
environmental procurement strategy
of the case company, which is depicted 
in Box 21. 14 KPIs were included which, 
in collaboration with stakeholders, was 
evaluated as being a manageable number 
to the user. This number should, however, 
not be exceeded. Filtering these 14 KPIs 
based on high impact would also give the 
user a lower number of KPIs to measure.

of the KPI filtering are illustrated in Figure

the relevant KPIs would be divided into 
supplier KPIs and company KPIs. The data 
from supplier should be collected already 
at tender stage, as part of the request for

spreadsheet as part of the evaluation tool 
by the suppliers and the buyer, it

Fourth iteration: 
The final support 
The fourth iteration involved developing 
the support at a detailed level and 
integrating the proposed changes from 
the testing sessions. The goal was to 
have a presentable version of the support 
that could be used for final testing and 
thorough evaluation in the Descriptive 
Study II. 
The feedback from the testing of the 
second prototype was mostly related 
to the procurement model guidance and 
the environmental evaluation tool. With 
regards 
to 
the 
procurement 
model 
guidance, some stakeholders expressed 
that a check list would be easier to handle 
than the drop-down list in the decision 
model (Appendix L.2). The set-up of the 
updated decision model is presented in 
Appendix M.1 and Appendix M.2 to enable 
replicability. It was also mentioned that it 
was difficult to understand the upper part 
of the one-pager (Appendix L.3) and was 
proposed replaced with something that is 
easier and more quickly understandable. 
Additionally, the key factors to consider 
when procuring PSS, that were collected 
through SRQ 2.1 in the Descriptive Study 
I, were incorporated to the one-pager, 
allowing more awareness of the various 
procurement models. 
Furthermore, in agreement with the 
stakeholders it was concluded that all KPIs 
should be calculated by supplier, and not 
the company. This was decided because 
all knowledge around consumption in 
relation to the technology is possessed by 
the supplier only. It would not be possible 
for the company to retrieve data for most 
of the KPIs as most of the information 
related to an offering would be unknown 
for the company. Thus, it was decided that 
the tool should not contain any further 
filtering of the KPIs, as this would result in 
different KPI sets for each supplier, which 
prevent a fair benchmark. The feedback 
listed in this paragraph was the most 
significant response from the previous 
rounds of testing, which was considered 
when developing the final details of the 
support.
In the fourth iteration, it was also 
evaluated how risks of rebound effects or 
other failure modes could be linked to
110
the suggested PSS, and how suggestions 
on how to mitigate or prevent these from 
happing could be a part of the support. 
However, after discussed with stakeholders 
it was decided that procurement currently 
is not mature enough to evaluate the 
environmental 
performance 
of 
the 
offerings at such a profound level. Only 
measuring the environmental KPIs will 
even be a big step in itself put next to 
the existing approaches to evaluating 
environmental performance. Thus, adding 
considerations of rebound effects might 
complicate the decision process at current 
stage. 
Nevertheless, 
since 
rebound 
effects have been evaluated as important 
factors to consider when procuring PSS, 
it is necessary to reassess how this can 
become a part of the support further into 
the future when procurement is more 
mature and accustomed to evaluating 
environmental impact.

Prescriptive Study
109
==PAGE:61==
As a result of the iterative development 
process 
described 
in 
the 
previous 
sections, the last version of the support 
was developed and is presented in the 
following section. 
A tool for identifying the 
supplier offering with 
highest sustainability 
performance 
The support is designed as a decision 
support tool that assists procurement 
with identifying the supplier offering 
with highest sustainability performance 
in terms of economic and environmental 
performance. It is important to emphasise 
that the tool will not identify any “correct” 
or “incorrect” choice of offer but give 
an indication of the business case of 
the offer with regard to economic and 
environmental behaviour. Thus, the buyer 
is free to make an independent decision 
but is encouraged to be guided by the 
results from the tool. In other words, the 
tool will not make the decision for the 
buyer but works as a support for making 
the most sustainable choice. 
The tool is built in Excel, which is a format 
that can handle the multiple functions that 
the tool is built upon. It comprises several 
worksheets consisting of five activities that 
the user should complete in sequence. All 
activities include a description of purpose 
and instructions on how to complete it. The 
activities are depicted in Figure 39, and put 
in relation to how they solve the identified 
breakdowns in the procurement process 
(Box 18). The first part of the tool provides 
an introduction for the user, that gives an 
overview of the content of all activities. In 
addition to that, the purpose and impact 
of the tool is thoroughly explained to give 
the user a drive for utilising the tool for 
decision making. Thus, ensuring that the 
users understand the expected impact of 
the tool might encourage them to adapt 
it to their own processes, and let go of 
current ways of behaving, e.g. product 
oriented thinking.
The first activity is to define need, that will 
support the buyers with explicitly defining 
the need and required function of what 
is being sourced. The second activity is 
intended to help the buyers identify a 
suitable direction of supplier relationship 
and is conducted in two steps. In the 
first step, the buyers will use a decision 
model that provides a suggestion for 
a suitable procurement model based 
on the specific investment context. As 
part of the second step, the buyers are 
presented a procurement model guidance 
to learn what the suggested procurement 
model contains and get examples and 
considerations for this specific model. 
Based on the suggested procurement 
model, the buyers should make a decision 
on which model to go for, as part of 
the third activity, and ask for supplier 
offerings that matches the chosen model. 
After receiving the offerings from the 
suppliers, the buyers should in the fourth 
activity insert TCO data for the offerings 
that are selected for the comparison. 
The tool enables two or three offerings 
to be benchmarked, with one of the 
offerings being the reference point in the 
comparison. In the fifth and last activity 
the environmental KPI data from the 
suppliers should be inserted. Here, the 
buyers have the possibility to prioritise 
KPIs by assigning a weight to each KPI 
based on their perceived impacts. After 
completing the five activities, no more 
The support
111
Figure 39 The activities of the tool for identifying the supplier offering with highest sustainability performance, and how the activities address the breakdowns 
112
actions are required from the buyer’s 
side. Based on the input from the buyer, 
results will be generated presenting 
benchmarks of TCO, financial analysis, 
and environmental KPIs. These results 
are available to be used for evaluating the 
economic and environmental viability 
of the compared offerings. In addition, 
a final scorecard, presented as part of 
the tool, provides an overview of the 
evaluation of the offerings, summarising 
the results of the TCO, financial analysis, 
and environmental KPI benchmark. The 
overview can be used to guide the buyer’s 
Prescriptive Study
Prescriptive Study
decision of supplier offering.    
==PAGE:62==
Activity 1 - Define need
The purpose of the first activity is to 
explicitly define the need and functionality 
that the supplier offering must fulfil. An 
explicit need definition will encourage 
the buyers to be more open to possible 
solutions and offerings that can satisfy 
the specific need. This will also create a 
shift in mindset, where the way to source 
will be concentrated around buying 
a needed function rather than buying 
specific physical products. As seen in the 
literature review and Descriptive Study 
I, this mentality is crucial for decoupling 
resource 
consumption 
from 
value 
creation. 
To initiate the definition of the need, the 
user is encouraged to list obligatory and 
optional properties of the functionality. The 
obligatory properties should reflect what 
features the required function should 
carry in order to fulfil the need. Legally 
required features could be included there. 
Examples of obligatory properties are 
“cleaning and sterilisation of pipette tips”, 
“eliminate viruses, mycoplasma, bacteria, 
fungi and DNA” and “capacity to provide 
50 cleaned and sterilised pipette tips at 
once”. The optional properties, should 
express other possible features of the 
function that can make it more attractive, 
e.g. “quick process (less than two hours)”, 
“easy handling of medical waste”, and “easy 
handling of the cleaned and sterilised 
pipette tips”. The next part of the activity 
involves answering the questions of 
“what?”, “how much?”, “how many times?”,
“for how long?”, “where?”, and “how well?”, 
which will help the user to define and 
understand the need. As seen in Figure 
40, the user is instructed to answer these 
questions by filling the empty fields. The 
user is provided with an example on how 
to do so, as well as an example of how not 
perform this activity.
Activity 2 - Select procurement model
The purpose of the second activity is to 
identify a relevant procurement model 
based on the defined need and the context 
of the need. 
Step 1- Use decision model
In the first step, the buyer is instructed to 
use the decision model by answering either 
yes or no to the questions asked, as seen 
in Figure 41. The answers will reflect the 
contextual circumstances and conditions 
connected to the need. Therefore, based 
on the answers, the decision model 
will provide an output suggesting a 
procurement model matching the buyer’s 
specific situation. Here, it is essential 
to keep in mind that the suggested 
procurement model might not be the 
best choice in terms of economic and 
environmental impact. The decision model 
simply suggests a direction for supplier 
relationship based on the context of the 
specific need. How the offering performs 
economically and environmentally can be 
evaluated through Activity 4 (insert TCO 
data) and Activity 5 (insert environmental 
KPIs), which generate results revealing 
benchmarks of TCO, financial analysis, 
and environmental KPIs.

Activity 1
Define need
Purpose: 
Quantify the need that the supplier offerings must fulfil. Having defined the need will facilitate a competetive sourcing, where alternative ways of procuring a function are investigated before making a final decision. The need should always be the basis for what you procure. 
Instructions: 1. Add obligatory properties (must-have feautures to fulfil the needed function, and might also include legally required features) and optional properties (nice-to-have features that the function could posess). Look at the example for inspiration
2. Define need. Use the obligatory and optional properties to fill in the empty fields, answering the questions. Look at the example on how to do it as inspiration.
3. Include the list of obligatory and optional properties and the defined need in the request for tender. 
Note:
Avoid thinking about physical products, and rather think about the functionality you want to obtain.  Take a look at the examples on how to and how NOT to do it as inspiration.

Step 2- Use procurement model guidance
In 
the 
second 
step, 
the 
buyer 
is 
encouraged to use a one-pager that 
provides a procurement model guidance 
to support understanding the results of 
the decision model, as presented in Figure 
42 and Appendix M.5. This will provide the 
buyer with additional details about the 
suggested procurement model, including 
description of the type of supplier 
relationship, familiar examples in which 
the model is applied, considerations when 
choosing that specific model, together 
with pros and cons. The guidance will also 
give the user an opportunity to consider 
other procurement models that they 
might find relevant to investigate further. 
The intention of the guidance itself is to 
create more awareness around sourcing a 
needed function and show how a function 
or access to a technology can be obtained 
several ways. The one-pager can also 
be used separately from the tool, e.g. 
in situations where it is useful toget an 
overview of types of procurement models 
to consider in specific contexts. It can also 
be used in procurement to inspire and 
encourage other ways of obtaining access 
to technology. After having completed 
Activity 2, the buyer would be qualified to 
select a suitable procurement model that 
matches the need.

Prescriptive Study
Prescriptive Study
==PAGE:64==
Figure 42 Activity 2 - Select procurement model (step 2 - Use procurement model guidance) (Appendix M.5)
117
Activity 3 - Ask for supplier offerings 
The purpose of the third activity is to 
outline a minimum of two supplier 
offerings for the comparison of economic 
and 
environmental 
performance. 
To 
initiate the activity the buyer is instructed 
to ask suppliers for available offerings that 
match the selected procurement model 
that was identified in Activity 2. This is not 
an activity that takes part of the tool itself 
but is necessary to complete, as the next 
activities of the tool (Activity 4 and Activity 
5) are dependent on data related to the 
supplier offerings. Thus, to proceed with 
the tool, the buyer must have received the 
offers from the suppliers. In the request 
for tender (RFT), a list consisting of 11 
environmental KPIs should be included, 
in which the suppliers are asked to fill 
out (Figure 43). The KPIs are extracted 
from the sustainability screening tool 
described in the section presenting the 
Second iteration. The KPIs included in 
the list measure material, energy and 
water 
consumption 
and 
reflect 
the 
environmental procurement strategy of 
the case company directly. The included 
indicators also ensure a cradle-to-cradle 
coverage, where the supplier is asked
to provide numbers to consumption 
within production, manufacturing, use 
and operation, and EoL of the technology. 
To ensure a fair comparability, the supplier 
is asked to provide data for the functional 
unit, which is automatically generated and 
placed above the list together with the 
obligatory and optional properties of the 
PSS, based on the user’s input in Activity 1 
(presented in Appendix M.6). The supplier 
is asked to fill in data for all KPIs to ensure 
transparency of the offering and to avoid 
greenwashing.   
Activity 4 - Insert TCO data
The purpose of the fourth activity is 
to collect all data of costs connected 
to the ownership of all the compared 
supplier offerings, to enable an economic 
benchmark. Thus, as seen in Figure 44, the 
buyer is informed to insert data of relevant 
cost and income for all the offers, for all 
the years that the technology is needed, 
which is defined as part of the functional 
unit in Activity 1. As a result, the TCO is 
automatically calculated and visualised. If 
all required data is available, the activity is 
expected to be completed within minutes.

==PAGE:66==
Activity 5 - Insert environmental KPI 
data
The purpose of the fifth, and last, activity 
is to collect the measured environmental 
KPIs provided by the suppliers, in order to 
benchmark the offers on environmental 
performance. As seen in Figure 45, the 
user is first encouraged to apply weighting 
to the indicators, which should reflect 
anticipated impact of the indicators in the 
specific case. The weighting scale goes 
from one to three, with one indicating 
lowest weight. In order to evaluate the 
impact of the indicator, the buyer could 
consider, e.g. the energy intensity of the 
technology in use, whether the energy 
used is sourced from non-renewable 
sources, or whether use/operation of 
the technology takes place in a water 
stressed area. If the latter were not true, 
hazardous 
waste 
generation 
during 
use or after EoL, for instance, could be 
prioritised above water consumption. 
Making considerations like these, could 
help the user with allocating appropriate 
weighting to the indicators. Next, the user 
is instructed to insert the KPI data into the 
sheet. To ensure fair comparability, a KPI 
should be excluded from the list if a
supplier has failed to provide data for 
one or more offers. If all required data 
is available, the activity is expected to be 
completed within minutes.

Results - Sustainability benchmark
As a result of the five performed 
activities, the buyer will be provided with 
a sustainability benchmark as a result, 
consisting of a benchmark of TCO, financial 
analysis, and environmental impact, which 
are presented in Appendix M.9, Appendix 
M.10 and Appendix M.11, respectively. 
The environmental benchmark shows 
which offers perform best within each 
KPI. In addition, the tool performs an 
internal 
normalisation 
of 
each 
KPI, 
allowing comparison of the offers across 
KPIs. Thus, a total score representing the 
environmental impact for each offer is 
also presented. The benchmark within 
each KPI is visualised in a spider web 
diagram, while the benchmark across KPIs 
is visualised in a bar chart. All benchmarks 
are summarised in a final scorecard, as 
depicted in Figure 46, which the buyer 
can use for guidance and justification of 
choice in supplier offering.

Explanation: Evaluate which offer to select based on the economic and environmental scores

Prescriptive Study
Prescriptive Study
==PAGE:68==
The support has been designed with the 
intention of assisting procurement with 
selecting a PSS that can satisfy the needs 
and match the context of the investment. 
However, whether it is able to fulfil this 
goal is not yet known. Therefore, in the 
next phase of the project, an evaluation 
of the support is conducted. This is first 
done in relation to a new investment 
wheel within procurement of laboratory 
equipment for R&D within the case 
company. Hereby, the support is used 
and evaluated for a real company case 
within procurement. Because suppliers 
are users of the support in completion 
of certain actions, and due to their 
contribution being crucial for generating 
a sustainability benchmark, the suppliers 
are also considered key stakeholders in the 
evaluation of the support. The support is 
evaluated with regard to two parameters: 
usefulness and usability. In other words, 
the users are to provide feedback for how 
well the tool works and how easy it is to 
use, in addition to suggestion for changes 
and areas of improvement. Based on this, 
with a retrospective view, it is evaluated 
whether the support solves the identified 
problem, and whether it fulfils the defined 
Outlined evaluation plan 
125
goals and requirements.  
Due to the limited time of the project, a 
timeline for the evaluation process was 
made, as depicted in Figure 47. To simplify 
the process, the evaluation was divided 
into two phases: The pre-quotation and 
the post-quotation phase. In the pre-
quotation phase the participants in 
the evaluation are asked to first select 
an investment in which the tool would 
be applied. The investment should be 
selected based on the following two 
criteria: 
budget 
(preferably 
above 
1 
million DKK) and readiness for application. 
With a budget for investments above 1 
million DKK, there is a higher possibility 
for higher supplier engagement and 
participation for investigating alternative 
procurement models. With regards to 
the limited time frame, the investment 
should also be selected  based on how 
ready it is for application in relation to the 
tool. For instance, if the user requirement 
specification (URS) is already received 
from Line of Business, the investment is 
mature enough for the buyer to initiate 
the first activity of the tool, i.e. define need. 
If not, the buyer would have to request a
URS from Line of Business, which would 
involve waiting time. Thus, upon receiving 
the URS, the buyer can perform Activity 1, 
and define need, which is not expected to 
take more than 15 minutes. Subsequently, 
the buyer is ready to select a procurement 
model as part of Activity 2, which is also not 
expected to take more than 15 minutes. 
Then, Activity 3 was planned as a meeting 
with suppliers where suppliers are asked 
whether they can provide an offering 
meeting the defined need through the 
suggested procurement model retrieved 
from Activity 2. For the sake of benchmark, 
the suppliers are also asked for a regular 
pure product quotation. In addition, the 
environmental KPI list is presented to 
suppliers and are given one week to fill it 
out.
As soon as the quotations from the 
suppliers are received, as well as data 
for the environmental KPIs, the post-
quotation phase is initiated, where the 
buyer can validate the supplier offerings 
based on economic and environmental 
performance. First, the buyer spends 
about 20 minutes to do Activity 4, where 
TCO data is inserted. Afterwards, the
buyer uses 20 minutes to insert the 
environmental KPI data, and 10 minutes 
on interpreting the results from the 
benchmark. In total, the evaluation of 
the tool is expected to take two to three 
weeks.
Figure 47 Timeline for the evaluation process 
To collect the feedback from  the 
evaluation, the buyers are asked to 
answer a survey with questions in relation 
to usefulness and usability of the tool.
Prescriptive Study
Prescriptive Study
126
==PAGE:69==
The developed support works as a decision 
support tool that assists the buyer with 
identifying a procurement model that 
matches the investment context and 
evaluating sustainability performance of 
the related supplier offerings. The tool 
has been evaluated to be valuable and 
necessary within procurement due to 
several gaps in the current procurement 
process identified in Descriptive Study I. 
First, it has been noted that the existing 
product oriented mindset might prevent 
the buyer from thoroughly investigating 
alternative offers that would satisfy the 
need, and preferably in a more sustainable 
way. The tool can help changing their 
mindsets by encouraging the buyer to 
focus on the need rather than the physical 
product. It can also help ensuring that 
available offerings matching the defined 
need 
are 
investigated 
through 
the 
procurement model guidance. Another 
important gap identified in Descriptive 
Study I was the lack of tools for evaluating 
environmental impact of different supplier 
offerings in a quick and thorough way. 
The support, provides a tool to make this 
assessment possible through a simple KPI 
benchmark setup. Nevertheless, although
the intention of the support is to quickly 
identify the supplier offering with highest 
sustainability 
performance, 
there 
is 
a possibility this might not happen in 
reality. The buyer should go through 
several activities as part of the tool before 
achieving the results, which might be 
more time consuming than expected. In 
addition, there are a couple of transactions 
between buyer and supplier that must 
be successful for the tool to work. Thus, 
considering 
the 
smaller 
investments 
where the time dedicated might be limited 
from both buyer’s and supplier’s side, it 
can be questioned whether the efforts 
required for using the tool are too many. 
This also leads to another discussion 
point; whether the support should be used 
for all types of investments, or if there 
are investments where such a decision 
support is irrelevant. This is assessed as 
part of the evaluation in the Descriptive 
Study II.
The appropriate number of KPIs
The environmental evaluation of the 
offerings are related to uncertainties and 
risks. The evaluation is built upon a KPI 
setup that is customised to the case 
Discussion of PS
company to ensure that it reflects the 
company strategy and related business 
objectives. Comparing the approach of 
the tool with other conventional methods 
for evaluating environmental impact, 
e.g. LCA, which might take more than a 
year to perform, inserting supplier data 
for 11 KPIs is a quicker way to measure 
environmental 
impact. 
Inserting 
the 
KPI data is expected to be done within 
minutes, and the benchmark results 
are 
automatically 
generated 
within 
seconds. However, it can be questioned 
whether this method can be qualified as 
a complete environmental evaluation and 
whether 11 indicators are sufficient. The 
selection of impact coverage within the 
KPI setup is based on the environmental 
prioritisations that the company make 
through the procurement strategy. The 
results of the impact evaluation are, 
therefore, only based on indicators 
connected to the strategy. These are only 
related to the impact categories material, 
energy and water consumption, which can 
be translated to resource and water use 
at midpoint level in the International Life 
Cycle Data System (ILCD) (Hauschild, M. 
Z., Rosenbaum, R. K., & Olsen, S. I., 2018).
Inclusion of more KPIs that cover more 
impact categories would probably increase 
validity of the results of the benchmark as 
it could potentially uncover other negative 
impacts that have not been considered 
prior by the company. Including more 
KPIs to the list could also alter the results 
of the benchmark. However, adding 
more KPIs would require more effort and 
time spent for both supplier and buyer. 
From a supplier’s perspective, it can 
already be too demanding to handle and 
measure 11 KPIs, particularly for smaller 
size investments. Additionally, the more 
data the supplier is asked for, the risk 
of receiving more incomplete and poor 
quality data is likely to be higher. Thus, 
there is a trade-off between accuracy or 
validity of measurement and the effort 
put into the use of the tool, hence it might 
be necessary to find a breakeven point as 
part of the evaluation.   
Risk of false results
As depicted in Figure 48, there are several 
elements of the support that, if not 
completed correctly, would lead to false 
results. In such a case, the supplier offering 
presented as the most sustainable
choice might not be the best offering in 
reality. As a consequence, the buyer might 
make the wrong decision. One of the main 
weak links are found in the transition 
between Activity 3 and Activity 4 and 5. For 
instance, there is a risk that the suppliers 
provide uncertain, false, or poor quality 
data. Moreover, the suppliers might 
only provide data for KPIs for which they 
perform well, or suppliers might provide 
data for different functional units. This 
would directly influence the validity of the 
economic and environmental benchmark, 
which would lead to wrong interpretation 
and decision making. There is also a risk 
that suppliers are not able to provide 
the requested data at all, which would 
prevent the buyer from proceeding with 
the evaluation of the supplier offering. 
Thus, the success of the tool depends 
significantly on the supplier contribution. 
However, it is likely that most suppliers 
which initially lack data, with some effort, 
would be able to get hold of that data if 
the wish for remaining a supplier for a 
customer is strong enough.  The buyer 
could also contact other suppliers that are 
able to provide such data, and that can 
deliver a solution that can fulfil the
buyer’s need.
There are a few things to consider 
concerning the environmental benchmark, 
before making a decision. As mentioned, 
the support is intended to help the buyer 
identifying the supplier offering with 
the highest sustainability potential. This 
means that compared to each other in a 
benchmark, the buyer would be able to 
detect the most sustainable offering of 
the ones included, in terms of economic 
and environmental performance. Thus, 
the most sustainable offering would be 
most sustainable relatively to the others. 
However, it cannot be excluded that 
the most sustainable offering actually 
has a high environmental impact from 
an 
absolute 
sustainability 
point 
of 
view. Measuring absolute sustainability 
however, is a complex process, that would 
require defining absolute environmental 
sustainability reference points for each 
offer in relation to specific global targets 
(Hauschild, M. Z., Rosenbaum, R. K., 
& Olsen, S. I., 2018). Nonetheless, it 
can be argued that evaluating relative 
sustainability would be most relevant and 
sufficient in this case because one
Prescriptive Study
Prescriptive Study
127
128
==PAGE:70==
Figure 48 Unfortunate events that might happen during the application of the support
supplier offering must be selected under 
all circumstances, which should be the 
alternative with highest sustainability 
performance. In addition, it is necessary 
to be critical about how the environmental 
impact 
scores 
are 
created 
in 
the 
benchmark. Expressing the different KPI 
scores on a common scale through 
normalisation 
enables 
measuring 
environmental impact across KPIs, and 
makes a benchmark of the different offers 
possible. However, it is essential to keep 
in mind that the results depend on how 
the different KPIs are weighted, which is 
purely based on subjective perceptions. 
Weighting differently could alter the 
results of the benchmark. Furthermore, 
potential rebound effects could lead to 
induced impact for the different offerings, 
which would also modify the benchmark 
outcome.
Environment vs. economy 
The buyer might find themselves in a 
position where the results show one offer 
with highest economic performance, 
and another with highest environmental 
performance, which might challenge 
the decision making. From a nested 
sustainability point of view, the most 
sustainable solution would be the offer 
scoring highest on the environment. 
However, this may not be a sufficient 
justification 
from 
a 
procurement 
perspective due to e.g. budget limitations. 
Thus, a good starting point could be to 
look at the difference in environmental 
performance across the offerings. If only 
small differences are separating them, 
they can justify selection of an offer 
performing higher in regards to economy 
as there is only slight difference in the 
environmental performance. Regarding 
the most environmentally sound solution, 
the buyer could be advised to evaluate 
whether its financial prospects live up 
to the budget limitations by looking at 
the NPV, IRR and payback period, and 
make a decision based on that. Selecting 
an offer based on either economic or 
environmental performance is also a  
matter of prioritisation, which would vary 
from case to case.
Prescriptive Study
Prescriptive Study
129
130
==PAGE:71==
In this chapter, the development of a 
support, intended to solve the gaps and 
breakdowns that were identified in the 
Research Clarification and the Descriptive 
Study I, was described. The support was 
developed with the goal of enabling an 
identification of the supplier offering 
with highest sustainability performance 
in terms of economic and environmental 
performance. This resulted in a decision 
making 
tool 
composed 
of 
several 
elements that together lead to the 
identification of such an offer. As part 
of the development process, it was 
uncovered that there might occur events 
during the use of the tool that might lead 
to inaccurate results. An examples of such 
an event is supplier providing uncertain 
or poor quality data or not being able to 
provide data, which creates complications 
for the environmental evaluation and 
affects the validity of the final benchmark. 
Although it may be difficult for the buyer 
to prevent these events from happening, 
it is important to keep those risks in mind 
when interpreting the results and taking 
a decision. 
Conclusion of PS

Descriptive Study II
In this chapter the developed support is evaluated following the outlined 
evaluation plan from the Prescriptive Study. First, the support is applied for 
two investments within procurement of laboratory equipment, for the user 
to evaluate the usefulness and usability of the tool. Based on the feedback 
and suggestions for improvement from the users, the support is evaluated 
and changes are implemented. As the final step, the final version of the 
support is tested and evaluated. The chapter is divided into the following 
sections:
Applying the developed support
	
Investment A: Nurse calling system (NCS)
	
Investment B: Microplate reading system
Evaluation of the support
	
Suggestions for improvement 
Changes to the support

DS-I
PS

Box 22 The investments in which the 
support is used and evaluated
Investment A Nurse calling system (NCS)
Investment B Microplate reading system
Applying the developed support
The support was used and evaluated 
individually 
by 
two 
stakeholders 
in 
procurement in the context of the 
quarterly investment wheel in the case 
company. These two stakeholders were 
selected because they were the two out 
of three responsibles for the procurement 
of all the investments in this investment 
wheel, 
hence 
making 
them 
highly 
representative 
within 
procurement. 
The 
investment 
wheel 
contains 
33 
investments of laboratory equipment 
and technologies for R&D in total. The 
support was tested by the two selected 
stakeholders for one investment each 
from the wheel. The two investments are 
denoted Investment A and Investment B in 
this thesis, and were selected due to their 
high maturity to initiate the procurement 
process, meaning that the procurement 
responsibles already had received the 
user 
requirement 
specification 
(URS) 
from the equipment responsible. Thus, 
the procurement responsibles knew the 
content of the investment, and they were 
qualified to start with the first activity of 
the support. Other than that, no other 
parameters influenced the selection of 
the investments. Hence, the 
investments 
constitute 
completely 
random 
investments 
of 
laboratory 
equipment. 
This section provides a description of 
how the tool was used for Investment A 
and Investment B, going subsequently 
through each activity of the tool. The 
content of the investments is described 
in Box 22. 
Investment A:
A nurse calling system (NCS)
According to the URS of investment 
A (Appendix N.1), the purpose of the 
investment was to source a nurse calling 
system (NCS) for clinical trials. Participants 
might stay from one hour to several 
weeks in the clinic, and the participants’ 
safety must be closely monitored at 
all times during their stay via a system 
that allows the participant or staff to 
trigger an alarm to call the medical staff. 
This might be when the participant is 
feeling unwell, experiencing a medical 
calamity or requires medical attention in 
another way. Then, the medical staff will 
immediately attend to the participant and 
take appropriate action. The participant
or staff need to be able to trigger an alarm 
at any time, regardless of the participant’s 
location in the clinic. Thus, the coverage 
should extend into bedrooms, recreational 
areas, 
functional 
areas, 
bathrooms, 
showers and toilets. 
Pre-quotation phase
Activity 1 - Define need
With help from the URS, the user listed 
all obligatory properties for the system, 
as depicted in Table 11. No optional 
properties were defined. These were used 
to quantify the functional unit, which is 
highlighted in Box 23.

Activity 2 - Select procurement model
As a result of using the decision model 
in Activity 2 of the tool, the user was 
suggested to go a use-oriented direction, 
where access to the technology could 
be achieved through a product lease 
(Appendix N.3). This was because the 
context 
of 
the 
investment 
involved 
specific conditions that would match 
such a PSS. First, the investment was not 
related to a specific project, meaning 
that the technology is needed for an 
ongoing basis for a long time frame, 
where the technology is used until it 
cannot longer be operated. Secondly, 
the technology might be fast-changing, 
meaning that significant improvements 
of the technology might emerge at the 
market regularly, and frequent upgrade 
or substitution of the technology could 
be necessary. Thus, it could be a good 
idea to get access to the technology 
without buying and being the owner of 
the equipment. The need could, however, 
not be fulfilled as an output delivered by 
a third party as the staff or participants 
need to interact with the technology to 
trigger the alarm notification. Therefore, 
result-oriented PSS got excluded from 
Descriptive Study II
Descriptive Study II

136
==PAGE:74==
the options. Furthermore, there was a need 
for exclusive access to the technology, 
meaning that product pooling would not 
be an option. Since the need for using 
the technology is constantly active (24/7), 
the decision model suggested a product 
lease as the best alternative.  The decision 
model path is presented in Figure 49.  
Figure 49 Decision model flowchart for Investment A
Activity 3 - Ask for supplier offerings
Based on the results and guidance from 
Activity 1 and Activity 2, the user asked 
the suppliers for the offering suggested 
by the decision model. As seen in Box 24, 
the supplier provided three quotations. 
Although the buyer only asked for 
quotations of a pure product purchase and 
a leasing agreement, they also provided 
annual prices for a service lifecycle plan 
(SLP). This service package is included 
in the leasing agreement, but can also 
be attached to a pure product purchase. 
Therefore, it was decided that it also 
could be interesting to compare the pure 
product (reference) against a product with 
product related services.
The first quotation (Reference) was for a 
pure product purchase, where only the 
products of the NCS were included in the 
offering. The second quotation (Offer 
1) also included a pure product but with 
additional lifecycle services included as 
well. These involved 24/7 support hours 
365 days a year, high response time in case 
of incidents, remote support, software 
updates and upgrades, and free shipping 
and return. This service package was also

provided as a lease instead of purchasing 
and owning the products. The leasing 
agreement also gave the customer the 
opportunity to upgrade the hardware of 
the technology during the contract period 
when new features or upgrades occur. 
However, if the customer would wish to 
upgrade the whole system, a discount 
amounting to 50% of the applicable year’s 
rental fee could  be granted as an offset 
against the total price of the new system, 
and the ownership of the equipment 
would pass on to the customer. When 
the customer no longer wants to use 
the technology, the leasing agreement 
terminates, and the supplier takes back 
the equipment. Signing the contract, the 
agreement is valid for three years, and 
cannot be terminated within this period. 
At the end of the term the contract will 
continue from year to year until either 
party decides to end the agreement. An

Activity 4 - Insert TCO data
Upon receiving quotations, the user 
followed the instructions from Activity 4, 
and inserted the TCO data for the three 
quotations, as presented in Appendix 
N.9, Appendix N.10 and Appendix N.11. 
The salvage value of the equipment was 
estimated to be 25%-50% of the sales 
price by End-of-Life, which would give an 
average salvage value of 37,5% of the sales 
price. This was used for calculating the 
depreciation cost, following Equation XIV. 
As depicted in Figure 50, the acquisition of 
the physical product and its depreciation 
constitute the main contributors to the 
total cost of ownership for the Reference 
and Offer 1. The service lifecycle plan 
also constitutes a significant expenditure 
for Offer 1 and Offer 2. For Offer 2, the 
leasing fee is the biggest expense of the 
total cost of ownership.

Activity 5 - Insert environmental KPI data
As instructed in Activity 5, the user 
assigned a weight to the environmental 
indicators that were considered having 
higher impact. The user argued that 
since all energy that is provided to the 
laboratory comes from renewable sources, 
the higher impact would lie on material 
consumption. Therefore, amount of non-
sustainably sourced materials in product 
(KPI M1), product hazardous or non-
recyclable materials in product (KPI M2), 
hazardous or non-recyclable solid waste 
mass generated in production and service 
provision (KPI M3), and hazardous or non-
recyclable waste mass generated during 
operation or use (KPI M6) were considered 
being 2,5 times as impactful as the KPIs 
related to energy consumption. The KPI 
measuring total solid waste mass (KPI 
M5) was also considered being twice as 
important as the energy indicators. With 
minimising water consumption in water 
stressed areas being of very high priority, 
this indicator was considered three times 
as important as the energy indicators.  
The supplier was only able to provide data 
for six out of 11 indicators (Appendix N.4), 
which was delivered together with the 
quotations. Regarding the non-applicable 
data, the supplier did not have an overview 
of the amount of product hazardous or 
non-recyclable materials in the product 
(KPI M2). Neither did they know the 
amount of total energy consumption in 
manufacturing of the product not coming 
from renewable sources (KPI E2) nor the 
specific energy consumption in operation 
of the technology (KPI E1). The total 
amount of water used in production was 
also unknown (KPI W1), as well as the total 
water consumption in areas with medium-
high water stress (KPI W2). However, the 
user followed the instructions of Activity 
5, and inserted the data that was available 
(Appendix N.13). Considering that the 
offerings in the evaluation involved the 
same technology and type of equipment, 
the lack of data for the mentioned KPIs 
would not be severe, as they would not 
influence the results of the benchmark. 
However, if assessing the offerings from 
an absolute sustainability point of view, 
the limited data would lead to a deficient 
evaluation. In the context of absolute 
sustainability, it would be particularly 
critical not to be able to measure the 
indicators with a high weighting.  
Sustainability benchmark
As a result of the user’s input to TCO and 
the environmental KPI data, the support 
tool generated a sustainability benchmark. 
Considering the economic benchmark, 
Figure 50 visualises that the total cost 
of ownership is lowest for the reference 
offering. 
Surprisingly, 
the 
leasing 
agreement only turned out to be 18% as 
more expensive than the pure product 
purchase, which deviates significantly 
from the hypothesis. The reason might 
be the visualisation and accounting of 
the depreciation cost when buying and 
owning the technology, which is often 
neglected by the buyer. Additionally, the 
annual leasing fee decreases year by year, 
as the technology ages, which positively 
influence the total cost of ownership for 
Offer 2. The most expensive solution, on 
the other hand, would be to purchase 
the NCS with the SLP, which has a 34% 
higher total cost of ownership than the 
reference.  The economic indicators from 
the financial analysis also reflect the higher 
costs associated with selecting Offer 1 
and Offer 2, as they give continuous cash

141
142
==PAGE:77==
outflow, contributing to negative NPVs and 
no paybacks of the investments (Appendix 
N.14). Offer 2 provided a rate of 205% when 
the NPV equals zero – a rate that usually 
reflects a very profitable investment. 
However, since a profitable investment 
for Offer 2 is not the case, considering the 
other economic indicators, the produced 
IRR was false. This error may occur in 
situations where positive cashflows are 
followed by negative, i.e. not occurring 
in the “correct order”. Therefore, Buyer 
A disregarded the IRR in the financial 
benchmark of this investment. Looking 
at the results from the environmental 
evaluation (Table 13), the environmental 
impact of the leasing agreement turned 
out to be zero compared to the other 
purchase offerings. This is a result of 
the take-back service from the supplier, 
which ensures that the customer’s waste 
by End-of-Life is eliminated. Furthermore, 
because the supplier claims that the 
offerings have zero impact in all the other 
measured indicators, the total solid waste 
mass is the only KPI that influence the 
result of the benchmark. 
Table 13 Environmental KPI benchmark of Investment A (Appendix N.15) 
Investment B:
Microplate reading system
According to the URS of Investment B 
(Appendix O.1), the purpose was to source 
a system that could read fluorescence, 
luminescence, UV/Vis-absorbance, and 
alpha detection from a microplate. The 
system should also be able to collect the 
read data and store these.
Pre-quotation phase
Activity 1 - Define need
The user intended to start with listing all 
the obligatory and optional properties 
of the system based on the system 
requirements in the URS provided by 
Line of Business. However, a significant 
part of the system requirements were 
product specific. For instance, the first 
requirement specified the power supply 
of the equipment by stating that it should 
operate with AC 220-240V. Usually, a fixed 
power consumption is not relevant to the 
user of the equipment. What is important, 
on the other hand, is that the equipment 
provides the required function. Another 
requirement stated that the equipment 
must have a high energy xenon flash
lamp. Being this restrictive and product 
oriented might limit the possible solutions 
and suppliers. Thus, the user should 
rather ensure that the requirements only 
covers what relates to the function of the 
product by reformulating the obligatory 
and optional properties of the system, and 
defining the functional unit, as depicted 
in Table 14 and Box 26, respectively. For 
example, the requirement regarding the 
flash lamp was redefined as provide high 
energy lighting. The requirement about 
the fixed power supply was deleted. The 
reformulated and deleted requirements 
in the URS are highlighted in Appendix 
O.2 and Appendix O.3.

Activity 2 – Select procurement model
As a result of using the decision model 
in Activity 2 of the support tool, it was 
suggested to the user to go a product-
oriented direction, where access to 
technology could be achieved by buying 
products combined with product-related 
services 
or 
advice 
(Appendix 
O.5). 
According to the decision model, the 
context of the investment involved specific 
conditions that would match such a PSS 
best. Although the investment was related 
to a specific project, the need for the 
technology could also be found outside the 
project. Therefore, the technology could 
be used for other purposes after End-of-
Use in the project. The technology was 
also characterised as stable and classic, 
and not fast changing. Thus, the need for 
upgrade would be low. Additionally, there 
was budget for acquiring the technology 
and infrastructure and capacity were 
available to operate the technology onsite. 
Hence, buying the technology combined 
with product-related services or advice 
was suggested as the best alternative. 
The decision model path for Investment B 
is presented in Figure 51. 
Figure 51 Decision model flowchart for Investment B
Activity 3 – Ask for supplier offerings
Following 
the 
suggestion 
from 
the 
decision model, the user asked the 
supplier for quotations on a pure product 
and a product with a product-related 
service. As seen in Box 27, the supplier 
provided three quotations. The first 
quotation only involved buying a pure 
product with installation qualification (IQ) 
and operation qualification (OQ) included 
in the offering. These services means that 
the supplier is responsible for delivering 
the equipment, ensuring a qualified 
installation, and performing system tests 
so that it is ready to be used. The second 
quotation also involved a pure product but 
with an additional silver service contract 
agreed on a one year service cycle. The 
service contract contained an annual 
on-site performance check, including 
basic cleaning of instrument, preventive 
maintenance service kit where applicable, 
system test, performance testing, and 
labour and travel cost related to on-site 
inspection. 
Additionally, 
the 
contract 
includes two repair visits within the 
contract period, 48 hours response time 
on emergency calls from time of service 
request, phone and email technical

Reference: Pure product with IQ and OQ
Offer 1: Product with product related services 
(silver level)
Offer 2: Product with product related services 
(gold level)
support, and 10% discount on spare parts 
needed for repair. The third quotation 
involved 
the 
same 
annual 
on-site 
performance check as the silver solution, 
same response time and technical support, 
but included also ulimited repair visits and 
spare parts within the contract period. An

The suppliers were also asked to fill in 
the environmental KPI list, where all data 
should be provided for the technology 
fulfilling the described functional unit.

Activity 4 - Insert TCO data
Upon receiving quotations, the user 
followed the instructions for Activity 
4, and inserted TCO data for the three 
quotations, as presented in Appendix O.8, 
Appendix O.9 and Appendix O.10. The 
depreciation cost of the equipment was 
calculated following Equation XIV, and 
using a salvage value of 37% of the sales 
price, as for Investment A. As depicted 
in Figure 52, the physical product and 
its depreciation cost constitute the main 
expenditures in the total cost of ownership 
for all three offerings. The service level 
agreements are also main contributors to 
the total cost of ownership for Offer 1 and 
Offer 2. 
Box 28 Hypothesis for the evaluation of 
the offerings for Investment B
Economic profile:
The TCO for the offering without service contract 
will be lower than the TCO for the offerings with 
service contracts.
Reference < Offer 1 (silver) < Offer 2 (gold)
Environmental profile:
The environmental impact will be higher for 
the offering without service contract than the 
offerings with service contract.
Reference > Offer 1 (silver) > Offer 2 (gold)
Post-quotation phase
In the offer evaluation, Offer 1 and Offer 
2 were evaluated against the reference 
offer to see what the economic and 
environmental outcome would be if 
buying 
the 
equipment 
with 
service 
packages instead of a pure product 
solely. As done for Investment A, the user 
made a hypothesis for the results of the 
offer evaluation before conducting it, 
which is presented in Box 28. Since the 
two service offerings included an annual 
service fee, they were assumed to have 
a higher total cost of ownership than the 
pure product offer (reference offering). 
Considering the environmental profile, 
the service offerings were expected to 
have a lower environmental impact than 
the pure product. This was due to the 
assumption that the service contract 
would involve additional services, such as 
preventive maintenance, cleaning, repair, 
performance testing, all circular economy 
strategies that presumably would extend 
product lifetime, and lead to a reduced 
resource consumption.

Activity 5 - Insert environmental KPI data
As instructed in Activity 5, the user 
allocated a weight to the indicators that 
were considered having higher impact. 
The user had a similar argumentation as 
Buyer A, and considered the indicators 
related to material consumption and 
waste as 2-2,5 times as important as the 
energy indicators. Knowing that neither 
the production of the equipment nor 
operation would take place in a low to 
medium water stressed area, the water 
indicators were not weighted higher. 
The supplier provided data for eight 
out of 11 KPIs in the environmental 
KPI list (Appendix O.6), which was 
delivered together with the quotations. 
The non-applicable data was related to 
consumption during manufacturing of 
the equipment (KPI  M3, M4, W1). Since 
this case concerned an evaluation of 
three offerings with the same equipment 
that were manufactured equally, the 
inclusion of these KPIs was nevertheless 
not considered relevant as it would not 
influence the benchmark. Thus, the KPIs 
that were measured in this environmental 
evaluation were total solid waste mass
and energy consumption in operations. 
According to the supplier, the impacts 
of the other indicators were zero, e.g. all 
materials used in product were recycled 
and 
compliant 
with 
Restrictions 
of 
Hazardous Substances (RoHS) (KPI M1 
and M2), no hazardous or non-recyclable 
solid waste mass was generated during 
operation or use (KPI M6), all energy 
consumption 
in 
manufacturing 
was 
sourced from solar power (KPI E2), and 
the equipment was produced in a low-
medium water stressed area (KPI W2). 
Having the filled out KPI list at hand, the 
user followed the instructions of Activity 
5, and inserted the data (Appendix O.12).
Sustainability benchmark
The 
sustainability 
benchmark 
was 
generated as a result of the user’s input 
to TCO and environmental KPI data. First 
considering the economic benchmark 
(Figure 52), it is seen that the total cost 
of ownership of the two offerings with 
service contracts are 13% and 25% 
higher than the pure product purchase, 
respectively for Offer 1 (silver) and Offer 2 
(gold). Because there would be no savings 
if sourcing Offer 1 or Offer 2 instead of the 
reference offer, the financial outcome 
was as expected in the hypothesis. The 
higher costs when sourcing the PSS are 
reflected in the economic indicators, 
where there are negative NPVs, non-
applicable IRR, and no paybacks because 
of the continuous cash outflow (Appendix 
O.13). Considering the results from the 
environmental evaluation, the total score 
of the environmental impact turned out 
to be the same for all three offerings, as 
depicted in Table 16.
