Evaluation of the support
As mentioned in the outlined evaluation 
plan presented in the Prescriptive Study, 
the support should be evaluated in terms 
of both usefulness and usability. Applying 
the tool for two different investments, as 
seen in the previous section, enabled an 
evaluation of these parameters. 
To start with, the tool was met with 
reluctance 
from 
several 
users 
as 
they feared it would take too much 
additional effort and time when doing 
the investments. They were also worried 
that it might interrupt the flow of their 
work process, and mess up negotiations 
with their suppliers. In general, they 
expressed that time was a scarce resource 
in the investment projects, which made it 
challenging to test new things and make 
changes to current processes. Despite 
of this, the stakeholders prioritised 
the testing of the  tool for the selected 
investments, as presented in the previous 
section. After using the tool, the users 
were asked to fill in a survey with 
questions that would indicate the level of 
usefulness and usability of the support, 
and give suggestions for changes and 
improvements. They were asked
whether the purpose and instructions 
of the activities were clear, whether they 
lacked information, how much time they 
spent on each activity, how easy it was to 
perform the activities, and whether they 
found the output of the activities useful. 
The evaluation results from using the 
support are collected in Appendix P.1, 
Appendix P.2, and Appendix P.3, and are 
presented in this section. 
Activity 1 – Define need
Usability
The users of the tool found it difficult 
to define the need in Activity 1, due to 
lack of knowledge regarding technical 
requirements to the technology. However, 
with help from the equipment responsible, 
the URS, and additional supervision from 
the designer of the tool, the user managed 
to list the obligatory properties and define 
the functional unit. Since input from the 
equipment responsible was necessary, it 
was discussed whether the need should 
be defined by the equipment responsible 
instead, and afterwards conveyed to 
procurement to be used as basis for the 
investment. Letting the equipment 
responsible define the need and quantify 
the function might also help removing 
the product-oriented mindset in Line 
of Business. Since the product-oriented 
direction often is determined at end user 
level in Line of Business, involving the 
equipment responsible could also help 
addressing the root of the problem.
Usefulness
Through the survey, the users expressed 
that they found Activity 1 interesting, 
and thought it made sense to think need 
instead of product. According to feedback, 
Activity 1 enabled a broader scope for the 
investment, which would open up a wider 
range of possibilities on how to fulfil the 
need, which again would increase the 
potential for sourcing more sustainable 
solutions.   
Activity 2 – Select procurement model
Usability
Feedback with regards to using the 
procurement model guidance revealed 
that the users found it easy and quick to 
use. In some cases more information from 
Descriptive Study II
Descriptive Study II
153
154
==PAGE:83==
the equipment responsible was needed 
to be able to answer the questions in the 
decision model, as they would have more 
knowledge regarding the context of the 
investment. However, the users explained 
that through a short phone call with the 
equipment responsibles, they were able 
to answer the questions rapidly, which led 
to a suggested procurement model based 
on those. They also mentioned that they 
found it easy to identify the suggested 
procurement model in the one-pager 
and find additional information about its 
content. 
Usefulness
According to the survey, the users 
described Activity 2 as having high 
usefulness, as it quickly suggests a suitable 
procurement model for the investment. 
They also perceived the procurement 
model guidance as valuable, since it 
would tell them what the suggested 
model involves, and what considerations 
to have in mind when procuring it. 
Additionally, they thought it was useful 
to get an overview of how to establish 
other possible supplier relationships, as 
provided through the one-pager.   
Activity 3 – Ask for supplier offerings
Usability
According to the feedback, thanks to 
the results and guidance in Activity 1 
and Activity 2, it was clear to the users 
what to ask supplier about in Activity 3. 
The suppliers were also able to provide 
quotations matching the requests from 
the users. However, some suppliers did 
not experience the same low level of 
effort when handling the environmental 
KPI list. For instance, the supplier for 
Investment A was only able to provide 
data for six out of 11 environmental 
KPIs. Presumably, the availability of data 
will vary a lot from supplier to supplier 
but in the cases where only a small 
fraction of the data is provided, it would 
be difficult to get a complete overview 
of 
the 
environmental 
performance 
across different impact categories. This 
would influence the usefulness of the 
environmental evaluation.  In addition, 
the users mentioned that the usability 
of this activity could be improved by 
incorporating the KPI list into the business 
spend management platform (Coupa) 
used for sourcing events in the case
company. On this platform the supplier 
could directly insert data to the list and 
submit these together with the quotations. 
This unification would eliminate the need 
for sending the list as a separate file back 
and forth to supplier, and would appear 
more as an integrated part of the sourcing 
event. It would also allow exporting all 
vendors’ data into one single Excel file, 
and rank them accordingly.     
Usefulness
Based on the instructions from Activity 
3, the users succeeded in asking for one 
pure product offer that would satisfy the 
investment need, as well as one offer that 
would match the selected procurement 
model. Both suppliers responded with 
quotations for one pure product offer, 
as well as two alternative offers each, 
that matched the users’ requests. The 
suppliers stated that they were not used 
to being asked for environmental data 
at such a profound level. However, they 
expressed that the setup of the KPI list, its 
descriptions and explanations on how to 
measure the indicators were useful in the 
calculation and provision of environmental 
data for the offerings. It can be argued
that performing Activity 3 will increase 
suppliers’ 
engagement 
for 
collecting 
environmental data from the entire value 
chain of the offering and increasing 
environmental transparency.  
Activity 4 – Insert TCO data
Usability
The 
response 
regarding 
Activity 
4 
expressed that it was clear how to perform 
the activity. This might be because 
calculating TCO is a familiar procedure. 
According to the users, they were provided 
all the necessary data from the supplier to 
be able to perform the activity, and it was 
clear where to insert the data. 
Usefulness
The users thought it was useful that the 
TCO revealed all the hidden costs that 
are embedded within a pure product 
purchase, which usually are not taken into 
account. Buyer A was surprised that these 
costs actually would alter the results of 
the benchmark, and prove his hypothesis 
wrong. 
Activity 5 – Insert environmental KPI 
data 
Usability
Both users required additional personal 
support 
as 
a 
supplement 
to 
the 
instructions before performing Activity 5. 
However, after giving them extra guidance 
they were able to perform the Activity 
independently. According to feedback, it 
was clear to them when, where and how 
to weight the KPIs, and where to insert 
data. 
Usefulness
As observed in the offer evaluation 
of investment B, the score of the 
environmental impact turned out to be 
the same for all three offerings. This can 
most probably be explained by the fact 
that most KPIs measure performance 
related to the product which, in the case 
of Investment B and Investment A, is the 
same for all offerings, since the same 
supplier is used. With the current pool 
of KPIs, the results of the environmental 
evaluation would show more differences 
between the purchase of pure products 
and more use or result-oriented PSS. 
This is usually because supplier ownership 
creates incentives for optimising product 
longevity, and due to services such 
as take-back, which eliminates waste 
for the customer. For result-oriented 
PSS in particular, the suppliers would 
have motivation for reducing resource 
consumption as it often would end up 
at their record.  However, as seen in 
the case of Investment B, the score of 
the environmental impact evaluation 
would probably not vary significantly 
for investments where product-oriented 
PSS are compared in general as the 
circular 
economy 
strategies 
involved 
in the offerings are not reflected in the 
current pool of KPIs. This might lower 
the usefulness of the environmental 
evaluation tool in such decision making 
cases. 
Nevertheless, 
stakeholders 
expressed that when comparing to the 
current situation in procurement where 
almost no environmental considerations 
are made but ecolabel checks, the 
environmental KPIs would still be more 
useful and valuable. The list of KPIs would 
provide more transparency around the 
materials, energy, and water consumption 
connected to the technology, compared
Descriptive Study II
Descriptive Study II
155
156
==PAGE:84==
to the current setup. As a result, 
procurement would have a richer insight 
into what they actually are buying and 
the environmental impact they make by 
buying specific solutions. 
Summary of the evaluation
Overall, the users were able to use the 
support independently, apart from Activity 
1 and Activity 5, where supplementary 
personal support was required. Therefore, 
it might be necessary to provide an 
additional introduction and training to the 
users before they start using the tool. This 
might give the buyers sufficient confidence 
and knowledge to independently use the 
tool for future investments.  Furthermore, 
it is believed that the more they use the 
tool, the easier it will be each time.
Looking overall at the usefulness and 
usability of the support in retrospective, 
the response from the users was positive. 
First of all, according to the feedback, 
Figure 53 Time spent on the activities in the tool
they were surprised about how quick and 
effortless it was to perform the activities. 
As seen in Figure 53, the average time to 
perform each activity was eight and 23 
minutes for the pre-quotation activities, 
and 35 and 15 minutes for the post-
quotation activities. However, the buyers 
had to wait for one to two weeks for the 
quotations and environmental KPI data 
before they could proceed with Activity 
4. Looking at the time spent on the tool 
in total, excluding the non-value adding 
activities, e.g. waiting times, it sums up to 
one hour an 36 minutes. This would be the 
additional time spent on each investment 
when using the tool. However, the entire 
process from defining the need and 
ending with a sustainability benchmark 
157
took from one to two weeks in total. 
The purpose, demand, and usefulness 
of the tool were also acknowledged by 
the users and management. During a 
presentation of the principles within the 
support, the CFO and other managers of 
the case company seemed to recognise 
the way of thinking that the support 
promotes, and could see that it made 
sense to switch from a product-oriented 
mindset to a mentality that focuses on 
fulfilling needs and sourcing a required 
function. Therefore, they also understood 
that it is essential to evaluate how access 
to technology can be achieved in each 
specific investment, which might implicate 
that owning the equipment is not always 
the ideal way. According to the CFO, 
integrating the principles of the support 
to the current procurement process 
will result in the success of R&D and 
Laboratory Sourcing in the case company. 
Suggestions for 
improvement
It is essential that the tool works as a 
support, and that it does not require 
more effort than it is useful. Based on 
the evaluation, it was concluded that 
some elements of the support should 
be redesigned so that it more painlessly 
blends into the current procurement 
system, as well as adjusting to the 
current processes and flow of information 
within it. Additionally, ensuring that the 
environmental KPI list takes the circular 
economy strategies that are embedded 
in the PSS services into account, might 
make the evaluation even more valuable. 
The suggestions for improvement of the 
support are presented and prioritised in 
Box 29.
Ensuring 
that 
the 
environmental 
evaluation 
also 
measures 
circular 
economy strategies was a highly prioritised 
suggestion as it would directly influence 
the usefulness of the support. As a result, 
it would be possible to even distinguish 
the environmental performance between 
product-oriented

PSS that are offered from the  same 
supplier, which would be useful when 
taking 
the 
final 
decision 
for 
such 
investments. 
The next priority was integrating the need 
definition to the URS. This means that as 
part of filling out the URS, the equipment 
responsible will define the obligatory 
and optional properties of the needed 
technology, as well as quantifying the 
function by defining the functional unit. 
As a result of this change, procurement 
will not be responsible for Activity 1. 
Based on the evaluation, doing it this way 
would make more sense, since the end 
user or equipment responsible is the one 
that possesses all knowledge related to 
the need, e.g. the context of the need and 
technical requirements. When the URS is 
filled out, it will contain all the information 
that procurement needs to know regarding 
the needed function of the investment. 
Thus, the new version of the URS would 
work as a boundary object between the 
equipment responsible and procurement 
to communicate requirements about the 
needed function and set the boundaries 
for the investment. However, this
implementation would entail changes 
in 
standard 
operating 
procedures, 
influencing the current processes of several 
stakeholders, hence it would require time 
to put out in action. Meanwhile, the tool 
can be still be used with the current setup, 
where procurement performs Activity 
1 based on input from the equipment 
responsible. Since, the usefulness of 
this part of the support is not directly 
threatened in the current setup, the 
initiative could be implemented later.
The last suggestion for improvement 
was integrating the KPI list to Coupa, 
as described in the “Evaluation of the 
support” section. Because the current 
setup works as it is, this initiative was 
not evaluated as urgent. However, by 
implementing it there is a potential to 
improve the usability for both supplier and 
procurement. Incorporating the KPI list to 
Coupa is not expected to take more than 
a couple of hours as the system already 
contains modules that allow setting up 
such as list. 
As 
a 
result 
of 
implementing 
the 
suggestions 
for 
improvement, 
the 
support would blend more smoothly 
into the current procurement process, as 
illustrated in Figure 54. Thus, completely 
new structures and procedures would 
not be necessary. However, some of the 
current processes would get additional 
elements or tasks that the stakeholders 
would have to perform. For example, the 
equipment responsible would have to 
describe the  obligatory and positioning 
properties of the function, and define the 
functional unit in the URS. The suppliers 
would also have to provide environmental 
KPI data for the offers.  Additionally, 
two new processes are added to the 
procurement process, where the buyer 
is given the opportunity to investigate 
available 
offerings 
that 
match 
the 
investment context and evaluate the 
sustainability performance of those, using 
the support tool.

Changes to the support
As presented previously in Box 29, the 
first initiative was allocated the highest 
priority, due to its potential for improving 
the usefulness of the support. Therefore, 
a fifth iteration of the support was made 
to integrate this initiative. The goal with 
this iteration was to redesign the support 
so that the environmental evaluation also 
reflects the environmental potential from 
the service component of the PSS to a 
higher extent. This would most probably 
enable the buyer to differentiate the 
environmental performance of product-
oriented PSS offerings provided from the 
same supplier, as well as measure the 
impact from providing services in all types 
of PSS in general. 
Initially, different services that might be 
part of different PSS were listed, as well 
as the potential environmental outcome 
from those services, as seen in Table 
17.  Then, KPIs that could reflect those 
outcomes were identified. For example, 
cleaning, preventive maintenance, and 
regular system and performance testing 
could lead to fewer breakdowns of the 
equipment. This could then lead to a lower 
need for the service provider to attend the
equipment at the customer, which could 
result in less transport and less need for 
spare parts. A study from 2017 conducted 
in the software providing company, Limble 
CMMS, found an 11% reduction in year-
on-year maintenance cost when applying 
preventive maintenance (Christiansen, 
B. 2020, April 29). Thus, assuming that 
preventive maintenance would result in 
a reduction of spare part use by 11%, the 
total solid waste mass would decrease. 
Therefore, transport (km) and material 
mass for spare parts (kg) could work as 
indicators to measure the environmental 
potential of these services. Services like 
repair would mean that disposal of the 
equipment could be avoided, which could 
be measured by the average reference 
flow for the functional unit without repair, 
measured in material mass (kg). Closing 
material loops by including take-back 
services could be measured in total solid 
waste mass (kg). Assuming that upgrade 
would prevent disposal of the old product, 
this service could be measured by the 
number of upgraded versions available 
of the technology during product lifetime, 
which multiplied with the product material 
mass would indicate the avoided waste
generation (kg). Other services such 
as quick response time and technical 
support through phone and email, would 
lead to a lower down time, and add more 
value to the offering. However, these 
services would not directly improve the 
environmental impact as such, hence no 
KPIs were identified for those. 
Considering the potential KPIs that could 
express the environmental potential of 
the PSS services, it was realised that, with 
the input from the supplier regarding 
the content of their provided services, it 
would be possible to affect the existing 
KPIs in the current list. As a result, it 
would be possible to add more depth 
to the evaluation while simultaneously 
avoid that the list of KPIs got longer. 
For instance, the material mass of the 
expected number of spare parts could be 
added to the total waste mass that would 
be applicable by End-of-Life. The expected 
minimum lifetime of the product without 
repair services could also be an indicator 
for the increased generation of total waste 
mass in pure product offerings. This led 
to the creation of four additional service 
indicators to the KPI list, as seen in Figure
161

Testing the updated 
environmental evaluation
The redesigned environmental KPI list was 
tested on Investment A and Investment B 
to see whether the suppliers were able to 
answer the service related questions and 
whether any changes to the environmental 
evaluation occurred. 
Investment A
The supplier for Investment A was able 
to provide input to all service related 
questions, which allowed measuring the 
service indicators that were added to the 
new version of the support (Appendix Q.1). 
The material mass for the most frequently 
used spare part and the expected 
frequency for using spare parts to replace 
components during use of the technology 
were used to estimate the expected 
additional material mass in the life time 
of the PSS. The supplier’s expectations 
regarding the technology’s minimum life 
time also indicated the average reference 
material flow for the different offerings 
with or without repair of the technology. 
Since the expected minimum lifetime of 
the system was assumed to be 10 years, 
i.e. more than the functional unit, the
material reference flow of the offerings 
would be anticipated to be equal to 
the previous results. The new service 
indicators also take into consideration 
whether 
the 
offering 
includes 
the 
possibility to upgrade. Assuming that 
there would be two updated versions of 
the technology available during the use of 
the equipment, the entire system would 
have to be disposed to make space for 
the upgrade for the reference offering 
and Offering 1, which do not include 
upgradability. However, in the leasing 
offering (Offer 2) the supplier includes 
the possibility to upgrade the system, and 
take back the old one. As a result, material 
loops would be closed, and zero waste 
would be accounted for the customer. 
Since none of the offerings include 
preventive maintenance by default in the 
offering (can be added as an optional 
service in the SLP, with an additional fee), 
there would be an equal consumption of 
spare parts during the system lifetime. 
As a result of considering the additional 
service indicators, the environmental 
performance of the leasing agreement 
was still highest, with an improvement 
from the Reference corresponding to 80% 
instead of 100%, due to the accounting for 
spare parts during the system lifetime. The 
environmental impact of the Reference 
and Offering 1 still remained equal. The 
updated environmental benchmark for 
Investment A is presented in Table 18. 
How the different offerings perform 
within each indicator is also illustrated 
in the spiderweb diagram in Figure 56, 
where all offerings have equal scores for 
all indicators except for the total solid 
waste mass (KPI M5). For this KPI, both 
product-oriented 
offerings 
(Reference 
and Offer 1) have an impact score of 
five, while the leasing agreement has a 
score of one, due to the significant waste 
reduction as a result of the take-back 
service from supplier.  The differences in 
the overall environmental impact for the 
three offerings are visualised in Figure 57.

Selection of offering
With 
an 
updated 
environmental 
benchmark available, the user took a look 
at the new final scorecard (Table 19), to 
be able to interpret both the economic 
and environmental scores in one place. 
According to the benchmark, the total 
cost of ownership is lowest for the pure 
product offering (Reference). The leasing 
agreement (Offer 2) is only 18% more 
expensive than the Reference. On the 
other hand, the product offering with 
product related services (Offer 1) has the 
highest total cost of ownership, being 34% 
higher than the Reference. Thus, Offer 
1 was eliminated as an option as part of 
the decision making process, which left 
the user with the pure product offer and 
the leasing agreement. Due to the leasing 
agreement’s lower environmental impact 
and acceptable TCO, the buyer ended up 
with selecting this for the investment.

additional service indicators (Appendix 
R.1). 
Assuming 
that 
the 
preventive 
maintenance included in Offer 1 (silver) 
and Offer 2 (gold) would result in a 
reduction of spare part use by 11%, the 
total solid waste mass would decrease.

in the offerings, the minimum lifetime of it 
would be 10 years, which is the needed time 
for using the technology as defined in the 
functional unit. Thus, it could be assumed 
that the repair services in the service 
offerings would not be necessary and the 
reference flow would be the same for all 
offerings. As a result, the only difference 
between the three offerings would be 
the added solid waste mass coming from 
the increased use of spare parts for the 
reference offering, which does not include 
any preventive maintenance. This gave a 
2% lower environmental impact overall 
for both Offer 1 and Offer 2, since they 
include the same amount of services and 
preventive maintenance. The updated 
environmental benchmark for Investment

only small differences between the

waste mass (KPI M5). Since the compared 
pieces of equipment are the same, the 
energy consumption in operation would 
be the same (KPI E1). The differences in 
the overall environmental impact for the

Selection of offering
With 
an 
updated 
environmental 
benchmark available for Investment B, 
it made sense for the user to reconsider 
the final scorecard (Table 21), which 
also contains the financial comparison. 
According to the benchmark, the total 
cost of ownership was lowest for the pure 
product offering, as for Investment A. 
However, it is important to consider other 
hidden expenditures during the lifecycle 
of these pure product offers that might 
make them look cheaper than what they 
actually are. Offer 2 (gold) was 25% more 
expensive, and was therefore eliminated 
as an option. However, Offer 1 (silver) was 
only 13% more expensive. Additionally, 
due to the additional services in the silver 
offering and 2% lower environmental 
impact, it could have a sourcing potential 
and be competitive against the pure 
product offer. Thus, the buyer contacted 
the equipment responsible to discuss 
whether the silver service contract could 
be beneficial to them despite the additional 
costs. The equipment responsible stated 
that they never actually had thought 
about including services in the purchase 
but perceived them as advantageous.
Therefore, the buyer ended up with 
selecting the offer with product related 
services at a silver level for the investment 
(Offer 1).   
Potential risks, barriers and failures 
when implementing the PSS
Despite 
that 
the 
selected 
PSS 
for 
both investments have potentials for 
environmental impact reduction and have, 
according to the buyer, an acceptable 
total cost of ownership, there might still 
be potential risks connected to these 
investment. For instance, there might 
occur different barriers that complicate 
the procurement of the offerings, and 
failures might arise after the procurement 
and implementation of the PSS. First, the 
purchasing process itself might be a barrier 
when sourcing the PSS, as there will be a 
transition from fixed to variable costs, as 
discussed in the Descriptive Study I. This 
might mean that processes and systems 
related to budgeting have to be adjusted, 
which might require significant resources 
and time. For the leasing agreement in 
particular, the conversion from owning to 
not owning equipment might be a big leap 
for many stakeholders in the company, 
169
e.g. procurement, finance, and Line of 
Business. As the PSS domain is an unfamiliar 
territory for several of these stakeholders, 
the processes of accommodating PSS 
integration in the company might require 
time to be developed.  Secondly, the 
increase in contractual complexities might 
create another barrier when procuring 
the PSS. Moving from simple transactional 
contracts to more compound contracts, 
where more legal conditions need to 
be considered, would also require time 
to get used to, and more training of the 
employees would be necessary. The 
added services in the offerings would 
also entail increased communication 
and interdependencies between supplier 
and customer. All these new elements 
would involve changes to the current 
routines and processes. The supplier’s 
added interference in the company’s 
operational processes when providing 
services, might also promote conflicts 
with regards to confidentiality. This might 
in particular be an issue for Investment A, 
where the technology is used for patients 
participating in undisclosed clinical trials. 
The laboratory in which Investment B is 
used might also not appreciate that
Table 21 Final scorecard for investment B (Appendix R.3)
170
external 
stakeholders 
get 
close 
to 
confidential 
tests 
and 
research. 
Considering 
the 
leasing 
agreement, 
there might  occur misunderstandings 
regarding ownership and responsibilities 
related to the handling and management 
of the equipment. For instance, according 
to the contract, the users of the equipment 
are not allowed to move the equipment, 
or remove the installation from its original 
position but needs to be carried out by 
the supplier. Thus, if users are not aware 
of rules like these, complications would 
probably occur, and the company might 
be held responsible for any breakdown 
that might follow. These are risks of 
failures that the company would have to 
consider when buying this PSS.   
Direct rebound effects
Although 
the 
selected 
PSS 
were 
evaluated to have a higher environmental 
performance than the other offerings, 
there might occur risks of unintentional 
rebound effects, as depicted in Figure 60. 
First, as expressed by Koide, R., Murakami, 
S., & Nansai, K. (2022) in Figure 21 in the 
Descriptive Study I, use behaviour and
Descriptive Study II
Descriptive Study II
==PAGE:91==
product substitution are identified as 
the most frequently occurring factors 
for rebound effects to upgrade of the 
product. Seen in relation to upgrade of 
the equipment, as part of the leasing 
agreement, more components and product 
modules would probably be required. 
As a consequence, there would be a risk 
of increased material consumption and 
waste generation. As a countermeasure, 
the users could adjust use behaviour and 
ensure that upgrade of equipment only 
is performed to substitute retirement of 
the entire piece of equipment, and not 
substitute perfectly functioning pieces 
of equipment. If a significant part of 
the equipment has to be disposed of as 
part of the upgrade, it is also essential 
to ensure that the waste is kept in the 
material loop by either e.g. reusing, 
refurbishing and remanufacturing, or 
recycling components.
As confirmed by Koide, R., Murakami, S., 
& Nansai, K. (2022), the leasing, and not 
owning the technology, could also result 
in uncareful use behaviour, which could 
cause more damages to the equipment. 
As a consequence, equipment might need
Figure 60 Potential direct and indirect rebound effects from investment A and B (Appendix S)
171
172
to be retired earlier than the expected 
life time, which would result in increased 
waste 
generation 
and 
material 
consumption to replace the damaged 
equipment. The same might happen for 
Investment B, where the complimentary 
repair visits from supplier might lead 
to less cautious use of the equipment, 
which might give similar consequences. 
To prevent this uncareful use behaviour 
from happening, it might be a good idea 
to establish contracts that incentivise 
the user to treat equipment carefully. 
For example, in any case of damage 
caused by uncareful use when leasing the 
equipment, the customer would have to 
cover the potential expenses connected 
to repair or replacement. In the case of 
Investment B, it might be necessary to put 
a limit to the included services and repairs 
within the contract period, to promote 
more cautious use.  
The added service provision included in 
both investments will also, presumably, 
lead to more transportation, which is one 
of the most frequently reported reason 
for potential rebound effects, according 
to the studies conducted by Koide, R., 
Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. (2022). Added 
trasnportation 
increases 
energy 
and 
CO2 consumption if using fossil based 
fuels. The added transportation might be 
difficult to avoid when increasing service 
provision. Hence, the key is to ensure that 
the energy used comes from renewable 
sources, which also, according to Koide, 
R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. (2022), 
constitutes a factor that minimise the 
potential for rebound effect. 
Indirect rebound effects
Higher material consumption might also 
occur indirectly as a consequence of the 
eliminated acquisition cost in the leasing 
agreement. Elimination of the acquisition 
cost would lead to a higher Networking 
Capital (NWC), which might be allocated to 
the purchase of more equipment or other 
non-sustainable investments. However, it 
might be argued that facility expansion 
and other investments are crucial for 
growth of the company. Therefore, it 
is important to measure sustainability 
performance of all investments that are 
made in the company, e.g. with help 
from the tool for evaluating sustainability 
performance of supplier offerings. 
Due the efficiency improvements in 
Investment A and Investment B, there 
might be a risk that the end user’s 
satisfaction 
prevent 
investigation 
of 
other PSS that fulfil the same need at 
later stages. This will possibly substitute 
towards consuming those PSS rather 
than others that might even have a lower 
resource 
consumption. 
This 
product 
substitution might result in a higher 
environmental impact, as reported by 
14% of the participants of the study from 
Koide, R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. 
(2022). As a preventive action it is essential 
to promote competitive sourcing for each 
investment, e.g. with help from the tool 
for evaluating sustainability performance 
of supplier offerings.
Economy wide rebound effects 
As a result of sourcing more PSS with 
help from the support tool, a push in the 
market for PSS would possibly occur. If this 
happens with limited supplier maturity 
or readiness, it might lead to suppliers 
providing their regular products as PSS, 
which are not designed for PSS purposes, 
e.g. not designed for durability or higher 
number of uses, in which the latter is one
Descriptive Study II
Descriptive Study II
==PAGE:92==
of the most frequently reported reasons 
for rebound effect, according to Koide, 
R., Murakami, S., & Nansai, K. (2022). This 
might promote more damages to the 
equipment, which might have to be retired 
earlier than its expected lifetime. Thus, 
providing CE training to the suppliers to 
increase knowledge about how to connect 
CE strategies to the offerings to boost 
profit and increase resource efficiency, 
might result in more products being 
particularly designed for PSS purposes. 
Hence, increase of waste generation and 
resource consumption can be avoided.
Another problem connected to increased 
demand of PSS is the extension of current 
markets and the development of new 
markets instead of converting existing 
production systems to adapt PSS. This 
development might require completely 
new infrastructure and systems, which will 
entail a higher consumption of resources. 
To minimise this increased impact, it would 
be necessary to investigate how existing 
production systems and infrastructure can 
be utilised to the highest extent possible 
to adapt development and production of 
PSS.
Figure 61 Potential economy wide rebound effects connected to sourcing PSS (Appendix S)
As a consequence of introducing more 
PSS to the market, there might also occur 
structural changes in existing production 
patterns, e.g. more products might be 
designed and manufactured for durability, 
which might result in an increased energy 
and resource consumption. In this case, it 
would be important to integrate circular 
strategies to the PSS that minimise waste 
generation and resource consumption 
by closing material loops, to compensate 
for the increased energy and resource 
consumption. It would also be important 
to ensure that the energy used comes from 
renewable sources. Potential economy 
wide rebound effects are presented in 
Figure 61.
Evaluation of the fulfilment of the 
support goal 
After applying the developed support for 
two different investments, it was possible 
to see that it actually could be integrated 
and used in the current procurement 
process, to facilitate the sourcing of 
the 
supplier 
offering 
with 
highest 
sustainability potential. First, it was seen 
how it could encourage the user to think 
how a function could satisfy their needs 
instead of a physical product. The support 
also helped the buyers with identifying a 
suitable PSS that would match the context 
of their specific investment and provided 
knowledge and guidance around how 
access to technology can be achieved 
in different ways, and the benefits 
from that. Then, the environmental 
evaluation enabled the user to prioritise 
environmental KPIs, and get detailed 
insights into how the offerings, that they 
consider buying, perform environmentally. 
Being able to add this environmental 
evaluation to the calculation of total cost 
of ownership facilitated the selection 
of the supplier offering with highest 
sustainability performance, as aimed for 
in the goal of the 
support, creating a stronger foundation 
for making valid decisions in procurement. 
Thus, it can be argued that the support 
has the potential to close the gaps that 
are preventing the procurement of the 
most optimal PSS in terms of economic 
and environmental performance.
Evaluation of the fulfilment of the 
requirement 
specification 
for 
the 
support
Reconsidering the defined requirement 
specification 
as 
outlined 
in 
the 
Descriptive Study I (Table 10), all the 
demands connected to the usefulness 
of the support (requirement 1, 2 and 3) 
are fulfilled. As part of the survey, the 
users explained that they understood 
the purpose of each activity (requirement 
1). Then, through the evaluation of the 
support it has been possible to see that 
the tool supports the decision process for 
investments of equipment by enabling 
an identification of the PSS with highest 
sustainability performance (requirement 
2). To enable this, it was necessary to 
include a tool that would allow the buyer 
to evaluate economic and environmental 
performance of the PSS. The requirement 
Discussion of DS-II
specification also states that the time spent 
on the evaluation of the environmental 
performance of the PSS should not exceed 
one hour (requirement 3). Using the results 
from the evaluation this requirement is 
also argued to be fulfilled, as buyer A and 
B spent 15 minutes in average for Activity 
5 (insert environmental KPI data). 
The users also explained that they 
understood how to use the support 
(requirement 6). However, the usability 
of specific parts of the support had 
improvement potential, as discussed 
in the “Suggestions for improvement” 
section.  Implementing the suggestions 
for 
improvement 
would 
presumably 
make the support fit better into current 
processes, and the use of the support 
would become more effortless for both 
buyer and supplier. 
Another requirement for the support 
was that the buyer should not actively 
spend more than two hours using the tool 
(requirement 7). As presented previously in 
Figure 53, the average active time spent 
for the two users in total for all activities 
was one hour and a half, hence satisfying 
Descriptive Study II
Descriptive Study II
173
174
==PAGE:93==
this requirement. A wish defined in the 
requirement specification was to facilitate 
applicability of the support for different 
types of investments than for laboratory 
equipment (requirement 8). Since the 
evaluation of the support was based on 
investments for laboratory equipment in 
particular, it is not possible to conclude that 
the entire support would be applicable for 
different investments. Nevertheless, the 
part of the support that includes the offer 
evaluation would with high guarantee 
work for most investments, as evaluating 
the financial and environmental outcome 
is necessary for all investments in 
general. The environmental KPI list is 
also composed of standard indicators 
that measure material, energy and water 
consumption of the PSS. As the KPIs 
are directly linked to the environmental 
procurement strategy within the case 
company, measuring them would also 
be relevant in procurement other parts 
of the organisation. Due to the generic 
character of the indicators, it can also be 
assumed that the list of KPIs also would 
be applicable in procurement in other 
companies. The part of the support that 
encourages the buyer to shift from a 
product-oriented mindset to a need and 
function based mindset is probably also 
not specific to investment of laboratory 
equipment. In fact, this element of the 
support could be valuable for all types 
of investments. The same would apply 
for the part of the support that provides 
a generic PSS guidance. Considering the 
decision model, it is possible to argue 
that the required inputs are basic to most 
investments, such as defining time frame 
of the needed technology, budget, and 
available infrastructure. Thus, it is argued 
that all elements of the support would be 
applicable to most types of investments. 
The current version of the support does 
not include two of the wishes from 
the 
requirement 
specification. 
The 
support does not yet link environmental 
mechanisms or CE strategies of the 
evaluated PSS together with risks of 
rebound effects and possibilities for 
mitigation (requirement 4). Neither does 
it help the user with easily identifying 
potential risks or failure modes, nor 
suggests 
ways 
to 
mitigate 
these 
(requirement 5). These support capabilities 
would provide additional depth to the
offer evaluation, and create an even 
stronger foundation to perform valid 
decision making. However, they could be 
added in next iterations of the support 
moving forward. 
Limitations of the support
As observed for Investment B, the 
environmental evaluation did not give 
very different results for the three 
different offerings. As mentioned in the 
“Evaluation of the support”, this might 
be due to the fact that many of the KPIs 
measure the environmental performance 
related to the product, which is not 
expected to vary significanly for product-
oriented PSS. Another important factor is 
that the offerings were provided from the 
same supplier, meaning that there are no 
variations of the product. Therefore, the 
ability to make decisions for investments 
like those might be limited from an 
environmental point of view. After adding 
more indicators that cover the service 
delivery of the PSS there would be 
some differences in the environmental 
impact score, which would help with the 
decision making. Also reconsidering the 
mechanisms that influence the 
175
might require a different procedure 
than what is proposed for equipment in 
the current support, which might also 
include a different decision model. Due 
to the consumables’ dependency of the 
equipment, it is possible that a support for 
consumables should be built as an add-on 
to the current support. This would imply 
that the support for the consumables 
would take the suggested PSS for the 
equipment into account when proposing 
a suitable PSS for the consumables. 
Integrating 
guidance 
for 
purchasing 
consumables as PSS should be prioritised 
in the near future.   
Another important limitation of the 
support is that it lacks linking potential 
risks of rebound effects to the suggested 
PSS and the CE strategies connected to 
it, as well as suggesting ways to mitigate 
these. Not considering this will create some 
restraints to the environmental evaluation 
part of the support, as the buyer would 
not know the potential induced impacts 
that the PSS might lead to and how to 
prevent these from happening. Thus, 
an important prioritisation for the next 
iteration of the support is to integrate this 
feature. This could be done by listing 
the major reasons for potential backfire 
effects that Koide, R., Murakami, S., & 
Nansai, K. (2022) identified, e.g. transport, 
number of uses, product substitution, 
etc., and linking them to the CE strategies 
and service content of the selected PSS. 
Furthermore, proposals on how these 
backfire effects might be managed should 
be provided to the buyer.  
Sensitivity of the support 
Using the support for Investment A and 
Investment B enabled the buyers to 
evaluate the environmental performance 
of the offerings. Nevertheless, it is 
important to consider the sensitivity of 
the data provided by the vendors. First of 
all, as mentioned in the “Discussion of PS”, 
the availability of data might be limited 
to the supplier, meaning that supplier 
only provides data for few KPIs, as seen 
for Investment A. As a consequence in 
that case, the leasing agreement was 
evaluated to have a lower environmental 
impact, although it only was measured by 
one indicator, i.e. total solid waste mass 
(KPI M5). This makes the offer evaluation 
a sensitive part of the tool. The lack of 
176
environmental potential of a PSS, as 
described by Koide, R., Murakami, S., & 
Nansai, K. (2022) and Tukker, A. (2004), 
the product related services and advice 
normally have the potentials to make 
equal to moderate improvements to the 
environmental impact. Thus, it can also 
be argued that similar scores for the 
offerings could appear in any case, also 
if using different KPIs. However, in cases 
such as Investment A, the environmental 
evaluation would give clearer results 
when comparing a pure product against 
a use-oriented PSS, normally due to 
the additional life-extending services 
embedded in the offering, e.g. take-
back. Comparing a pure product against 
a result oriented PSS would presumably 
also provide more interesting and diverse 
results to the benchmark. 
Consumables are often required to run 
some types of equipment. However, what 
to consider when purchasing consumables 
for the equipment is not fully covered 
by the support. Access to consumables 
could also be obtained through PSS, and 
perhaps even a different PSS than what is 
suggested for the equipment itself. This 
Descriptive Study II
Descriptive Study II
==PAGE:94==
available data might also mean that 
the suppliers have to provide estimated 
numbers. 
As 
a 
consequence, 
the 
environmental evaluation would be based 
on uncertain data, which reduces the 
soundness of the benchmark. There are 
also specific KPIs that almost under all 
circumstances promote uncertain and 
assumption based data. These are leading 
indicators, and are connected to expected 
consumption during operation and to the 
expected contents of the service delivery. 
As the lifecycle of the PSS would not have 
been initiated when providing the data, it 
is natural that numbers for operation and 
service based consumption would have 
to be provided as projected estimations. 
For example, the amount of hazardous 
or non-recyclable solid waste generated 
during operation or through service 
provision cannot be known in advance 
but can be predicted based on experience 
or expectations. The same applies for 
indicators such as expected minimum 
lifetime of the technology, expected 
number of upgraded versions of the 
technology available during the amount 
of years that the technology is needed, 
and expected frequency of changing
spare parts. An even bigger potential 
jeopardization to the results of the 
benchmark would be if the suppliers 
provide data for different functional 
units, as this would lead to an unfair and 
invalid comparison. Despite that the final 
iteration of the support intends to ensure 
that the suppliers provide data for the 
same functional unit, the risk of it not 
happening might still occur. However, the 
buyer would have to trust that the data 
they get from the suppliers address the 
same functional unit when making the 
evaluation. Therefore, there are several 
elements connected to the environmental 
evaluation tool that make the support 
sensitive.
Conclusion of DS-II
As a result of applying the developed 
tool for two different investments within 
procurement of laboratory equipment, 
it is possible to conclude that the tool 
supports decision making in the selection 
process of supplier offerings.  This is 
primarily because of its ability to enable 
the user to identify the offerings with 
highest sustainability performance in 
terms of economy and environment. 
With the highest prioritised suggestions 
for 
improvement 
implemented 
the 
support became even more valuable 
as the environmental evaluation also 
would reflect the potential increase of 
environmental performance, as a result 
of services added in the offerings. The 
changes to the support allowed the 
buyer to also identify differences within 
environmental performance for product 
oriented PSS, provided by the same 
supplier. However, the evaluation showed 
that changes related to the usability would 
be required for the support to blend better 
into the current procurement process.

Descriptive Study II
