==PAGE:95==
Readiness for PSS in procurement 
Applying the support for investments 
of laboratory equipment in the case 
company gave insights about how ready 
the organisation, as well as the market 
at large, are for PSS application. Despite 
the high interest in integrating PSS in 
procurement from management, the 
considerable organisational inertia at 
the equipment responsible and end-user 
levels could not be ignored. Still, people, 
procedures, systems, and culture within 
procurement are all based on a product-
oriented mindset. This rigid state would 
most likely take time to change. Testing the 
support in the investments also strongly 
indicated that stakeholders within both 
procurement and in Line of Business 
might be victims of the endowment effect, 
where they might perceive the assets they 
own as more valuable than those they 
do not own. Thus, getting used to not 
owning the equipment might also be a 
slow process in the journey towards PSS 
adoption. The support activities involving 
supplier’s contribution also revealed the 
suppliers’ readiness for PSS. The suppliers 
expressed that their customers rarely ask 
for additional services to the offerings 
or alternative procurement models than 
pure transactional product solutions. They 
were not used to providing PSS, which 
might indicate a low maturity of the market 
for providing laboratory equipment as 
PSS.  However, market dynamics would 
directly influence this degree of maturity, 
meaning that the number of PSS offerings 
would be expected to grow as a result 
of increasing requests for PSS from 
customers. Knowledge, experience, and 
capabilities within the PSS area would 
all be expanded if the market’s appetite 
for PSS increased. Therefore, one could 
say that the enrolment of PSS in the 
pharmaceutical industry needs to begin 
with the customers and their interest for 
added value to their offerings, pushing 
the prevalence of PSS within the suppliers’ 
offerings. Thus, it can be argued that 
the suppliers’ readiness for PSS depends 
on the customers’ readiness and their 
demands.  
Neither were the approached suppliers 
used to providing environmental KPIs 
to 
customers 
as 
common 
practice. 
This reflected in their low level of data 
availability.  However, they acknowledged
the case company’s need for obtaining 
environmental 
transparency 
of 
the 
offerings and were impressed by its 
increased environmental ambitiousness. 
With more insights into the environmental 
performance 
being 
demanded 
by 
customers, 
the 
suppliers’ 
efforts 
in 
collecting and reporting environmental 
data would also presumably increase. 
Again, the development in measuring 
environmental 
performance 
would 
start 
with 
the 
customers’ 
demand 
and commitment, which would drive 
the 
suppliers 
to 
ensure 
complete 
environmental transparency. One could 
also imagine that providing data regarding 
the 
environmental 
performance 
of 
the offerings would be a mandatory 
and natural procedure in the future, 
as the customer deserves to know the 
environmental impact they are buying. 
Discussion
Managing change
When introducing the tool to the buyers, 
it was met with initial reluctance. This 
reaction is reasonable, considering that 
the tool could be perceived as a disruptive 
element in their current routines and 
regular investment procedure. Some 
procurement responsibles might have 
been doing the same job for a number 
of years, coping well with their job 
demands and performing their tasks 
efficiently, relying on the same level 
of 
organisational 
support, 
routines, 
methods, and technological solutions. 
These are also most likely repeated 
patterns from generation to generation 
in the procurement organisation. After 
many years of performing the same job, 
they might also have  accumulated a 
wealth of success stories with different 
suppliers and end-users, all contributing 
to making sense of their job situation. 
Thus, as a consequence of introducing a 
foreign tool, an individual and their state 
of readiness could be thrown off balance. 
Sudden changes in work procedures 
might also create a feeling of loss of power, 
bewilderment, as well as a reduction in 
self-esteem and dignity.
Kotter, J. P. et al. (2013) identify four main 
reasons why organisational individuals 
might be reluctant to support a proposal 
for change. These are lack of trust, low 
tolerance for change, different assessments 
of the need for change and consequences 
of the change, and parochial self-interest. 
Stakeholders might also be unwilling 
to support a change when they do not 
understand the implications it might 
have for them or when they believe that 
the change will cost them more than 
they will gain. Therefore, Weick, K. E., 
Sutcliffe, K. M., & Obstfeld, D. (2005) 
argue that when implementing changes, 
it is essential to provide “sense-making” 
to the stakeholders that will be impacted 
by it. In the context of integrating the 
support, it is crucial that the users of the 
tool understand its purpose, and that the 
visions connected to using the tool are 
well communicated from management. 
The fact that the CFO of the case company 
expresses that there is a need for adopting 
the concepts that the support carries 
creates an extra strong drive, motivation, 
and purpose. According to the authors, 
providing a business direction that will 
lead to achieving key results and an
overview of the organisational capabilities 
required to achieve and sustain this vision, 
is essential to ensure organisational 
alignment and discretionary motivation. 
However, the fact that the tool has 
successfully 
been 
applied 
for 
two 
investments already will, in addition to the 
visualisation of the tool’s benefits, most 
likely contribute to the “sense-making” 
for several stakeholders and ease the 
individual push back.   
Lewin, K. (1952) suggests a three-step 
process to ensure a successful change: 
Unfreezing, moving and refreezing. In the 
context of the support tool, unfreezing 
and unlocking the existing level of 
behaviour would require creating a 
shared vision that explains the purpose 
and intended impact of the support. As 
mentioned, a push from management 
would be necessary for this situation to 
guarantee extra strong drive, motivation, 
and sense-making for the stakeholders. 
Next, the moving and shift to a new level 
would involve using the support, which 
would contribute to adjusting attitudes 
and beliefs and modifying the processes, 
systems, and structures that shape
179
180
==PAGE:96==
behaviour. Finally, the process of refreezing 
behaviour at this new level would involve 
ensuring 
that 
stakeholders 
receive 
feedback that signals effectiveness and 
consistency of the new behaviours and 
incentives that reward new levels of 
performance. In this context, feedback 
could be cost savings, observed lower 
environmental 
impact, 
satisfied 
end 
users and equipment responsibles, and 
improved supplier relationships. These 
results would then probably compensate 
for the efforts and energy spent on 
moving to this new level. Additionally, this 
feedback would contribute to sustaining 
the change, preventing regression to its 
original state.
Impact of the support
Impact at artefact level
If ensuring effective change management 
during 
implementation, 
the 
support 
will have significant impact potential 
in procurement.  First, by encouraging 
need and function-based thinking in 
procurement, a strong and competitive 
sourcing will be observed. Instead of 
deciding what type of equipment would be 
required to satisfy their needs, they would 
instead identify the required functions 
that will fulfil their needs.  Accordingly, the 
number of possible solutions to how their 
needs can be fulfilled will increase. The 
buyers also get the opportunity to explore 
different ways to achieve access to the 
technology that will realise their needs. As 
a result of using the decision model and 
procurement model guidance, they will be 
able to quickly identify the PSS that is most 
appropriate for their specific investment, 
for which they can ask suppliers. This 
was not possible before. Additionally, 
the support tool allows the buyer to 
compare the sustainability performance 
of the supplier offerings by providing an 
integrated package consisting of 
economic and environmental calculation 
tools, which have not been available 
earlier either. This will provide a strong 
foundation for the buyer’s decision-
making when purchasing equipment. 
As seen for the investments used for 
the evaluation in the Descriptive Study 
II, the sustainability evaluation also has 
the power to alter the choice in offering 
altogether. In the case of Investment A, 
the leasing agreement was not considered 
until the sustainability results of the 
offering became available. Neither would 
the equipment responsible for Investment 
B even consider adding supplementary 
services to the offering if it were not for the 
offer evaluation. Thus, as a result of using 
the support, these offerings were selected 
for the investments, which will reduce the 
company’s waste and, hence, lower the 
environmental impact. Due to its general 
and universal structure, the support tool 
can also be applicable in other areas than 
the sourcing of laboratory equipment. If 
scaled up in all procurement areas, the 
support tool would have the potential to 
create an even more significant impact 
on the company by reducing cost and 
environmental impact.
Impact at system level
Looking at the big picture, the support 
tool will initiate the design of new systems 
that prevent resource consumption. The 
switch from a product-based to a need-
based mindset will promote thinking in a 
function capacity rather than in physical 
products. Since the way to fulfil a need 
would no longer be fixed to a physical 
product, the buyers have the opportunity 
to purchase higher value and fulfil their 
needs with the lowest consumption 
of resources possible. This will play a 
significant role in the decoupling of value 
creation from resource consumption, 
which presumably will be one of the main 
contributors to achieving the science-
based targets. Creating more freedom to 
fulfil needs through PSS will also stimulate 
more innovative and bold thinking. Using 
innovation to solve the most critical issues 
that the world is facing today is essential 
to reach these ambitious targets, both 
within companies and society. Due to 
procurement taking part in a considerable 
share of the value chain, using the support 
has the potential to contribute significantly 
to the European Commission’s target of 
reducing domestic material
consumption by 70% by 2050 (BIO 
Intelligence Service, Institute for Social 
Ecology and Sustainable Europe Research 
Institute, 2012).
By enabling procurement to evaluate 
the 
economic 
and 
environmental 
performance of the offerings, the support 
allows the buyer to identify and source 
more sustainable solutions. This means 
that more suppliers will be asked to 
provide more value in their offerings 
through the application of PSS. By creating 
increased demand and momentum for 
PSS solutions from the customers, the 
support tool has the power to influence 
the market dynamics of the PSS domain, 
creating a wave after which procurement 
will stop the product-centred mentality, 
and instead focus on attaining access 
to a technology or function that fulfils a 
specific need.  Given the arguments, it can 
be concluded that the support tool has 
the potential to significantly influence the 
entire value chain. 
181
182
==PAGE:97==
Recommendations for further work
Some recommendations and suggestions 
for future work were provided to the case 
company to support the implementation 
of the tool, and to ensure that the tool 
remains useful in a more distant future. 
As presented in Figure 62, a roadmap 
was developed to illustrate when the 
recommended actions should be taken, 
and how the application of the support 
is expected to evolve. The assumptions 
related to the roadmap are presented in 
Appendix T. 
Since the environmental KPI list has the 
potential to be utilised independently 
from the tool, several procurement 
responsibles have already used it for 
sourcing events as part of the procurement 
process. Suppliers were asked to provide 
numbers to the indicators in requests for 
information, which the buyer could use 
to either store  data on environmental 
performance of the individual offerings, or 
to compare environmental performance 
of different offerings before taking the 
ultimate choice. Assuming that the KPI list 
already is used for 80% of the investments 
in the current investment wheel, means 
that a 70% environmental transparency
is ensured for 80% of the investments 
in laboratory sourcing, presuming that 
the suppliers provide data for 70% of 
all KPIs in average. This also means 
that the environmental transparency 
of investments increases by 2% in the 
entire company, assuming that there 
are 972 annual investments available 
in the company. As a result, significant 
impact can already be achieved before 
integrating the tool as a whole. Therefore, 
the company is recommended to use the 
environmental KPI list as an independent 
component until the entire tool is ready 
for application.  
As discovered based on the evaluation of 
the support, there are a few things that 
should be set in motion before using the 
tool in practice. First, contract templates 
should be generated for all types of 
PSS agreements with supplier that are 
suggested by the decision model. Without 
those, supplier would have to spend a 
lot of time on creating contracts for each 
specific investment, and it would be 
challenging to close the agreements. The 
template contracts would, on the other 
hand, lower the barrier for sourcing PSS, 
and make the process progress more 
efficiently and professionally. After having 
created template contracts, the tool would 
be ready for use in practice. However, there 
is one low-hanging fruit that the company 
is recommended to implement before 
introducing it to the users. As presented 
in “Suggestions for improvement”, a 
suggestion for improving usability is to 
incorporate the environmental KPI list 
to the business spend management 
platform, Coupa, which would make the 
environmental evaluation an integrated 
part of the sourcing event. There, suppliers 
would be able to directly insert data for 
the KPIs as part of the tender, which 
they would submit together with the 
quotations. This would enable the buyer 
to export all suppliers’ data into one single 
Excel file, and rank them accordingly. As 
a result, the need for sending an Excel 
sheet back and forth between supplier 
and buyer is avoided, which would reduce 
the total time spent on the procurement 
process. This is estimated to reduce the 
time spent on Activity 3 (ask for supplier 
offerings) by 33%, and 67% for Activity 5 
(insert environmental KPI data), meaning 
that the total time spent on the tool
Figure 62 Roadmap for the case company, presenting when recommended actions should be taken 
183
184
==PAGE:98==
would be reduced by 16%. Less handovers 
between supplier and buyer would also 
lead to less non-value adding activities, 
where the waiting time between Activity 
3 and Activity 4 would also be expected to 
be reduced.
As the last element before applying the 
support tool, the support and its purpose 
should be presented to the potential 
users, i.e. the buyers in procurement. As 
mentioned in the “Discussion”, it is essential 
that managers clearly communicate why 
this change is necessary, why this change 
is happing now, what is wrong with what 
procurement is doing today, and what will 
happen if procurement do not change. 
Manangers should present their vision 
of what a more desirable state might 
look like, and how the support can help 
move towards this better future. This will 
create and extra strong drive, motivation 
and purpose, which will contribute with 
unfreezing the current levels of behaviour. 
As observed during the application of the 
support for Investment A and Investment 
B, 
additional 
personal 
support 
was 
required 
when 
performing 
specific 
activities (mainly Activity 1 and Activity 5). 
Thus, a more thorough presentation 
of how the tool works, and training of 
the users within the PSS area would be 
required before it should be used. This 
will probably increase the buyers’ trust, 
knowledge, and competences regarding 
PSS application, and will make the buyers 
feel more confident about using the tool 
in their own routines. The training will 
also support the movement to a new level, 
considering 
the 
three-step 
approach 
suggested by Lewin, K. (1952).     
Assuming that the implementation of the 
aforementioned recommendations would 
take one month, the tool is expected to be 
ready for use by laboratory sourcing by 
September 2022. The tool should be used 
for all investments, which would mean 
that a 70% environmental transparency 
is ensured for 100% of the investments 
in Laboratory Sourcing, corresponding 
to about 90 investments. This would 
correspond to a 5% increase of the total 
environmental 
transparency 
of 
the 
investments in the company. Assuming 
that 80% of the investments applying the 
tool end up with sourcing PSS, results in 
application of PSS for 74 investments, 
which corresponds to 8% of the company’s 
total investments. 
After initiating the application of the tool, 
management should call for adjusting the 
setup of the URS, where Activity 1 would 
be an integrated part of it. This would 
mean that the equipment responsible 
would 
have 
to 
perform 
Activity 
1 
instead of procurement. This would 
eliminate definition of product specific 
requirements in the URS but rather 
promote definition of the obligatory and 
optional properties required for fulfilling 
the need. Additionally, they would have to 
define the functional unit to sum up the 
needed function and set the boundaries 
for the supplier, which will form the basis 
of the investment. This is expected to take 
more time than the previously mentioned 
suggestions, 
as 
this 
would 
require 
changes to current standard operating 
procedures. 
In 
addition, 
approvals 
from several stakeholders would most 
likely be necessary, and new processes 
would require some training of the 
equipment responsibles. However, when 
implemented, the time spent on Activity 1 
is estimated to be reduced by 35%, 
reducing the total time spent on the tool 
by 8%.    
As a result of applying the tool for 
investments in laboratory sourcing, it 
would be possible for procurement to 
collect, track, and present the results of 
using the support. According to Kreye, M. 
2020, systems rely on information about 
their outputs to regulate their inputs and 
transformation 
processes. 
Therefore, 
tracking 
potential 
cost 
savings 
and 
decreased environmental impact will play 
a crucial role in strengthening purpose 
and sustaining the change. Individual or 
collective recognition, rewards, group 
celebration, or even something as simple as 
a personal acknowledgement of progress 
are also recommended actions that will 
contribute with reinforcing change, and 
enabling refreezing behaviour at this new 
level in procurement. The positive results 
and success stories will also be important 
in a potential scale-up of the application 
of the support in the entire company. 
After having collected sufficient data and 
results, a scale-up in all procurement 
departments of the case company could 
be expected to take place six months after
implementing the support in Laboratory 
Sourcing. This would mean that the tool 
would be used for about 970 investments, 
where approximately 780 of those are 
expected to source PSS. Still assuming 
that a 70% environmental transparency 
would be realistic for the investments in 
average, this scale-up would lead to a 63% 
increase in environmental transparency 
of all investments in the case company. 
If using the tool turns out to make these 
impacts, the support also has the potential 
to be scaled up further externally. 
Sharing knowledge and good practises 
in other companies based on experience 
about using the support has a potential 
for creating a wave in industry, where 
procurement would aim at decoupling 
resource consumption from value creation. 
Assuming that the positive results from 
using the support are communicated to 
other companies, and that the tool also 
has the power to convince more buyers 
to source PSS, as observed for Investment 
A and Investment B, the scale-up of PSS 
adoption would be boosted.
In 
near 
future, 
the 
company 
is 
recommended to explore how access 
to consumables also can be achieved 
through PSS as a supplement to the 
current 
support, 
which 
primarily 
focuses on equipment. Since the type 
of 
consumables 
would 
depend 
on 
the equipment or technology, it could 
make sense integrating an additional 
decision model that suggests suitable 
ways to achieve access to consumables 
depending on the answers from the 
current decision model. As a result, the 
buyers also get the opportunity to ask the 
supplier providing the equipment to also 
provide the consumables as PSS, or ask 
other suppliers to provide the required 
consumables through better or smarter 
PSS offerings.  
The case company should revisit the list 
of environmental KPIs at an annual basis, 
and adjust this so that it matches current 
organisational 
strategies. 
This 
might 
involve adding new KPIs and eliminating 
some. 
Since 
several 
stakeholders 
expressed 
that 
they 
perceived 
the 
additional services in the offerings as 
valuable, the company could also consider
185
186
==PAGE:99==
Reflection of the design process
integrating more indicators  that would 
enable an evaluation of the service 
delivery. Then, the buyer could be given 
the opportunity to weight the importance 
or degree of usefulness of the different 
service elements. It can also be valuable 
to add indicators that measure social 
sustainability. Additionally, the company 
should strongly consider linking potential 
risks of environmental rebound effects to 
the different PSS suggestions provided by 
the decision model, and a list of proposals 
on how to mitigate these. Addressing these 
risks, social sustainability, and assessing 
the value of the service offerings, as 
supplement to the current sustainability 
benchmark would give more depth to 
the offer evaluation, strengthening the 
decision making.
After having used the support for a longer 
period of time, the buyer also has the 
opportunity to collect the evaluation data, 
identify a pattern regarding the selection 
of PSS, and create a more scientifically 
based decision model that would suggest 
suitable PSS to specific investments. This 
would probably provide more accurate 
suggestions, as it would be more
experience and data-based, compared to 
the current decision model that is based 
on different investment archetypes.
As a reccurring event, preferably at a 
half-yearly basis, the support should 
be evaluated by the users in terms 
of usefulness and usability to ensure 
continuous improvement. One thing to 
consider when evaluating usability could 
be whether the current format and layout 
is adequate, or whether the support 
should be integrated to another software.
As a  result of  organising  the  project 
around the Design Research Method, 
design 
has 
been 
used 
to 
change 
existing situations into preferred ones. 
The methodology has also enabled a 
structured approach to the thesis, and has 
made it possible to manage the complexity 
and dynamics that characterise the design 
process. First, answers to the preliminary 
research questions for the thesis were 
found using relevant literature that was 
identified through a systematic literature 
review. The literature review managed 
to raise more questions, which were 
formulated as research questions for the 
thesis. A research plan describing when in 
the process the research questions were 
expected to be answered was outlined 
as part of the Research Clarification. This 
formed the foundation for the entire 
study. Next, following the research plan 
the aim was to find answers to the two 
first research questions in the Descriptive 
Study I. First, looking both in literature and 
industry, applied PSS solutions offered 
within procurement in industry (RQ 1) was 
explored. Subsequently, using literature it 
was discovered how the selection of the 
most optimal PSS can be supported (RQ
2). This was done by looking into which 
factors that must be considered when 
procuring a PSS (SRQ 2.1), what the key 
factors are influencing the environmental 
potential of a PSS (SRQ 2.2), and which 
guidelines, 
methods, 
and 
tools 
for 
selecting an appropriate PSS exist for 
procurement (SRQ 2.3). Fortunately, the 
availability of data in industry was high, in 
particular from the case company, which 
contributed significantly to identifying the 
main gaps in the Descriptive Study I. Next, 
in the Prescriptive Study, a support that 
would close the gaps that were identified 
in the Descriptive Study I, was designed in 
close collaboration with key stakeholders. 
The involvement of stakeholders from the 
beginning enabled a good clarification of 
the kind of support that was needed, and 
made it possible to collaboratively define 
the content of the support. Regular testing 
of the different support prototypes allowed 
identification of missing elements of the 
support, and how it should be changed 
to better accommodate the users. At last, 
in the Descriptive Study II, it was possible 
to evaluate how useful and usable the 
support is for selecting the most optimal 
PSS (RQ 3). This was considered a 
particularily 
valuable 
part 
of 
the 
methodology, as it gave insights into 
how useful the support actually is in 
practice. The evaluation also made the 
support more valuable in itself, as the 
successful results probably will lower the 
organisational resistance to change, and 
hence ease the implementation of the 
support. Feedback and suggestions for 
improvement from the evaluation then 
determined how the final iteration of the 
support should be designed.
Limitations of the design process
Although the Design Research Method 
has been valuable for structuring the 
design process, there are still some 
limitations connected to the project, and 
there are parts of the process that could 
have been done differently.  Looking at the 
process chronologically, the first research 
question enabled an investigation of 
which PSS solutions are offered or applied 
in B2B contexts (RQ 1). However, this 
research question does not cover how 
widespread the application of PSS is within 
B2B contexts, which could give valuable 
insights to the study. Research questions 
asking, e.g. how common or widespread 
187
188
==PAGE:100==
190
procurement of PSS is in B2B contexts, how 
mature the market is for PSS application in 
procurement, or how the offer availability 
of PSS from suppliers look like could give 
useful knowledge regarding this topic. 
Investigating 
these 
questions 
would 
might involve running more interviews 
with different suppliers and conducting 
additional studies in more pharmaceutical 
companies and other companies. 
Going more in depth regarding the 
information flow in the procurement 
process in the Descriptive Study I could 
also provide a better understanding 
about the many repetitive handovers 
between 
the 
stakeholders 
involved. 
Having this knowledge could mean that 
the first activity of the support, define 
need, would be designed for the “correct” 
user 
(the 
equipment 
responsible) 
from the beginning. This overview and 
understanding could also make the 
support blend even more into the current 
procurement process, and there would 
be a lower risk of the procurement and 
equipment responsible perceiving the 
support as a strange and disturbing 
element, and the reluctance to the support
could be reduced.
As a result of identifying the gaps that 
prevent the most optimal PSS to be 
selected in the current procurement 
process, it was determined in collaboration 
with key stakeholders that the support 
should contain the following elements 
to close these gaps: Need definition, 
PSS 
guidance 
and 
offer 
evaluation. 
Nevertheless, having defined such a wide 
scope for the support has also brought 
some limitations to the design project. 
Having a more narrow scope, could give 
more depth to each element, and would 
make it possible to address more wished 
from 
the 
requirement 
specification. 
For instance, only focusing on the offer 
evaluation would free resources to also 
link potential risks of rebound effects 
for the selected PSS and suggest how to 
mitigate these, strengthening the buyer’s 
decision making. If only focusing on the 
two first elements (need definition and PSS 
guidance), there would also be sufficient 
time to also include more detailed 
guidance regarding consumables as PSS. 
Thus, narrowing the scope to specific 
elements of the support content would
give extra depth to each element. However, 
the evaluation of the support revealed that 
it was necessary to let the buyers define the 
need and provide them with PSS guidance 
before doing the offer evaluation. Without 
those two elements, the buyers would 
not know what to ask supplier about, and 
there would not be any PSS to compare. 
Additionally, without defining the need 
using the functional unit it would not be 
possible to ensure functional equivalence 
and fairly compare different offerings. 
Thus, the success of the offer evaluation 
clearly depends on the two first elements 
of the support, and would not take place 
without those. Based on the evaluation it 
is argued that the three support elements 
are necessary to identify the most optimal 
supplier offering in terms of economic and 
environmental performance. The features 
and wishes to the support that have been 
excluded in this round, can be added as 
supplements at a later stage, as described 
in the section of Recommendations for 
further work.
Since 
the 
buyers 
asked 
questions 
regarding how to use the support during 
the evaluation process,  it has been difficult
to validate how “stand-alone” the support 
is, and how much additional support the 
buyers would need to understand how 
to use it. However, if more time were 
dedicated to the evaluation process, the 
buyer would have sufficient time to more 
thoroughly read and understand the 
instructions of the activities. As a result, 
the need for personal support would 
most likely be reduced. As part of testing 
the support, it could also be interesting to 
encourage the buyers to ask for offerings 
from more than one supplier if time 
allowed.  It is also important to keep in 
mind that the evaluation is only based 
on the application of the tool for two 
investments. Including more investments 
in the evaluation would result in more 
feedback, which would make it possible 
to improve the support even more before 
implementation. However, more feedback 
and suggestions for improvement are 
expected to be generated moving forward 
while applying the support in practice.
189
==PAGE:101==
Evaluation of the fulfilment of the learning objectives
The thesis has aimed at fulfilling the 
learning objectives that were defined in 
the beginning of the project. How and 
when these have been fulfilled during the 
project is presented in this section.
1. Plan, structure and execute a long 
term project in collaboration with mul­
tiple stakeholders and make requested 
deliveries.
A project was initiated in collaboration with 
Laboratory Sourcing in a pharmaceutical 
company with the wish of making the 
procurement of laboratory equipment 
more 
sustainable. 
Thus, 
preliminary 
research questions that would guide the 
investigation of how procurement can 
be made more sustainable through the 
integration of circular business models, 
such as PSS, were created for a literature 
review. This raised more questions related 
to the application of PSS in procurement 
(RQ 1), the support of selecting the most 
optimal PSS (RQ 2), and testing and 
evaluation of a support for selecting an 
appropriate PSS (RQ 3). Thus, the project 
was planned and structured around 
these research questions, and executed 
following a defined research plan.  
Occurring requests from key stakeholders 
have been collected along the project 
and have influenced the end results and 
deliveries. 
2. Collect and structure information 
and data from a problem context, using 
contextual interviews and other appro­
priate methods, and extract and utilise 
the relevant components for an analy­
sis.
In the Descriptive Study I, the research 
question asking which PSS solutions are 
applied or offered within procurement 
in B2B contexts (RQ 1) was investigated 
looking in both literature and industry. 
With regards to the latter, a survey was 
used to effectively collect information and 
data within the case company concerning 
the application of PSS in procurement. 
The information collected in the survey 
was used to create an overview of which 
PSS solutions are applied or offered within 
procurement in the case company, the 
criteria for procuring or not procuring the 
PSS, and whether the implementation of 
the PSS was perceived a success or not. To 
add more depth to the answers from the 
survey, supplementary interviews were
conducted as well, which provided an 
overview of the elements in the current 
procurement 
process 
preventing 
the procurement of PSS. This helped 
identifying the main gaps in the current 
situation. 
3. Develop a support that guides the se­
lection of an optimal PSS, by evaluating 
economic and environmental perfor­
mance, to support decision making in 
procurement. 
In the Prescriptive Study, a support 
was developed to address the problem 
statement and gaps identified in the 
Descriptive 
Study 
I, 
with 
the 
goal 
of enabling the buyer to select an 
optimal PSS in terms of economic and 
environmental performance. The most 
promising guidelines, methods, and tools 
that were found applicable for supporting 
the selection of the most optimal PSS 
as part of the Descriptive Study I, were 
integrated to the support, constituting an 
evaluation tool to compare sustainability 
performance 
of 
different 
supplier 
offerings. The support would help the 
buyer with identifying the most optimal 
PSS to procure in terms of economic and
environmental 
performance, 
hence 
support 
decision 
making 
in 
the 
procurement process. 
4. Evaluate and justify where alterna­
tive business models should be applied 
using a decision-support model. This 
should be used by key stakeholders to 
conduct qualified decision making.
Since findings of  the Descriptive Study I 
showed that there are several elements 
that prevent the buyer from considering 
PSS in the procurement process, it was 
determined, in the Prescriptive Study, that 
the support must contain other elements 
as well as the sustainability evaluation, 
that would promote consideration of 
PSS and help identifying a suitable PSS. 
Thus, a decision model was created to 
help matching suitable PSS to specific 
investments. The buyer would then 
answer a few questions regarding the 
context of the investment, and the 
decision model would suggest a suitable 
PSS based on those inputs. Each specific 
PSS is suggested due to its ability to 
accommodate the different conditions of 
the investment and the considerations 
related to it, as well as its potential
for reducing resource consumption. The 
considerations made for selecting each 
specific PSS would be justified to the user 
by the model, and will hence support the 
buyer’s decision making regarding what 
to ask supplier for. 
5. Develop guidelines for selecting ben­
eficial PSS business models using an 
appropriate format e.g., a model or 
method. This is to facilitate qualified 
and efficient decision making in other 
projects and other parts of the organ­
isation.
As a supplement to the decision model that 
was developed in the Prescriptive Study it 
was necessary to provide the buyer with 
more awareness around the PSS domain. 
Thus, a procurement model guidance 
was created, using a one-pager format, 
to inform the buyer about the numerous 
existing opportunities and variations of 
supplier-customer relationships, and to 
provide an overview of when to consider 
which ones. This would give the buyer the 
possibility to investigate the content of 
the PSS suggested by the decision model, 
what considerations to have in mind when 
procuring it, and the advantages
and dis-advantages related to it. The 
buyer might also use the guidance to 
identify other promising PSS that could 
fit the investment. Thus, creating more 
awareness about PSS will help the buyer 
with qualified and efficient decision 
making. The guidelines also have the 
potential to be used independently from 
the support for procurement in other 
parts of the organisation.
6. Investigate, describe and present al­
ternative business models and evalu­
ate and justify which have the highest 
potential in terms of environmental 
impact reduction and cost saving. 
As part of the evaluation of the support 
in the Descriptive Study II, the support 
was applied for two different investments 
of laboratory equipment. After having 
defined the need of the investment and 
used the decision model, two different PSS 
business models were suggested for the 
buyers. Then, the suppliers were asked 
to provide quotations for the suggested 
business models as well as conventional 
pure product offerings. These supplier 
offerings were then evaluated in terms of 
environmental and economic
191
192
==PAGE:102==
Conclusion
performance using the offer evaluation 
tool from the support. By inserting TCO 
data and the environmental KPI data, 
which were provided by the supplier, a 
sustainability benchmark was created, 
that would make it possible for the 
buyer to identify and justify which offer 
has the highest potential in terms of 
environmental impact reduction and cost 
savings. This justification was presented 
in the Descriptive Study II.
7. Identify, describe and address barri­
ers, risks and failures that may occur 
when implementing a selected prod­
uct/service system.
After having utilised the sustainability 
benchmark in the Descriptive Study II, the 
buyers ended up with selecting specific PSS 
for their investments. The potential risks, 
barriers, and failures when implementing 
these PSS were identified and described 
in the Descriptive Study II. Additionally, 
potential risks of environmental rebound 
effects that might lead to induced impacts 
were 
also 
described. 
Furthermore, 
suggestions for what to be aware of when 
procuring and applying the selected PSS 
were provided to help preventing these 
rebound effects from happening. 
8. Connect the proposed recommenda­
tions to a timeframe and illustrate it 
using a roadmap. This should give key 
stakeholders an indication of when dif­
ferent actions would be beneficial. 
Although a final version of the support 
was presented in the Descriptive Study 
II, suggestions on how to improve it 
were presented and put on a roadmap to 
illustrate for the company when it would 
be beneficial to implement them. Other 
recommendations that would guide the 
implementation and maintenance of the 
support were also placed on the timeline.
9. Present the analysis, results and 
conclusions, and provide recommen­
dations to key stakeholders in written, 
oral and graphical form, using appro­
priate methods, tools and models. The 
presentations should enable key stake­
holders to make qualified decisions.  
The analysis, results, conclusions, and 
recommendations of the project have 
been presented in written and graphical 
form through a thesis. In addition to that, 
an oral presentation of the key findings
and the developed models and tools from 
the project is planned performed for the 
project owner and other key stakeholders 
in the case company. Additionally, a 
presentation of the support tool and how 
to use it is expected to be realised in near 
future as part of the implementation of 
the support and training of the users. As 
a result, the application of the tool will 
be initiated in practice, and buyers can 
start using it to make qualified and more 
sustainable decisions in procurement.  
This thesis aimed to investigate how 
a more sustainable procurement of 
laboratory equipment can be facilitated 
through PSS in terms of environmental 
and economic performance. The study 
has been conducted through a design 
project following the Design Research 
Method which structurally and iteratively 
guides the designer through four phases. 
The project initiated with a Research 
Clarification that found PSS an enabler 
of a circular economy due to its ability to 
create lasting added value for customers 
through different circular means, e.g. 
closing 
material 
loops, 
adding 
life-
extending 
services, 
and 
increasing 
product utilisation, while consuming as 
few resources as possible. It was also 
seen how more integrated and bundled 
solutions, such as PSS, could solve common 
procurement issues, e.g. high waste 
generation, high spend, comprehensive 
inventory management, and other non-
value adding activities. However, despite 
the high potentials of PSS in procurement, 
few instances of PSS offered in the B2B 
context in the pharmaceutical sector were 
found in literature.  Thus, the goal of the 
thesis was to develop a support that would 
facilitate the selection of an optimal PSS 
for procurement in terms of economic and 
environmental performance, to support 
decision-making in procurement. Three 
research questions were investigated 
throughout the thesis to reach this goal:
RQ 1 Which PSS solutions are applied or    
         offered within procurement in B2B 	
         contexts?
RQ 2 How can the selection of the most 	
         optimal PSS in procurement be 
         supported?
	
SRQ 2.1 Which factors must be 
                           considered when
                           procuring a PSS?
	
SRQ 2.2 What are the key factors 
                           influencing the 
                           enviromental potential of 	
	
	
    a PSS?
	
SRQ 2.3 Which guidelines, 
	
               methods,  and tools for                
                           selecting an appropriate         
                           PSS for procurement exist 
                           in literature?
RQ 3 How useful and usable is the   
         support in supporting the selection  
         of the most appropriate PSS?
193
194
==PAGE:103==
RQ 1: Which PSS solutions are applied 
or offered within procurement in B2B 
contexts?
Successful PSS solutions for purposes 
such as printing, lighting, heat regulation, 
movement 
of 
water, 
and 
chemical 
management were found in industry. 
Empirical studies in the case company also 
showed examples of offered and applied 
PSS, e.g. machinery and manufacturing 
equipment covered by service level 
agreements, renting and leasing of 
laboratory 
equipment, 
environmental 
monitoring 
system, 
pallet 
shippers, 
and heating and cooling systems for 
purification 
plants. 
Supplementary 
interviews added further insights into 
reasons both encouraging and preventing 
the application of PSS. Some reasons 
encouraging the application of PSS were 
the need for testing and experimenting 
in a shorter time frame or for a project, 
testing of equipment, limited budget, 
urgent need for access to the needed 
technology, 
lack 
of 
capacity 
and 
infrastructure, possibilities for upgrade, 
and added services from a supplier. The 
reasons that, on the other hand, prevent 
the procurement of PSS were cases when
dealing with very specialised equipment 
that only can be provided by specific 
suppliers and when there is a budget 
to buy the equipment. Furthermore, 
with input from several stakeholders in 
procurement and Line of Business, it 
was concluded that a product-oriented 
mindset is dominating the systems 
and 
processes 
within 
procurement, 
which hinder the investigation of other 
alternatives from achieving access to the 
needed technology. 
RQ 2: How to support the selection of 
the most optimal PSS in procurement?
The 
second 
research 
question 
was 
explored by looking into which factors 
must be considered when procuring a PSS 
(SRQ 2.1), which mechanisms influence 
the environmental potential of a PSS (SRQ 
2.2), and which guidelines, methods and 
tools for selecting an appropriate PSS 
exist in literature (SRQ 2.3). The insight 
gained through these questions was 
used to design a support that would 
facilitate the selection of an optimal PSS 
in procurement, in terms of economic and 
environmental performance. 
SRQ 
2.1: 
Which 
factors 
must 
be 
considered when procuring a PSS?
A list of factors to consider when 
procuring PSS was identified in literature. 
Cost was identified as a factor to consider 
due to e.g. the expected change in 
economic structures as a consequence of 
moving from fixed towards more variable 
expenditures. Due to the risk of creating 
environmental rebound effects that would 
promote induced impact, the environment 
was also considered an important factor. 
Thus, it would be necessary to identify 
potential risks of rebound effects and 
solutions to mitigate them. Because of 
the contractual complexities connected to 
the procurement of PSS, legal regulation 
and 
supplier 
relationships 
have 
also 
been identified as essential factors. 
The 
increased 
interdependency 
and 
communication between supplier and 
customer require additional confidence 
and trust. Therefore, security, privacy, 
and confidentiality were also considered 
factors to acknowledge when procuring 
a PSS. Other factors identified were 
organisational 
readiness, 
risks, 
and 
uncertainties.  
SRQ 2.2: Which mechanisms influence 
the environmental potential of a PSS?
Through literature, different mechanisms 
that influence the environmental potential 
of PSS were identified. CE strategies 
included in result-oriented PSS were 
found to create the highest environmental 
impact improvements but are often 
connected to high risks of rebound effects. 
Furthermore, CE strategies, such as reuse, 
pooling, renting, repairing, upgrading and 
design for modularity, showed improved 
environmental performance. However, 
these might also lead to unwanted 
rebound effects in different degrees. 
In literature, the three most frequently 
occurring rebound effects were transport, 
number of uses, and product substitution. 
SRQ 2.3: Which guidelines, methods, 
and tools for selecting an appropriate 
PSS for procurement exist in literature? 
Several different guidelines, methods 
and tool to support the selection of the 
most optimal PSS in terms of economic 
and environmental performance were 
identified and described. As a result, it 
was possible to conclude that suitable 
methods exist to evaluate the economic
potential of procuring a PSS, e.g. TCO and 
economic investment analysis. However, 
most 
existing 
tools 
for 
assessing 
environmental impact were evaluated 
to be too comprehensive and time-
consuming for a procurement setting.
RQ 3: How useful and usable is the 
support in supporting the selection of 
the most appropriate PSS?
A support that would facilitate the 
selection of the most optimal PSS in 
terms of economic and environmental 
performance, was developed to close 
the gaps identified through the research. 
The support ended up as a tool enabling 
the buyer to evaluate the sustainability 
performance 
of 
different 
supplier 
offerings by going through five activities. 
In the first activity, the equipment 
responsible would have to define the need 
of the investment, which would allow 
the buyer to investigate how a required 
function could be procured rather than 
being limited by a physical product. Then, 
in the second activity, the buyer would use 
a decision model and procurement model 
guidance to identify a suitable PSS for the 
specific investment. The buyer should ask
suppliers for quotations on the suggested 
PSS in the third activity, together with 
environmental KPI data connected to the 
offering. The TCO data from the quotations 
and environmental KPI data received from 
the supplier should then be inserted in 
the fourth and fifth activities. As a result, 
a sustainability benchmark would be 
generated to support decision-making. An 
important limitation of the support was 
that it does not consider risks of failure or 
environmental rebound effects that might 
occur when procuring the suggested PSS.
The support was tested in practice for 
two different investments and evaluated 
in terms of usefulness and usability. The 
evaluation revealed that the usefulness 
of the support was high overall, as it 
enabled the users to identify the usually 
occulted cost and environmental impact 
of the offerings, which convinced them 
to select the suggested PSS. However, 
the application of the support showed 
that the usefulness of the environmental 
evaluation tool could be improved by 
integrating more indicators that would 
reflect the environmental potential of 
adding services to the offerings, which

was done in a fifth iteration of the support.

as high overall, as the users managed to

direct access to data.

decoupling 
of

creation 
from

externally to support more sustainable 
procurement in other companies, the 
support would create a significant push in 
the market for PSS delivery. Furthermore, 
with PSS being an enabler of a circular 
economy, 
the 
support 
creates 
an 
opportunity for companies to take part 
in more sustainable business models, by 
creating and utilising value for as long 
as possible whilst minimising waste and 
resource consumption.
